Sects and Disagreements

5 min
End of the Samanid Dynasty
Narrative of the Samanid downfall in 389 AH.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Samanid dynasty ended and the roles of Mahmud of Ghazni and Ilek Khan in that process.

Core Idea
The Samanids fell to two Sunni Turkic rulers, reshaping the political map.

This lesson begins the historical context for Maturidi theology by describing the fall of the last major Persian Sunni dynasty in the east. Understanding this political shift is crucial for appreciating the environment in which theological schools developed.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the narrative flow, then read the translation. Note the sequence of events.
Pay attention to the names and their roles.
2
Identify the key figures
Identify Mahmud bin Sebuktigin, Ilek Khan, and Abd al-Malik al-Samani. Understand their motives and actions.
Mahmud and Ilek Khan were both Sunni, which is significant.
3
Reflect on the significance
Consider why the author starts with this event. What does it tell us about the political and sectarian landscape?
This sets the stage for later conflicts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي سنة ٣٨٩ه انقرضت دولة السامانيين على يد محمود بن سبكتكين، وأيلك الخان التركي، فأما محمود فإنه ملك خراسان وبقيت بلاد ما وراء النهر بيد عبد الملك الساماني، فلما انهزم من محمود بن سبكتكين قصد بخاري واجتمع فيها مع بعض الأمراء وأعدوا أنفسهم لاسترجاع خراسان إلا أنهم فوجئوا بموت رأس القوة فيهم وهو فائق، فضعفت عزيمتهم، وبلغ خبر وفاته لأيلك الخان، فرأى أن الفرصة سانحة للانقضاض على عبد الملك وجيشه، فسار في جمع من الأتراك إلى بخاري، وأظهر المودة والموالاة لعبد الملك وقومه، فظنوه صادقا، وحينما اجتمعوا انقض عليهم، وألقى بجموعهم في السجن، وعلى رأسهم عبد الملك آخر ملوك الدولة السامانية، ولم يمض وقت كبير حتى مات عبد الملك، وانقضت بذلك الدولة السامانية (١). ومن هنا يتبين أنه بانقراض الدولة السامانية يكون الأمر قد استتب في خراسان لمحمود بن سبكتكين، وفي بلاد ما وراء النهر لأيلك خان، وكلاهما سني المذهب، واستمر حكم بلاد ما وراء النهر لأيلك خان وأخوته من بعده حتى انتهى سنة ٤٨٢هـ، ببداية الدولة السلجوقية، وسيطرة ملك شاه على بلاد ما وراء النهر (۲). ونخلص من هذا إلى أن بلاد ما وراء النهر كانت قبل الانفصال تحت سيطرة الدولة العباسية السنية، وبعد الانفصال أصبحت تحت سيطرة الأتراك وهم سنيون أيضا، فإذا ما أجلنا النظر في الأمة الإسلامية وجدنا أن السلطة الثانية، المناوئة للدولة العباسية هي سلطة الفاطميين في مصر والمغرب، وبلاد الشام، وهي سلطة شيعية، ومن هنا كان الصراع، والفتن، وسفك الدماء سمة أساسية من سمات القرن الخامس بين السلطتين العباسية، والفاطمية. (١) راجع الكامل لابن الأثير جـ ٩ ٦٢ ط. و تاريخ ابن خلدون جـ ٤ ص ۳۰۸ ط بولاق سنة ١٢٨٤هـ. (۲) الكامل لابن الأثير جـ ٨ ص ٣٨، ص ١٤٨. ومن تلك الصراعات ما يذكره ابن الأثير من حوادث سنة ٤٠٧ فقد قتلت الشيعة في جميع بلاد أفريقية، وانبسطت أيدي العامة فيهم، وأُحرقوا بالنار، وكان عامل القيروان يغري أهل السنة ويحرضهم على الفتك بالشيعة(١). ويذكر ابن كثير أنه في سنة ٤٠٨هـ وقعت فتنة عظيمة بين أهل السنة والروافض، قتل فيها عدد كبير من الفريقين(٢)، وفي سنة ٤٤٣ الروافض أبراجا كتبوا عليها بالذهب محمدا وعليا خير البشر فمن رضي فقد شكر، ومن أبى فقد كفر ، فأنكرت السنة إقران علي مع محمد عليه السلام، ودارت الحرب عاصفة بين الفريقين، وهم الروافض بقبر الإمام أحمد بن حنبل (۳). وتجددت الحرب فيما بين الفريقين سنة ٤٤٤هـ ، سنة ٤٤٥هـ، وكان كل فريق يجتهد في إحراق ما يملكه الآخر، وفي سنة ٤٤٧هـ تجددت الفتنة حتى عجزت السلطة أن تحجز بين الفريقين، وفى نفس السنة قامت فتنة بين الأشاعرة، والحنابلة (٤). ببغداد بحيث أصبح من المتعذر أن يشهد الجمعة أو الجماعة أحد من الأشاعرة ( ٤ ) . وبالنسبة للمعتزلة وغيرهم من الفرق الأخرى، فقد استتاب الخليفة القادر بالله فقهاء المعتزلة سنة ٤٠٨هـ ، وهددهم بالعقوبة وتلقى محمود بن سبكتكين في خراسان أمر الخليفة فأعمل القتل فى المعتزلة، والرافضة، والإسماعيلية، والقرامطة ،والجهمية والمشبهة، وصلبهم، وأمر بلعنهم على المنابر(). وفي خراسان أيضا، وفي سنة ٤٤٣ هـ وسنة ٤٤٧ هـ، وبسبب تعصب عميد الملك الكندري، وكراهيته للشافعية نقل إلى السلطان كلاما ليوشى بالأشاعرة، وأدى هذا إلى اضطهاد أهل السنة في تلك الفترة (٦) .
And in the year 389 AH, the state of the Samanids was extinguished at the hands of Mahmud bin Sebuktigin and Ilek Khan the Turk. As for Mahmud, he ruled Khurasan, while the lands of Transoxiana remained in the hand of Abd al-Malik al-Samani. When he was defeated by Mahmud bin Sebuktigin, he headed to Bukhara and gathered there with some of the amirs and prepared themselves to recover Khurasan, but they were surprised by the death of the head of their force, Fa'iq. So their resolve weakened, and news of his death reached Ilek Khan. He saw that the opportunity was ripe to swoop down on Abd al-Malik and his army. So he marched with a group of Turks to Bukhara, and showed affection and allegiance to Abd al-Malik and his people, so they thought him truthful. When they gathered, he swooped down on them, and threw their multitudes into prison, headed by Abd al-Malik, the last king of the Samanid state. And not much time passed until Abd al-Malik died, and thus the Samanid state ended (1). From here it becomes clear that with the extinction of the Samanid state, authority had been consolidated in Khurasan for Mahmud bin Sebuktigin, and in Transoxiana for Ilek Khan, both of whom were of Sunni creed. And the rule of Transoxiana continued under Ilek Khan and his brothers after him until it ended in the year 482 AH, with the beginning of the Seljuk state and the control of Malik Shah over Transoxiana (2). From this we conclude that Transoxiana before the separation was under the control of the Sunni Abbasid state, and after the separation it came under the control of the Turks, who were also Sunnis. Then if we reflect on the Islamic nation, we find that the second authority, opposed to the Abbasid state, was the authority of the Fatimids in Egypt, the Maghreb, and the Levant, which was a Shiite authority. Hence conflict, strife, and bloodshed were a fundamental characteristic of the fifth century between the Abbasid and Fatimid authorities. (1) Refer to al-Kamil by Ibn al-Athir vol. 9 p. 62, and Tarikh Ibn Khaldun vol. 4 p. 308 Bulaq edition 1284 AH. (2) al-Kamil by Ibn al-Athir vol. 8 p. 38, p. 148. And among those conflicts is what Ibn al-Athir mentions from the events of the year 407 AH: the Shia were killed in all the lands of Africa, the hands of the common people were extended against them, and they were burned with fire, and the governor of Qayrawan incited the Sunnis and urged them to kill the Shia (1). And Ibn Kathir mentions that in the year 408 AH a great fitna occurred between the Sunnis and the Rawafid, in which a large number of both groups were killed (2). And in the year 443 the Rawafid built towers on which they wrote in gold: 'Muhammad and Ali are the best of mankind; whoever is pleased has thanked, and whoever refuses has disbelieved.' So the Sunnis denounced the pairing of Ali with Muhammad (peace be upon him), and violent war raged between the two groups, and they threatened the grave of Imam Ahmad ibn Hanbal (3). The war between the two groups renewed in 444 AH and 445 AH, and each group strove to burn what the other possessed. In 447 AH the fitna renewed until the authority was unable to restrain the two groups, and in the same year a fitna occurred between the Ash'aris and the Hanbalis (4) in Baghdad such that it became impossible for any Ash'ari to attend Friday or congregational prayer (4). As for the Mu'tazila and other sects, the Caliph al-Qadir bi'llah called the Mu'tazila jurists to repent in 408 AH and threatened them with punishment. Mahmud bin Sebuktigin in Khurasan received the Caliph's order, so he killed the Mu'tazila, Rafidah, Ismailis, Qaramita, Jahmiyya, and Mushabbiha, and crucified them, and ordered them to be cursed on the pulpits (). And in Khurasan as well, in 443 AH and 447 AH, due to the bigotry of Amid al-Mulk al-Kunduri and his hatred of the Shafi'is, he conveyed words to the Sultan to slander the Ash'aris, and this led to the persecution of the Sunnis in that period (6).
Source pages p. 20

Commentary
This passage is a historical narrative. The author, likely setting the stage for his theological work, recounts the political and sectarian history of the 5th century AH. He begins with the end of the Samanid dynasty in 389 AH, describing how Mahmud of Ghazni and Ilek Khan divided Samanid territories, with both rulers being Sunni. The author notes that Transoxiana remained under Sunni rule, first under the Abbasids and then under the Turks. He then contrasts this with the rise of the Fatimids, a Shiite power, as the main rival to the Abbasids, leading to widespread conflict. Next, he details specific sectarian clashes: in 407 AH, the Shia were killed in Africa; in 408 AH, a major fitna between Sunnis and Rawafid; in 443 AH, the Rawafid erected towers with an inscription pairing Ali with Muhammad, causing outrage; hostilities continued through 444-445 AH; in 447 AH, a fitna between Ash'aris and Hanbalis in Baghdad made it impossible for Ash'aris to attend congregational prayers. The author also mentions the persecution of Mu'tazila and other groups ordered by Caliph al-Qadir and executed by Mahmud of Ghazni, including killing and crucifixion. Finally, he describes how in Khurasan, due to the bigotry of Amid al-Mulk al-Kunduri, the Ash'aris were persecuted. This historical context underscores the intense sectarian environment in which Maturidi kalam developed.

Key Terms
السامانيين
محمود بن سبكتكين
أيلك الخان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What was the immediate cause of Abd al-Malik's weakened resolve?
A) Mahmud's army attacked Bukhara.
B) The death of their leader Fa'iq.
C) Ilek Khan betrayed them.
D) The Samanid treasury was empty.

Key Takeaway
The Samanid dynasty ended in 389 AH through the combined actions of Mahmud of Ghazni and Ilek Khan, both Sunni rulers.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Samanids trusted Ilek Khan's show of affection and were destroyed. In your own life, where are you trusting appearances without verifying the truth? What relationship or situation is vulnerable to such deception, and what step will you take today to seek clarity?
?
The death of one commander (Fa'iq) caused the Samanids' resolve to weaken. How fragile is your own resolve when a key support is removed? Is your trust in Allah or in people and plans? What would happen if your 'Fa'iq' fell tomorrow?
?
Abd al-Malik, the last king, died in prison after his state ended. Power and status are fleeting. If everything you have built were taken away today, would your relationship with Allah sustain you? What are you clinging to that is not eternal?

Sects and Disagreements
beginner
4 min
Sunni Rule and the Fatimid Challenge
Transition from Samanids to Seljuks and the rise of Fatimid-Shiite threat.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Transoxiana remained under Sunni control while the Fatimid Shiite state emerged as a rival to the Abbasids.

Core Idea
After the Samanids, Sunni Turks ruled the east, while Shiite Fatimids challenged Abbasid authority in the west.

This lesson continues the historical overview by showing that the eastern Islamic world remained Sunni after the Samanids, but the rise of the Fatimids as a Shiite power created a polarised sectarian landscape.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation, noting the shift from narrative to analysis.
The author draws a conclusion about Sunni continuity.
2
Contrast the two powers
Identify the Abbasids and Fatimids. Why is their conflict described as a 'fitna'?
Fatimids were Ismaili Shiites.
3
Connect to the broader theme
Think about how this political context influenced theological debates and the need for creedal works.
Sectarian strife often spurs theological writing.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي سنة ٣٨٩ه انقرضت دولة السامانيين على يد محمود بن سبكتكين، وأيلك الخان التركي، فأما محمود فإنه ملك خراسان وبقيت بلاد ما وراء النهر بيد عبد الملك الساماني، فلما انهزم من محمود بن سبكتكين قصد بخاري واجتمع فيها مع بعض الأمراء وأعدوا أنفسهم لاسترجاع خراسان إلا أنهم فوجئوا بموت رأس القوة فيهم وهو فائق، فضعفت عزيمتهم، وبلغ خبر وفاته لأيلك الخان، فرأى أن الفرصة سانحة للانقضاض على عبد الملك وجيشه، فسار في جمع من الأتراك إلى بخاري، وأظهر المودة والموالاة لعبد الملك وقومه، فظنوه صادقا، وحينما اجتمعوا انقض عليهم، وألقى بجموعهم في السجن، وعلى رأسهم عبد الملك آخر ملوك الدولة السامانية، ولم يمض وقت كبير حتى مات عبد الملك، وانقضت بذلك الدولة السامانية (١). ومن هنا يتبين أنه بانقراض الدولة السامانية يكون الأمر قد استتب في خراسان لمحمود بن سبكتكين، وفي بلاد ما وراء النهر لأيلك خان، وكلاهما سني المذهب، واستمر حكم بلاد ما وراء النهر لأيلك خان وأخوته من بعده حتى انتهى سنة ٤٨٢هـ، ببداية الدولة السلجوقية، وسيطرة ملك شاه على بلاد ما وراء النهر (۲). ونخلص من هذا إلى أن بلاد ما وراء النهر كانت قبل الانفصال تحت سيطرة الدولة العباسية السنية، وبعد الانفصال أصبحت تحت سيطرة الأتراك وهم سنيون أيضا، فإذا ما أجلنا النظر في الأمة الإسلامية وجدنا أن السلطة الثانية، المناوئة للدولة العباسية هي سلطة الفاطميين في مصر والمغرب، وبلاد الشام، وهي سلطة شيعية، ومن هنا كان الصراع، والفتن، وسفك الدماء سمة أساسية من سمات القرن الخامس بين السلطتين العباسية، والفاطمية. (١) راجع الكامل لابن الأثير جـ ٩ ٦٢ ط. و تاريخ ابن خلدون جـ ٤ ص ۳۰۸ ط بولاق سنة ١٢٨٤هـ. (۲) الكامل لابن الأثير جـ ٨ ص ٣٨، ص ١٤٨. ومن تلك الصراعات ما يذكره ابن الأثير من حوادث سنة ٤٠٧ فقد قتلت الشيعة في جميع بلاد أفريقية، وانبسطت أيدي العامة فيهم، وأُحرقوا بالنار، وكان عامل القيروان يغري أهل السنة ويحرضهم على الفتك بالشيعة(١). ويذكر ابن كثير أنه في سنة ٤٠٨هـ وقعت فتنة عظيمة بين أهل السنة والروافض، قتل فيها عدد كبير من الفريقين(٢)، وفي سنة ٤٤٣ الروافض أبراجا كتبوا عليها بالذهب محمدا وعليا خير البشر فمن رضي فقد شكر، ومن أبى فقد كفر ، فأنكرت السنة إقران علي مع محمد عليه السلام، ودارت الحرب عاصفة بين الفريقين، وهم الروافض بقبر الإمام أحمد بن حنبل (۳). وتجددت الحرب فيما بين الفريقين سنة ٤٤٤هـ ، سنة ٤٤٥هـ، وكان كل فريق يجتهد في إحراق ما يملكه الآخر، وفي سنة ٤٤٧هـ تجددت الفتنة حتى عجزت السلطة أن تحجز بين الفريقين، وفى نفس السنة قامت فتنة بين الأشاعرة، والحنابلة (٤). ببغداد بحيث أصبح من المتعذر أن يشهد الجمعة أو الجماعة أحد من الأشاعرة ( ٤ ) . وبالنسبة للمعتزلة وغيرهم من الفرق الأخرى، فقد استتاب الخليفة القادر بالله فقهاء المعتزلة سنة ٤٠٨هـ ، وهددهم بالعقوبة وتلقى محمود بن سبكتكين في خراسان أمر الخليفة فأعمل القتل فى المعتزلة، والرافضة، والإسماعيلية، والقرامطة ،والجهمية والمشبهة، وصلبهم، وأمر بلعنهم على المنابر(). وفي خراسان أيضا، وفي سنة ٤٤٣ هـ وسنة ٤٤٧ هـ، وبسبب تعصب عميد الملك الكندري، وكراهيته للشافعية نقل إلى السلطان كلاما ليوشى بالأشاعرة، وأدى هذا إلى اضطهاد أهل السنة في تلك الفترة (٦) .
And in the year 389 AH, the state of the Samanids was extinguished at the hands of Mahmud bin Sebuktigin and Ilek Khan the Turk. As for Mahmud, he ruled Khurasan, while the lands of Transoxiana remained in the hand of Abd al-Malik al-Samani. When he was defeated by Mahmud bin Sebuktigin, he headed to Bukhara and gathered there with some of the amirs and prepared themselves to recover Khurasan, but they were surprised by the death of the head of their force, Fa'iq. So their resolve weakened, and news of his death reached Ilek Khan. He saw that the opportunity was ripe to swoop down on Abd al-Malik and his army. So he marched with a group of Turks to Bukhara, and showed affection and allegiance to Abd al-Malik and his people, so they thought him truthful. When they gathered, he swooped down on them, and threw their multitudes into prison, headed by Abd al-Malik, the last king of the Samanid state. And not much time passed until Abd al-Malik died, and thus the Samanid state ended (1). From here it becomes clear that with the extinction of the Samanid state, authority had been consolidated in Khurasan for Mahmud bin Sebuktigin, and in Transoxiana for Ilek Khan, both of whom were of Sunni creed. And the rule of Transoxiana continued under Ilek Khan and his brothers after him until it ended in the year 482 AH, with the beginning of the Seljuk state and the control of Malik Shah over Transoxiana (2). From this we conclude that Transoxiana before the separation was under the control of the Sunni Abbasid state, and after the separation it came under the control of the Turks, who were also Sunnis. Then if we reflect on the Islamic nation, we find that the second authority, opposed to the Abbasid state, was the authority of the Fatimids in Egypt, the Maghreb, and the Levant, which was a Shiite authority. Hence conflict, strife, and bloodshed were a fundamental characteristic of the fifth century between the Abbasid and Fatimid authorities. (1) Refer to al-Kamil by Ibn al-Athir vol. 9 p. 62, and Tarikh Ibn Khaldun vol. 4 p. 308 Bulaq edition 1284 AH. (2) al-Kamil by Ibn al-Athir vol. 8 p. 38, p. 148. And among those conflicts is what Ibn al-Athir mentions from the events of the year 407 AH: the Shia were killed in all the lands of Africa, the hands of the common people were extended against them, and they were burned with fire, and the governor of Qayrawan incited the Sunnis and urged them to kill the Shia (1). And Ibn Kathir mentions that in the year 408 AH a great fitna occurred between the Sunnis and the Rawafid, in which a large number of both groups were killed (2). And in the year 443 the Rawafid built towers on which they wrote in gold: 'Muhammad and Ali are the best of mankind; whoever is pleased has thanked, and whoever refuses has disbelieved.' So the Sunnis denounced the pairing of Ali with Muhammad (peace be upon him), and violent war raged between the two groups, and they threatened the grave of Imam Ahmad ibn Hanbal (3). The war between the two groups renewed in 444 AH and 445 AH, and each group strove to burn what the other possessed. In 447 AH the fitna renewed until the authority was unable to restrain the two groups, and in the same year a fitna occurred between the Ash'aris and the Hanbalis (4) in Baghdad such that it became impossible for any Ash'ari to attend Friday or congregational prayer (4). As for the Mu'tazila and other sects, the Caliph al-Qadir bi'llah called the Mu'tazila jurists to repent in 408 AH and threatened them with punishment. Mahmud bin Sebuktigin in Khurasan received the Caliph's order, so he killed the Mu'tazila, Rafidah, Ismailis, Qaramita, Jahmiyya, and Mushabbiha, and crucified them, and ordered them to be cursed on the pulpits (). And in Khurasan as well, in 443 AH and 447 AH, due to the bigotry of Amid al-Mulk al-Kunduri and his hatred of the Shafi'is, he conveyed words to the Sultan to slander the Ash'aris, and this led to the persecution of the Sunnis in that period (6).

Commentary
This passage is a historical narrative. The author, likely setting the stage for his theological work, recounts the political and sectarian history of the 5th century AH. He begins with the end of the Samanid dynasty in 389 AH, describing how Mahmud of Ghazni and Ilek Khan divided Samanid territories, with both rulers being Sunni. The author notes that Transoxiana remained under Sunni rule, first under the Abbasids and then under the Turks. He then contrasts this with the rise of the Fatimids, a Shiite power, as the main rival to the Abbasids, leading to widespread conflict. Next, he details specific sectarian clashes: in 407 AH, the Shia were killed in Africa; in 408 AH, a major fitna between Sunnis and Rawafid; in 443 AH, the Rawafid erected towers with an inscription pairing Ali with Muhammad, causing outrage; hostilities continued through 444-445 AH; in 447 AH, a fitna between Ash'aris and Hanbalis in Baghdad made it impossible for Ash'aris to attend congregational prayers. The author also mentions the persecution of Mu'tazila and other groups ordered by Caliph al-Qadir and executed by Mahmud of Ghazni, including killing and crucifixion. Finally, he describes how in Khurasan, due to the bigotry of Amid al-Mulk al-Kunduri, the Ash'aris were persecuted. This historical context underscores the intense sectarian environment in which Maturidi kalam developed.

According to the passage, what was the religious affiliation of the Fatimid state?
A) Sunni

Key Takeaway
After the Samanids, the east remained Sunni under Turkic rulers, while the Fatimids emerged as a Shiite rival, leading to widespread conflict.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage states that both sides were engaged in 'conflict, strife, and bloodshed'. Think of a time when you witnessed or were part of a conflict between two groups that claimed to be following the same religion. How did that affect your faith? What will you do to avoid being drawn into such sectarian strife in your own life?
?
The author notes that both eastern rulers were Sunni, yet they were rivals. This shows that political power can divide even those who share a creed. Are there relationships in your life where a difference in opinion or interest has caused division, despite shared beliefs? What step will you take today to heal such a division?
?
The Fatimids used their claimed descent from the Prophet's family to justify their rule, leading to bloodshed. In your own pursuit of status or influence, are you ever tempted to use religious or family connections to gain advantage at the expense of unity? How will you guard against such hypocrisy?

Sectarian Conflicts (407–447 AH)
Enumeration of sectarian conflicts from 407 to 447 AH.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the major sectarian conflicts that occurred between Sunnis, Rawafid (Shiites), Ash'aris, and Hanbalis in the 5th century AH.

Core Idea
The 5th century saw violent clashes between Sunnis and Shiites, and even among Sunni groups like Ash'aris and Hanbalis.

This lesson presents a series of specific incidents from the historical record, showing the intensity of sectarian strife. These conflicts are important for understanding the environment that motivated Sunni theologians to codify correct belief.

Guided Path
1
Read the sequence of events
Read the Arabic and translation, noting the years and the groups involved.
Events are in chronological order: 407, 408, 443, 444, 445, 447.
2
Identify the patterns
Notice how violence escalates and the state sometimes cannot control it. Also note the Rawafid inscription pairing Ali with Muhammad.
This inscription offended Sunni sensibilities.
3
Reflect on the Ash'ari-Hanbali fitna
Consider why conflict could arise between two Sunni theological schools.
Both claimed orthodoxy but differed on attributes of God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي سنة ٣٨٩ه انقرضت دولة السامانيين على يد محمود بن سبكتكين، وأيلك الخان التركي، فأما محمود فإنه ملك خراسان وبقيت بلاد ما وراء النهر بيد عبد الملك الساماني، فلما انهزم من محمود بن سبكتكين قصد بخاري واجتمع فيها مع بعض الأمراء وأعدوا أنفسهم لاسترجاع خراسان إلا أنهم فوجئوا بموت رأس القوة فيهم وهو فائق، فضعفت عزيمتهم، وبلغ خبر وفاته لأيلك الخان، فرأى أن الفرصة سانحة للانقضاض على عبد الملك وجيشه، فسار في جمع من الأتراك إلى بخاري، وأظهر المودة والموالاة لعبد الملك وقومه، فظنوه صادقا، وحينما اجتمعوا انقض عليهم، وألقى بجموعهم في السجن، وعلى رأسهم عبد الملك آخر ملوك الدولة السامانية، ولم يمض وقت كبير حتى مات عبد الملك، وانقضت بذلك الدولة السامانية (١). ومن هنا يتبين أنه بانقراض الدولة السامانية يكون الأمر قد استتب في خراسان لمحمود بن سبكتكين، وفي بلاد ما وراء النهر لأيلك خان، وكلاهما سني المذهب، واستمر حكم بلاد ما وراء النهر لأيلك خان وأخوته من بعده حتى انتهى سنة ٤٨٢هـ، ببداية الدولة السلجوقية، وسيطرة ملك شاه على بلاد ما وراء النهر (۲). ونخلص من هذا إلى أن بلاد ما وراء النهر كانت قبل الانفصال تحت سيطرة الدولة العباسية السنية، وبعد الانفصال أصبحت تحت سيطرة الأتراك وهم سنيون أيضا، فإذا ما أجلنا النظر في الأمة الإسلامية وجدنا أن السلطة الثانية، المناوئة للدولة العباسية هي سلطة الفاطميين في مصر والمغرب، وبلاد الشام، وهي سلطة شيعية، ومن هنا كان الصراع، والفتن، وسفك الدماء سمة أساسية من سمات القرن الخامس بين السلطتين العباسية، والفاطمية. (١) راجع الكامل لابن الأثير جـ ٩ ٦٢ ط. و تاريخ ابن خلدون جـ ٤ ص ۳۰۸ ط بولاق سنة ١٢٨٤هـ. (۲) الكامل لابن الأثير جـ ٨ ص ٣٨، ص ١٤٨. ومن تلك الصراعات ما يذكره ابن الأثير من حوادث سنة ٤٠٧ فقد قتلت الشيعة في جميع بلاد أفريقية، وانبسطت أيدي العامة فيهم، وأُحرقوا بالنار، وكان عامل القيروان يغري أهل السنة ويحرضهم على الفتك بالشيعة(١). ويذكر ابن كثير أنه في سنة ٤٠٨هـ وقعت فتنة عظيمة بين أهل السنة والروافض، قتل فيها عدد كبير من الفريقين(٢)، وفي سنة ٤٤٣ الروافض أبراجا كتبوا عليها بالذهب محمدا وعليا خير البشر فمن رضي فقد شكر، ومن أبى فقد كفر ، فأنكرت السنة إقران علي مع محمد عليه السلام، ودارت الحرب عاصفة بين الفريقين، وهم الروافض بقبر الإمام أحمد بن حنبل (۳). وتجددت الحرب فيما بين الفريقين سنة ٤٤٤هـ ، سنة ٤٤٥هـ، وكان كل فريق يجتهد في إحراق ما يملكه الآخر، وفي سنة ٤٤٧هـ تجددت الفتنة حتى عجزت السلطة أن تحجز بين الفريقين، وفى نفس السنة قامت فتنة بين الأشاعرة، والحنابلة (٤). ببغداد بحيث أصبح من المتعذر أن يشهد الجمعة أو الجماعة أحد من الأشاعرة ( ٤ ) . وبالنسبة للمعتزلة وغيرهم من الفرق الأخرى، فقد استتاب الخليفة القادر بالله فقهاء المعتزلة سنة ٤٠٨هـ ، وهددهم بالعقوبة وتلقى محمود بن سبكتكين في خراسان أمر الخليفة فأعمل القتل فى المعتزلة، والرافضة، والإسماعيلية، والقرامطة ،والجهمية والمشبهة، وصلبهم، وأمر بلعنهم على المنابر(). وفي خراسان أيضا، وفي سنة ٤٤٣ هـ وسنة ٤٤٧ هـ، وبسبب تعصب عميد الملك الكندري، وكراهيته للشافعية نقل إلى السلطان كلاما ليوشى بالأشاعرة، وأدى هذا إلى اضطهاد أهل السنة في تلك الفترة (٦) .
And in the year 389 AH, the state of the Samanids was extinguished at the hands of Mahmud bin Sebuktigin and Ilek Khan the Turk. As for Mahmud, he ruled Khurasan, while the lands of Transoxiana remained in the hand of Abd al-Malik al-Samani. When he was defeated by Mahmud bin Sebuktigin, he headed to Bukhara and gathered there with some of the amirs and prepared themselves to recover Khurasan, but they were surprised by the death of the head of their force, Fa'iq. So their resolve weakened, and news of his death reached Ilek Khan. He saw that the opportunity was ripe to swoop down on Abd al-Malik and his army. So he marched with a group of Turks to Bukhara, and showed affection and allegiance to Abd al-Malik and his people, so they thought him truthful. When they gathered, he swooped down on them, and threw their multitudes into prison, headed by Abd al-Malik, the last king of the Samanid state. And not much time passed until Abd al-Malik died, and thus the Samanid state ended (1). From here it becomes clear that with the extinction of the Samanid state, authority had been consolidated in Khurasan for Mahmud bin Sebuktigin, and in Transoxiana for Ilek Khan, both of whom were of Sunni creed. And the rule of Transoxiana continued under Ilek Khan and his brothers after him until it ended in the year 482 AH, with the beginning of the Seljuk state and the control of Malik Shah over Transoxiana (2). From this we conclude that Transoxiana before the separation was under the control of the Sunni Abbasid state, and after the separation it came under the control of the Turks, who were also Sunnis. Then if we reflect on the Islamic nation, we find that the second authority, opposed to the Abbasid state, was the authority of the Fatimids in Egypt, the Maghreb, and the Levant, which was a Shiite authority. Hence conflict, strife, and bloodshed were a fundamental characteristic of the fifth century between the Abbasid and Fatimid authorities. (1) Refer to al-Kamil by Ibn al-Athir vol. 9 p. 62, and Tarikh Ibn Khaldun vol. 4 p. 308 Bulaq edition 1284 AH. (2) al-Kamil by Ibn al-Athir vol. 8 p. 38, p. 148. And among those conflicts is what Ibn al-Athir mentions from the events of the year 407 AH: the Shia were killed in all the lands of Africa, the hands of the common people were extended against them, and they were burned with fire, and the governor of Qayrawan incited the Sunnis and urged them to kill the Shia (1). And Ibn Kathir mentions that in the year 408 AH a great fitna occurred between the Sunnis and the Rawafid, in which a large number of both groups were killed (2). And in the year 443 the Rawafid built towers on which they wrote in gold: 'Muhammad and Ali are the best of mankind; whoever is pleased has thanked, and whoever refuses has disbelieved.' So the Sunnis denounced the pairing of Ali with Muhammad (peace be upon him), and violent war raged between the two groups, and they threatened the grave of Imam Ahmad ibn Hanbal (3). The war between the two groups renewed in 444 AH and 445 AH, and each group strove to burn what the other possessed. In 447 AH the fitna renewed until the authority was unable to restrain the two groups, and in the same year a fitna occurred between the Ash'aris and the Hanbalis (4) in Baghdad such that it became impossible for any Ash'ari to attend Friday or congregational prayer (4). As for the Mu'tazila and other sects, the Caliph al-Qadir bi'llah called the Mu'tazila jurists to repent in 408 AH and threatened them with punishment. Mahmud bin Sebuktigin in Khurasan received the Caliph's order, so he killed the Mu'tazila, Rafidah, Ismailis, Qaramita, Jahmiyya, and Mushabbiha, and crucified them, and ordered them to be cursed on the pulpits (). And in Khurasan as well, in 443 AH and 447 AH, due to the bigotry of Amid al-Mulk al-Kunduri and his hatred of the Shafi'is, he conveyed words to the Sultan to slander the Ash'aris, and this led to the persecution of the Sunnis in that period (6).
Source pages p. 20

Commentary
This passage is a historical narrative. The author, likely setting the stage for his theological work, recounts the political and sectarian history of the 5th century AH. He begins with the end of the Samanid dynasty in 389 AH, describing how Mahmud of Ghazni and Ilek Khan divided Samanid territories, with both rulers being Sunni. The author notes that Transoxiana remained under Sunni rule, first under the Abbasids and then under the Turks. He then contrasts this with the rise of the Fatimids, a Shiite power, as the main rival to the Abbasids, leading to widespread conflict. Next, he details specific sectarian clashes: in 407 AH, the Shia were killed in Africa; in 408 AH, a major fitna between Sunnis and Rawafid; in 443 AH, the Rawafid erected towers with an inscription pairing Ali with Muhammad, causing outrage; hostilities continued through 444-445 AH; in 447 AH, a fitna between Ash'aris and Hanbalis in Baghdad made it impossible for Ash'aris to attend congregational prayers. The author also mentions the persecution of Mu'tazila and other groups ordered by Caliph al-Qadir and executed by Mahmud of Ghazni, including killing and crucifixion. Finally, he describes how in Khurasan, due to the bigotry of Amid al-Mulk al-Kunduri, the Ash'aris were persecuted. This historical context underscores the intense sectarian environment in which Maturidi kalam developed.

Key Takeaway
The 5th century AH witnessed violent sectarian clashes, including Sunni-Shiite strife and intra-Sunni conflict between Ash'aris and Hanbalis.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read about the violence between Sunnis and Shia in 407 AH, which side do you find yourself sympathizing with? What does that reveal about your own heart regarding sectarian divisions?
?
The Rawafid inscription equated Ali with Muhammad. Why do you think the Sunnis reacted so violently? Would you have protested, or would you have stayed silent? What would your silence say?
?
In 447 AH, Ash'aris could not attend Friday prayers due to Hanbali hostility. Imagine being prevented from praying because of a theological disagreement. How would you respond? Do you harbor any similar hostility toward fellow Muslims who differ from you?

Persecution of Theological Groups
Account of state-led persecution of theological groups in Baghdad and Khurasan.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Caliph and rulers persecuted Mu'tazila, Rafidah, and other groups, as well as the persecution of Ash'aris under the vizier al-Kunduri.

Core Idea
State-backed persecution targeted Mu'tazila and other sects, while Ash'aris also faced suppression by powerful officials.

This lesson covers the final part of the historical context, showing that theological differences led to state action. The Caliph al-Qadir ordered the repression of Mu'tazila and other groups, and the vizier al-Kunduri persecuted Ash'aris in Khurasan.

Guided Path
1
Read about the Caliph's order
Read the passage about al-Qadir's demand for repentance from Mu'tazila and Mahmud's execution of multiple sects.
Note the list of groups: Mu'tazila, Rafidah, Ismailis, Qaramita, Jahmiyya, Mushabbiha.
2
Read about al-Kunduri's persecution
Read the passage about the vizier's bigotry against Shafi'is and Ash'aris.
This shows internal Sunni persecution based on theological differences.
3
Compare the two episodes
Consider how state power was used against different groups. What does this tell us about the stakes of theological disputes?
Theological views could lead to death or political exclusion.

And in the year 389 AH, the state of the Samanids was extinguished at the hands of Mahmud bin Sebuktigin and Ilek Khan the Turk. How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Mahmud of Ghazni executed people for their theological views, believing he was obeying the caliph. Are there any teachings in your own tradition that you accept without question, even if they lead to harming others? How can you ensure your loyalty to God and His Prophet (peace be upon him) does not become a tool for oppression?

Cultural Flourishing and Theological Diversity
Describes cultural context and lists eight kalam schools.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the cultural context of al-Nasafi's era, the patronage of scholars by rulers, and the variety of kalam schools active in the 5th century.

Core Idea
The 5th century teemed with theological schools, each defending its creed with proof.

Before diving into the theological arguments, it's important to understand the intellectual environment in which al-Nasafi lived. This passage paints a picture of a vibrant era where multiple kalam schools coexisted and competed. The author highlights that this diversity, far from being chaotic, was a catalyst for truth-seeking.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on cultural context
Read the Arabic passage carefully, focusing on the contrast between political disturbances and cultural flourishing. Note how rulers patronized scholars.
The author emphasizes that Abbasid and successor states all supported learning.
2
Identify the eight kalam schools
Find the list of theological trends in the passage. Count them and note their names.
The schools are: Ash'aris, Mu'tazilis, Maturidis, Kharijis, Shi'is, Jabriyya, Qadariyya, and Karramiyya.
3
Reflect on the competitive dynamic
Notice that each school works hard to support its beliefs with proofs and invalidate opponents. Consider why this competition might be healthy for theological discourse.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الملامح الثقافية لعصر النسفي:
عرفنا أن أبا المعين ميمون النسفي ولد وعاش في القرن الخامس وبداية القرن السادس الهجريين.
وإذا كانت الحياة من الناحيتين السياسية والدينية قد حفلت ببعض الاضطرابات فعلى خلاف هذا تماما كان الوضع بالنسبة للناحية الثقافية. فمن المعروف أن حكام الدولة العباسية كان من دأبهم تشجيع العلم والعلماء، ولقد سار على نفس المنوال حكام الدويلات التي انقسمت عن الحكم العباسي بعد ضعفه، بل أربوا عليهم، حيث آووا العلماء وشجعوهم، ولعلنا نذكر صورا مبسطة يتبين منها الملامح الثقافية لعصر النسفي.
فإذا كانت بدايتنا بالعلوم الكلامية نجد أن القرن الخامس الهجري قد حفل بالكثير من الإتجاهات الكلامية، فهناك الأشاعرة، والمعتزلة، والماتريدية، والخوارج، والشيعة والجبرية والقدرية والكرامية، كل يعمل جاهدا على نصرة ما يعتقده بإقامة الأدلة عليه، وبإبطال رأي خصمه وأحسب أن الحقيقة لا تظهر جلية إلا من خلال تصارع الآراء، وأحسب أيضا أن بيئة مثل هذه تتصارع فيها الاتجاهات الكلامية تخرج الكثيرين من الأفذاذ منهم على سبيل المثال: القاضي عبد الجبار (۱) المتوفى سنة ٤١٥هـ، وكان إمام الاعتزال في زمانه، وقدم للمكتبة الكلامية العديد من المؤلفات منها موسوعته الشهيرة في علم الكلام - المغني وكتاب شرح الأصول الخمسة، ومنهم عبد السلام بن يوسف القزويني المتوفى سنة ٤٨٢ه (٢) ، وكان من كبار رجال الاعتزال أمد تلاميذه بتفسير كبير يقع كما قيل في سبعمائة مجلد. ومنهم أبو إسحاق الأسفراييني الأشعري المذهب والمتوفى سنة ٤١٨ هـ (٣) ، وأبو منصور بن أيوب المتوفى سنة ٤٢١ه (٤).
The cultural features of the era of al-Nasafi: We have known that Abu al-Ma'in Maymun al-Nasafi was born and lived in the fifth century and the beginning of the sixth century AH. And if life from the political and religious aspects was filled with some disturbances, then contrary to this entirely was the situation regarding the cultural aspect. For it is well-known that the rulers of the Abbasid state were in the habit of encouraging knowledge and scholars, and the rulers of the smaller states that split from Abbasid rule after its weakness followed the same pattern, rather they surpassed them, as they sheltered scholars and encouraged them, and perhaps we can mention simplified examples from which the cultural features of al-Nasafi's era become clear. If we begin with the theological sciences, we find that the fifth century AH was filled with many theological trends. There are the Ash'aris, the Mu'tazilis, the Maturidis, the Kharijis, the Shi'is, the Jabriyya, the Qadariyya, and the Karramiyya. Each works hard to support what he believes by establishing proofs for it, and by invalidating the opinion of his opponent. And I think that the truth does not become clear except through the clash of opinions. And I also think that an environment like this, where theological trends clash, produces many outstanding individuals, among them for example: al-Qadi Abd al-Jabbar (1) who died in 415 AH, and he was the imam of the Mu'tazila in his time, and he contributed to the theological library many works, including his famous encyclopedia in kalam - al-Mughni, and the book Sharh al-Usul al-Khamsa. And among them is Abd al-Salam ibn Yusuf al-Qazwini who died in 482 AH (2), and he was among the great men of the Mu'tazila; he provided his students with a great tafsir which, as it is said, comprised seven hundred volumes. And among them is Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini who followed the Ash'ari madhhab, died in 418 AH (3), and Abu Mansur ibn Ayyub who died in 421 AH (4).
Source pages p. 23

Commentary
This passage is a descriptive overview of the cultural context of Nasafi's era, specifically focusing on theological diversity. It begins by contrasting the political and religious disturbances with the flourishing cultural scene. The author notes that Abbasid caliphs and later successor states all patronized scholars, often surpassing earlier rulers. The core of the passage is a list of theological schools active in the 5th century: Ash'aris, Mu'tazilis, Maturidis, Kharijis, Shi'is, Jabriyya, Qadariyya, and Karramiyya. The author observes that each school vigorously defended its positions with proofs and refutations. He then states his opinion that truth emerges from the clash of opinions, and that such an environment generates exceptional scholars. To illustrate, he names four scholars: Qadi Abd al-Jabbar (Mu'tazili, author of al-Mughni and Sharh al-Usul al-Khamsa), Abd al-Salam al-Qazwini (Mu'tazili, known for a massive tafsir), Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini (Ash'ari), and Abu Mansur ibn Ayyub. The passage highlights the intellectual productivity and stature of these figures, demonstrating the vitality of kalam scholarship in this period.

Key Terms
الأشاعرة
المعتزلة
الماتريدية
الخوارج
الشيعة
الجبرية
القدرية
الكرامية
علم الكلام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That only Ash'aris and Mu'tazilis were active in the 5th century.
Correction: The passage lists eight schools, including Maturidis, Kharijis, Shi'is, Jabriyya, Qadariyya, and Karramiyya, showing a much wider diversity.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The 5th century featured a rich diversity of at least eight theological schools, each actively defending its doctrines.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a theological school you disagree with, do you dismiss it or examine its proofs? The scholars of the past engaged seriously with opponents. When was the last time you studied an opposing view from its own sources? What prevented you?
?
This passage shows a diversity of schools, each convinced of its truth. How do you ensure that your own beliefs are based on evidence rather than on the popularity of your group? Will you commit to testing your own positions against the proofs of others?
?
The scholars of that era worked hard to support their beliefs and invalidate opponents. Are you willing to invest that same effort in your own understanding of faith? What step will you take this week to deepen your knowledge of a theological issue you are unsure about?

Prominent Scholars of the 5th Century Kalam
Presents the author's theory that truth comes from opinion clash and lists four exemplary scholars.

Core Idea
Great scholars emerged from the clash of theological opinions, leaving monumental works.

Nasafi's Refutation of Sophists: Three Groups and Their Refutation
Nasafi refutes sophists by three arguments and a final treatment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam al-Nasafi refutes three types of sophists who deny objective realities, using arguments based on self-contradiction and practical treatment.

Core Idea
Denying reality is self-refuting: sophists cannot live or argue without assuming it.

This lesson presents Imam al-Nasafi's response to those who deny the existence of realities and knowledge. He identifies three distinct groups within the sophist movement and provides a specific refutation for each. The passage also discusses a controversial practical treatment for those who refuse rational debate, which underscores the seriousness of the challenge they pose to Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation. Notice how Nasafi first establishes the consensus of rational people before addressing the sophists.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the three groups
The passage mentions three distinct groups that deny realities. List them: the deniers of all reality, the agnostics, and the relativists. Note their claims.
3
Study each refutation
For each group, identify Nasafi's argument. For the first group, he uses self-refutation, practical behavior, and a punishment method. For the second, a question about knowledge. For the third, the opposite assertion.
Pay attention to how each argument exposes contradiction.
4
Reflect on the treatment method
Nasafi suggests inflicting punishment until they recant. Consider why he thinks rational debate is impossible without a common ground, and how this method was seen as a last resort.
The common misunderstanding is that this is a theological argument; it is a practical measure.
5
Summarize the overall strategy
Combine the lessons: Nasafi's approach is to force the sophists to admit their own ignorance or contradiction, either through logic or practical consequences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم يتحدث عن إثبات الحقائق والعلوم، فيقول : أجمع العقلاء على ثبوت العلم والحقائق للأشياء إلا أن هناك فرقة تقول : لا حقيقة لشيء، ولا علم بشيء، وإنما ظنون وحسبانات، ويرى أن هؤلاء لا مجال لمناظرتهم، لأن أساس المناظرة تكون لإثبات رأي، ونفي آخر، وتنبني على أساس مشترك بين المتناظرين، وله رأي وجيه في علاج مثل هؤلاء، وهو إنزال العقاب الشديد بهم فإذا ما ضجروا قيل لهم: لا حقيقة لشيء وإنما هي حسبانات وظنون ويستمر العقاب حتى يرجعوا عن عنادهم، كذلك فإنهم عندما ينكرون الحقائق يقرون أنهم لا يعلمون صحة مذهبهم و من أقر ببطلان مذهبه، فقد كفى خصمه مؤنة مجادلته، كذلك فإن حرصهم على المنافع، وابتعادهم عن المضار يؤكد أنهم يعلمون حقائق الأشياء، وإلا لما عاشوا . ويقول : إن ما استدلوا به من أن الحواس خادعة دليل على بطلان أقوالهم، لأنهم اشتغلوا بالحواس في بيان الإبطال. وهناك طائفة اللاأدرية، ومناقشة هؤلاء تكون بطرح هذا السؤال : هل تدرون أنكم لا تدرون؟ فإن قالوا: نعم. أقروا بإبطال مذهبهم وإن قالوا: لا استمر الكلام معهم عن نفي الدراية، وفي النهاية يعاملون بنفس ما عومل به الفريق الأول. ثم هناك طائفة ثالثة تقول : إن حقائق الأشياء تابعة لاعتقادات المعتقدين. والرد على هؤلاء بأن نقول عكسهم، فإن وافقونا أقروا بإبطال مذهبهم، وإن خالفونا، فقد أقروا أن المعتقد ليس بتابع للاعتقاد ويعاملون بنفس الطريقة، فإذا تألموا يقال لهم : اعتقدوا بأن ما يفعل بكم خير، وهذا الفرقة المنكرة للحقائق والمعلوم هي فرقة السوفسطائية.
Then he discusses the affirmation of realities and sciences, saying: The rational people have agreed on the affirmation of knowledge and realities for things, except that there is a group that says: There is no reality for anything, nor knowledge of anything, but only opinions and conjectures. And he holds that there is no room for debating these people, because the basis of debate is to affirm one view and negate another, and it is built on a common ground between the disputants. And he has a sound opinion in treating such people, which is to inflict severe punishment on them. Then when they become distressed, it is said to them: There is no reality for anything, but only conjectures and opinions, and the punishment continues until they return from their stubbornness. Likewise, when they deny realities, they admit that they do not know the correctness of their own doctrine, and whoever admits the falsity of his doctrine has spared his opponent the trouble of arguing with him. Likewise, their eagerness for benefits and their avoidance of harms confirms that they know the realities of things; otherwise they would not live. And he says: What they have argued that the senses deceive is evidence of the falsity of their statements, because they have occupied themselves with the senses in demonstrating invalidation. And there is the agnostic group, and debating these people is by posing this question: Do you know that you do not know? If they say yes, they admit the invalidity of their doctrine; if they say no, the discussion continues with them about the negation of knowledge, and in the end they are treated the same as the first group. Then there is a third group that says: The realities of things are dependent on the beliefs of the believers. The refutation of these is to say the opposite to them. If they agree with us, they admit the invalidity of their doctrine; if they disagree, they admit that the believed is not dependent on belief. And they are treated in the same way. When they feel pain, it is said to them: Believe that what is done to you is good. And this group that denies realities and knowledge is the group of sophists.
Source pages p. 57

Commentary
This passage presents Imam al-Nasafi's refutation of three groups that deny the reality of things (sophists). First, he states that all rational people agree on the existence of knowledge and realities. He then identifies the first group, who claim nothing has reality and knowledge is mere opinion. Nasafi argues that debate is useless with them because debate requires a shared premise; instead, they should be treated with coercion until they feel pain, at which point their own principle (no reality) is used against them. Their denial is also self-refuting because they claim not to know the truth of their own doctrine, and their practical behavior (seeking benefit, avoiding harm) shows they implicitly accept realities. He further refutes their argument that senses deceive, noting they use senses to argue invalidation. The second group (agnostics) are refuted by asking if they know they don't know; either answer leads to contradiction or infinite regress. The third group claims realities are relative to beliefs; Nasafi responds by simply asserting the opposite, forcing them to either agree (contradiction) or disagree (denying their own claim). All are to be treated with the same coercive method. The passage ends by identifying these as the sophist sect.

Key Terms
حقائق
علم
ظنون
حواس
سوفسطائية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that Nasafi advocates punishment as a theological argument for the existence of realities, but it is a practical treatment for those who refuse rational debate.
Correction: Nasafi does not use punishment to prove reality; he uses it as a last resort to break stubbornness, after establishing logical contradictions.

Checkpoint
How does Nasafi refute the agnostic group (la-adriyya)?
A) By inflicting punishment
B) By asking if they know that they do not know
C) By showing that senses are deceptive
D) By arguing from consensus of rational people

Key Takeaway
Nasafi refutes sophists by exposing self-contradiction in their positions and, when necessary, applying a coercive method to force them to recognize reality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you find yourself avoiding a painful truth about your own character or obedience, are you not acting like the sophist who denies reality to avoid discomfort? What reality are you denying, and what practical step will you take today to admit it and live accordingly?
?
Have you ever claimed that truth is relative to your personal beliefs or feelings, especially when that truth demands change? Nasafi says that if you feel pain, you acknowledge an independent reality—so what pain in your life is exposing the lie that truth depends on your belief, and what will you do about it?
?
If someone asked you 'Do you know that you do not know?', would you be able to answer honestly without self-deception? In what area of your Islamic knowledge have you settled for 'I don't know' rather than seeking the truth that Allah has already made clear? Commit to one specific unknown you will learn about this week.

The Dirariyya and Najjariyya View on Bodies
Al-Nasafi presents the Dirariyya-Najjariyya view on bodies as aggregated accidents and notes al-Maturidi's inclination.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the minority view that bodies are aggregated accidents and how al-Maturidi inclined toward it without adopting it as official doctrine.

Core Idea
A theological speculation that bodies are merely accidents, not substance.

Al-Nasafi introduces a controversial minority view from the Dirariyya and Najjariyya sects: that bodies are nothing but aggregated accidents. He then notes that Abu Mansur al-Maturidi was personally inclined to this position but did not formalize it as a school doctrine. This reveals the exploratory nature of early kalam debates.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then its translation. Let the author's phrasing sink in.
Notice the dash indicating a pause or parenthetical comment.
2
Identify the sects and their claim
Identify the two sects mentioned and their core claim about bodies being aggregated accidents.
The Arabic term is 'أعراض مجتمعة'.
3
Understand al-Nasafi's stance
Al-Nasafi says he does not settle on a view. This means he withholds judgment on this minority position.
4
Note al-Maturidi's inclination
Abu Mansur al-Maturidi 'inclined' toward this view but did not adopt it as his school. This shows a distinction between personal opinion and official doctrine.
The phrase 'مال إلى هذا' indicates leaning, not full commitment.
5
Reflect on the significance
Consider why al-Nasafi would include an open-ended discussion. It shows the fluidity of early kalam and the willingness to consider various possibilities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويعرض كلام الضرارية، والنجارية - أن الأجسام أعراض مجتمعة إلا أنه لا ينتهي إلى رأي بل يقول - إن أبا منصور مال إلى هذا وإن لم يعتقده مذهبا.
And he presents the statement of al-Dirariyya and al-Najjariyya that bodies are aggregated accidents. However, he does not settle on a view, but says that Abu Mansur inclined to this although he did not adopt it as a school.
Source pages p. 60

Commentary
This passage presents a minority position within kalam, held by the Dirariyya and Najjariyya sects, that bodies are nothing but aggregated accidents (i.e., they have no underlying substance). Al-Nasafi notes that he does not commit to a final verdict on this view, but mentions that Abu Mansur (al-Maturidi) was inclined toward it, though he did not formally adopt it as his school doctrine. This demonstrates the open-ended nature of some theological discussions.

Key Terms
ضرارية
نجارية
أعراض مجتمعة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Maturidi school teaches that bodies are aggregated accidents.
Correction: Al-Maturidi was personally inclined to this view but did not adopt it as a school doctrine. The official Maturidi position does not hold that bodies are aggregated accidents.

Checkpoint
What does al-Nasafi say about Abu Mansur's view on the claim that bodies are aggregated accidents?
A) He fully adopted it
B) He inclined to it but did not adopt it as a school
C) He strongly rejected it
D) He had no opinion

Key Takeaway
Al-Maturidi inclined to the view that bodies are aggregated accidents, but it was never adopted as the official Maturidi school position.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The Dirariyya-Najjariyya view reduces your body to a collection of accidents. How does that sit with your daily experience of being a responsible self before Allah? If your body is just accidents, where does your accountability lie?
?
This passage shows a subtle distinction between personal inclination and public doctrine. In what area of your life are you confusing a personal hunch with a settled truth? What step will you take today to seek clarity from reliable sources?

Three Groups Regarding the World's Origin
Al-Nasafi classifies views on the world's origin into three groups.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three major positions on the origin of the world and which one represents the Islamic orthodox view.

Core Idea
Three worldviews on the world's origin — only one is true.

Before we engage in detailed arguments about the world's beginning, it is essential to know the positions under debate. Imam al-Nasafi here presents a clear three-way classification of views on the matter and craftsmanship of the world. This passage sets the stage for the refutation that follows.

Guided Path
1
Read the classification
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the three groups and their key claims about matter (tina) and craftsmanship (sun'a).
The groups are: truth, eternalists, and philosophers.
2
Identify the key terms
Identify the technical terms: tina (matter), sun'a (craftsmanship), lam yazaliyya (eternalists), hayula (hyle). Understand how these relate to each group.
3
Reflect on the labeling
Notice that the first group is called 'people of truth', implying the other two are false. Consider why the author uses that label.
This sets up the following refutations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم يقسم الناس بعد ذلك إلى أقسام ثلاثة: أ- أهل الحق ويقولون : إن العالم بجواهره وأعراضه حديث الطينة، والصنعة. ب لم يزلية ويقولون: بقدم الطينة والصنعة وهو لم يزل. وقسم أخير يقول : العالم قديم الطينة حديث الصنعة، ومنهم بعض الفلاسفة ج وأصحاب الهيولي.
Then he divides people after that into three sections: A) The people of truth, and they say: the world, with its substances and accidents, is originated in matter and craftsmanship. B) The eternalists, and they say: the matter and craftsmanship are eternal, and it (the world) is eternal. And a final section says: the world is eternal in matter, originated in craftsmanship, and among them are some philosophers and the supporters of hyle.
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage classifies different theological and philosophical positions regarding the origin of the world into three groups. The first group is 'the people of truth' (ahl al-haqq), who affirm that both the matter (al-tina) and the craftsmanship (al-sun'a) of the world are originated (hadith). The second group is 'the eternalists' (al-lam yazaliyya), who hold that both matter and craftsmanship are eternal. The third group holds that matter is eternal but craftsmanship is originated; this includes some philosophers and the supporters of hyle (al-hayula). This classification sets up the framework for the subsequent arguments refuting the latter two views.

Key Terms
أهل الحق
لم يزلية
الطينة
الصنعة
الهيولي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All three views on the world's origin are equally valid opinions.
Correction: Only the first view (ahl al-haqq) is correct; the other two are false according to Islamic theology.

Checkpoint
According to the classification, which group says the world is originated in both matter and craftsmanship?
A) The eternalists
B) The people of truth
C) Those who say matter is eternal and craftsmanship originated
D) The Dahriyya

Key Takeaway
Only the people of truth correctly affirm that both the matter and craftsmanship of the world are originated.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that not only the shape of your life but the very substance of your existence was created from nothing by Allah? How does that change the way you value your possessions and your time?
?
The philosophers thought matter was eternal — do you ever find yourself treating parts of the world (like material systems, social structures, or even your own habits) as if they have always been and will always be, instead of seeing them as contingent and created?
?
If the eternalists deny that the world has a beginning, what in your life have you failed to start anew for Allah? Is there something you treat as if it has no origin in your choices, and therefore no need of repentance?

The Verbal Dispute with the Mu'tazila on God's Location
Nasafi characterizes the dispute as verbal but warns that the Mu'tazila's wording suggests disbelief.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Imam al-Nasafi considers the disagreement with the Mu'tazila over God's location to be verbal yet dangerous, and the principle of safeguarding correct belief in the absence of textual evidence.

Core Idea
Not all disagreements are substantive; some are verbal but can carry implications of disbelief.

In this passage, Imam al-Nasafi addresses the debate with the Mu'tazila about whether God is in a particular place. He argues that the disagreement is essentially verbal, but the Mu'tazila's choice of words can lead to disbelief. Let's examine his reasoning and the lesson for safeguarding correct belief.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the English translation to get the overall meaning.
2
Identify the two key claims
Notice that Nasafi first says the disagreement is verbal, then warns that the apparent meaning of their wording suggests disbelief. These two points set up the tension.
The word 'لَفْظِي' indicates the disagreement is in wording, not essence.
3
Understand Nasafi's question
Nasafi asks what necessity drove the Mu'tazila to say 'He is in every place' without any textual proof. This question shows the rational flaw in their position.
The absence of نص (text) is a key argument against their statement.
4
Grasp the underlying principle
Nasafi concludes that when there is no text, it is obligatory to safeguard the correct meaning. This means we should avoid ambiguous or potentially heretical expressions.
The principle is 'صيانة المعنى' (safeguarding the meaning).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إن خلافنا معهم خلاف لفظي، إلا أن عبارتهم يوهم ظاهرها الكفر ، ويقول لهم: أي ضرورة دعتكم إلى قولكم ، ولم يرد به نص لا في كتاب ولا في سنة، والواجب علينا عند انعدام النص صيانة هذا المعنى؟
Indeed, our disagreement with them is a verbal disagreement, except that the apparent meaning of their expression suggests disbelief. And he says to them: What necessity drove you to your statement, while no text came with it—neither in the Book nor in the Sunna? And the duty upon us in the absence of text is to safeguard this meaning?
Source pages p. 66

Commentary
This passage is a statement on the nature of the debate with the Mu'tazila regarding God's location. The author (likely reporting Nasafi's view) says that the disagreement is verbal (lafẓī), meaning they agree in essence but differ in wording. However, the apparent wording of the Mu'tazila's statement ('He is in every place') implies disbelief (kufr). Then Nasafi questions them: what necessity led them to this statement? No scriptural text supports it. In the absence of text, it is obligatory to safeguard the correct meaning (i.e., avoid such ambiguous statements).

Key Terms
خلاف لفظي
كفر
نص
صيانة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Verbal disagreements are always harmless and can be ignored.
Correction: This passage shows that even a verbal disagreement can be dangerous if the phrasing implies disbelief. The wording itself can lead to heretical belief.

Checkpoint
What does the author say about the Mu'tazila's expression 'God is in every place'?
A) It is correct but needs interpretation
B) Its apparent meaning suggests disbelief
C) It is supported by the Quran
D) It is a valid expression

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila's statement on God's location is problematic because it lacks textual basis and its literal meaning suggests disbelief, so the believer must avoid such phrasing.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
As you recite the Quran, are you careful to only affirm about Allah what He has affirmed, or do you sometimes adopt phrases that lack scriptural basis? The Mu'tazila added a statement without any text — what 'necessities' drive you to speak about Allah without clear evidence? Will you commit today to examine every expression you use about your Creator and abandon those not rooted in revelation?
?
The author says that even a verbal disagreement can imply disbelief. Have you ever dismissed a wrong statement about Allah as 'just wording'? Think of a time you heard someone describe Allah in a way that felt off but you said nothing. What will you do differently now that you know the gravity of even seemingly small verbal slips?
?
The principle here is to safeguard the meaning when there is no text. What does this require of you when you are asked a question about Allah's attributes that you don't know? Will you be humble enough to say 'I do not know' rather than fill the silence with a risky statement? This lesson calls for guarding your tongue — will you be more mindful today of every word you say about the Unseen?

Mu'tazilite Views on God's Speech
The author lists various Mu'tazilite views on God's speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the major Mu'tazilite definitions of God's speech and their common assumption that God is merely the creator of speech.

Core Idea
Mu'tazilites reduced God's speech to created entities, denying its eternal attribute.

The author begins by surveying the diverse Mu'tazilite positions on what exactly constitutes God's speech. From al-Jubba'i's letters and sounds to al-Nazzar's claim that the Qur'an is a body, these definitions share a crucial feature: God is called 'speaker' only in the sense of being the creator of speech. This passage lays out these views before showing their theological consequences.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice how each Mu'tazilite scholar defines God's speech differently.
The passage moves quickly from one scholar to another.
2
Identify the different definitions
List each Mu'tazilite mentioned and their specific definition of God's speech. Compare them.
Al-Jubba'i, Abu Hashim, Ja'far ibn Harb, al-Ka'bi, al-Nazzar.
3
Find the common thread
Despite differences, note that all these scholars agree that God is mutakallim only as the creator of speech. This is the key point.
The phrase 'أي خالق للكلام' is the common link.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider what it means for God's speech to be created. How does this differ from the Sunni view that speech is an eternal attribute?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
جنس الحروف والأصوات، ويزعم الجبائي أن كلامه تعالى عبارة عن حروف مؤلفة بأصوات مقطعة على وجه مخصوص. ويعرض النسفي لتعريف أبي هاشم ويفرق بينه وبين تعريف أبيه، ثم يعرض كلام جعفر بن حرب، وجعفر بن مبشر، ويلخصه في أنهما يقولان بأن كلام الله خلقه الله في اللوح المحفوظ، ولا يجوز أن ينقل عنه، وما يقرأ ويحفظ فهو حكاية القرآن. وهذا القول نفسه هو قول الكعبي ومن تابعه من معتزلة بغداد أما النظّار فيقول: إن القرآن جسم، وعلى هذا فإن الله عندهم جميعاً متكلم أي خالق للكلام، ويخرج النسفي من جملة هؤلاء من لا يمكنهم القول بأن الله متكلم مثل أبي بكر الأصم، ومعمر السلمي، وثمامة بن الأشرس ويقول: إنه بناء على كلامهم لا يكون الله أمراً ولا ناهيا، ولا يكون القرآن كلامه، وفي هذا رد للشرائع، وتكذيب للرسول.
The genus of letters and sounds, and al-Jubba'i claims that His speech—may He be exalted—consists of letters composed of segmented sounds in a specific manner. And al-Nasafi presents the definition of Abu Hashim and distinguishes between it and the definition of his father. Then he presents the statement of Ja'far ibn Harb and Ja'far ibn Mubashshir and summarizes that they both say that the speech of God is something God created in the Preserved Tablet, and it is not permissible for it to be transmitted from it, and what is read and memorized is a narrative (hikaya) of the Qur'an. And this very statement is the statement of al-Ka'bi and those who followed him from the Mu'tazila of Baghdad. As for al-Nazzar, he says: the Qur'an is a body (jism). And upon this, according to all of them, God is mutakallim (speaking) i.e., the creator of speech. And al-Nasafi excludes from among these those who cannot say that God is mutakallim, such as Abu Bakr al-Asamm, Mu'ammar al-Sulami, and Thumama ibn al-Ashras, and he says: based on their statement, God is neither a commander nor a prohibitor, nor is the Qur'an His speech, and in this is a refutation of the laws and a denial of the Messenger.
Source pages p. 70

Commentary
This passage is a survey of Mu'tazilite positions on the nature of God's speech. The author begins by mentioning al-Jubba'i's view that God's speech consists of composed letters and sounds. Then he presents Abu Hashim's definition, distinguishing it from his father's. He then summarizes the views of Ja'far ibn Harb and Ja'far ibn Mubashshir, who say God created speech in the Preserved Tablet and what we read is only a narrative (hikaya) of it, not the speech itself. The same view is held by al-Ka'bi and the Baghdad Mu'tazila. Al-Nazzar goes further, claiming the Qur'an is a body. For all these groups, God is called mutakallim only in the sense of being the creator of speech. The author then points out that some Mu'tazila (Abu Bakr al-Asamm, Mu'ammar al-Sulami, Thumama) cannot even say God is mutakallim on their principles. The consequence is that God is not truly commanding or prohibiting, and the Qur'an is not His speech—which amounts to a rejection of the revealed laws (shara'i') and a denial of the Prophet.

Key Terms
كلام الله
متكلم
خالق للكلام
حكاية القرآن
جسم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What common conclusion do all the Mu'tazilite views in this passage share?
A) God's speech is eternal and uncreated.
B) God is mutakallim only as the creator of speech.
C) The Qur'an is a body composed of letters.
D) The Qur'an cannot be transmitted from the Preserved Tablet.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilites agreed that God's speech is created and that God is 'speaker' only as its creator, differing only on the form of that creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear the Quran recited, do you feel the presence of Allah’s eternal speech or do you hear only sounds? What would change in your heart if you truly believed each verse is uncreated and directly from Him? Will you commit to reciting with more presence and awe?
?
The Mu'tazilites reduced the Quran to a created entity, making it ordinary. Have you ever treated the Quran as ordinary speech, reading it without reverence? What practice can you adopt tonight to honor it as the divine word—such as making wudu before touching it or pausing to reflect on a verse?
?
If you were to defend the belief that the Quran is uncreated, could you do so with clarity? This lesson shows how easily one can slip into reducing God’s speech. Are you prepared to correct someone who says the Quran is just human words? What will you say?

The Rejection of Divine Command and Revelation
The author draws the destructive consequence of some Mu'tazilite views.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why some Mu'tazilite positions logically lead to a denial of God's commands and the revelation itself.

Core Idea
Denying that God is truly speaking undermines the very foundations of Sharia.

After presenting the various Mu'tazilite definitions, the author identifies a dangerous consequence. Some Mu'tazila, like Abu Bakr al-Asamm, cannot even call God 'speaker' on their own principles. This, the author argues, strips God of command and prohibition and reduces the Qur'an to a creation, thereby refuting the laws and denying the Prophet.

Read the passage

Their own principles prevent them from attributing speech to God.
3
Grasp the consequence
The author states that on their view, God is neither a commander nor a prohibitor, and the Qur'an is not His speech. This is a rejection of the laws (shara'i') and a denial of the Messenger.
This is the climax of the passage.

The Quran as Uncreated Attribute: The Maturidi Distinction
Nasafi presents the Maturidi distinction between the uncreated attribute and created expressions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Maturidi distinction between the Quran as an uncreated divine attribute and the created letters and words.

Core Idea
The Quran's essence is uncreated; its expression is created.

Nasafi clarifies that the Mu'tazila are arguing about the wrong thing. The real issue is not whether the Quran is created or uncreated in its entirety, but that it has two aspects: the eternal attribute of God and the temporal expressions. This distinction resolves many confusions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let Nasafi's own wording show the distinction.
Notice how Nasafi first sets up the context of the Mu'tazila speaking outside the point of disagreement.
2
Identify the two aspects of the Quran
The passage explicitly states that the Quran is an attribute subsisting in God's essence, uncreated; but the letters and words are created. Locate this distinction.
This is the core Maturidi position.
3
Consider why the Mu'tazila are wrong
Nasafi says the Mu'tazila discuss the issue in other than the point of disagreement. Why? Because they treat the Quran as a single entity, while it has two aspects.
4
Test your understanding
Answer the checkpoint question to see if you can apply the distinction.
The quiz in the source reinforces this point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبعد أن ينتهي من رده على شبههم العقلية، يبدأ في الرد على ما تعلقوا به من النقل، ويقول بعد ذلك: إن المعتزلة يتكلمون في المسألة في غير محل الخلاف، فنحن نقول: إن القرآن صفة قائمة بذات الباري غير مخلوق، أما الحروف والألفاظ فهي مخلوقة ، وكلامهم على الحنابلة الذين يقولون : إن كلام الله هو الحروف المؤلفة والأصوات، وأنه حال في الألسنة، والمصاحف، ومع هذا فإنه غير مخلوق. ويعرض لكلام من يقول : إن القرآن كلام الله، ولا يزيد أهو مخلوق أو غير مخلوق؟ ويبطله إلا أن أمانة النسفي العلمية تجعله يقول: إن علي كل واحد ألا يجيب عن أحد يسأل في كلام موجه من جهتين إلا بعد التأمل، والتفحص فيما يقوله، فإذا كان يري بكلامه إرادة الصفة القائمة بالذات فلا ضرر، أما إذا أراد العبارات فهنا يكون الحديث.
And after he finishes his refutation of their rational doubts, he begins to refute what they cling to from transmitted evidence, and he says after that: The Mu'tazila speak about the issue in other than the point of disagreement. We say: indeed the Quran is an attribute subsisting in the essence of the Creator, uncreated; as for the letters and words, they are created. And his speech [turns] against the Hanbalites who say: indeed the speech of God is the composed letters and sounds, and that it inheres in tongues and copies, and with this it is uncreated. And he presents the speech of one who says: the Quran is the speech of God, and does not add whether it is created or uncreated, and he invalidates it. However, the scientific integrity of al-Nasafi makes him say: indeed everyone should not answer anyone who asks about speech directed from two aspects except after reflection and examination of what he says. If he intends by his speech the meaning of the attribute subsisting in the essence, there is no harm; but if he intends the expressions, then here is the discussion.
Source pages p. 72

Commentary
This passage covers Nasafi's response to Mu'tazila scriptural arguments and his critique of other views. After refuting their rational doubts, he begins refuting their scriptural evidence. He says the Mu'tazila are discussing the issue outside the point of disagreement. The Maturidi position is: the Quran is an attribute subsisting in God's essence, uncreated; the letters and words are created. He then critiques the Hanbali position that God's speech is the composed letters and sounds, present in tongues and copies, and yet it is uncreated. Nasafi also addresses those who simply say 'the Quran is the speech of God' without clarifying whether it is created or not, and he invalidates that position as incomplete. Then he demonstrates the care of the scholar: one must not answer a question about speech without careful consideration. If by 'speech' the questioner means the attribute subsisting in God's essence, there is no harm; but if he means the expressions, then the discussion is different. This highlights the need for precision in theological discourse.

صفة قائمة بالذات
غير مخلوق

Misconception: The Quran is either entirely uncreated or entirely created; it is a single entity with no distinction.
Correction: Nasafi shows that the Quran has two aspects: the eternal attribute (uncreated) and the temporal expressions (created).

A) The Quran as a whole is uncreated, including the letters and words.
B) The Quran as an attribute is uncreated, but the letters and words are created.
C) The Quran is both created and uncreated simultaneously.

Key Takeaway
The Quran is not a single entity; it has an eternal attribute (uncreated) and temporal expressions (created).

Critique of Hanbali and Ambiguous Positions, and the Scholar's Caution
Nasafi critiques the Hanbali and ambiguous positions and advocates careful discernment in answering.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Nasafi rejects both the Hanbali identification of God's speech with letters/sounds and the ambiguous position of not specifying createdness, and why he insists on careful discernment in answering.

Core Idea
Precision in speech about Allah requires distinguishing the eternal attribute from temporal expressions.

Nasafi critiques those who say God's speech is the letters and sounds in tongues and copies, yet claim it is uncreated. He also dismisses those who simply say 'the Quran is God's speech' without clarifying its status. Finally, he models scholarly integrity: do not answer without understanding the questioner's intent.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let Nasafi's own wording show the critiques.

2
Understand the Hanbali position and its flaw
The Hanbalites say God's speech is the composed letters and sounds, inhering in tongues and copies, yet still uncreated. Nasafi implies this is contradictory because letters and sounds are temporal.
This violates the distinction between attribute and expression.
3
Understand the ambiguous position and its flaw
Some say 'the Quran is God's speech' without stating whether it is created or uncreated. Nasafi invalidates this because it leaves the crucial question unanswered.
Clarity is required in theological statements.
4
Learn the principle of scholarly caution
Nasafi says one should not answer a question about 'speech' without first reflecting on the questioner's intention. If they mean the attribute, no harm; if the expressions, then discussion is needed.
This shows the importance of precise language.
