The Terminology of Hikaya: Can Human Expressions Be Called a Narrative of God's Speech?
Nasafi asks about permissibility of 'hikaya' and cites positions of early and later scholars.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the differing positions among early and later Maturidis, Ash'ari, and Qalanisi on using the term 'hikaya' for human expressions of God's speech.

Core Idea
Not all theological terms are permitted – the term 'hikaya' faced caution.

This short passage from Nasafi's Tabṣirat al-Adilla addresses a precise terminological question: should we call our verbal expressions a 'hikaya' (narrative) of God's eternal speech? The answer reveals how early Maturidi scholars differed from Ash'ari and later Maturidi thinkers, highlighting the care needed in theological language.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it, then read the English translation to get the gist.
Notice the concise style of the source.
2
Identify the three positions
Find the three positions mentioned: early Maturidis (allowed), Ash'ari and Qalanisi (disallowed), later Maturidis (joined the prohibition).
The passage uses 'al-awā'il' for early scholars and 'al-muta'akhkhirūn' for later ones.
3
Reflect on the theological sensitivity
Think about why the term 'hikaya' might be problematic. The commentary explains that it could imply the Quran is merely a human narrative, which is why later scholars avoided it.
This connects to preserving the divine nature of the Quran.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويسأل النسفي هل يجوز القول بأن العبارات حكاية عن كلام الله؟ ويجيب بأن الأوائل من أصحابه قالوا بجواز ذلك، محتجين بأن لفظة الحكاية مثل لفظة العبارة، أما الأشعري، والقلانسي فلا يجوزان ذلك، وينضم إليهما المتأخرون من الماتريدية.
And al-Nasafi asks: Is it permissible to say that the expressions are a narrative (hikaya) of the speech of God? And he answers that the early ones among his companions said it is permissible, arguing that the word 'narrative' (hikaya) is like the word 'expression' (ibara). As for al-Ash'ari and al-Qalanisi, they do not permit that, and the later ones among the Maturidis join them.
Source pages p. 72

Commentary
This passage addresses a specific terminology issue: is it permissible to say that human expressions are a 'hikaya' (narrative) of God's speech? Nasafi notes that the early Maturidis allowed it, arguing that the term 'hikaya' is similar to 'ibara' (expression). However, Ash'ari and al-Qalanisi disallowed it, and the later Maturidis joined them in prohibiting the term. This shows the evolution of theological terminology and the caution required to avoid implying that the Quran is merely a human narrative.

Correction: The passage shows that scholars debated the term precisely to avoid that implication, and most later scholars prohibited it.

Key Takeaway

The Mu'tazila Agreement and Disagreement on Divine Speech
Nasafi reports the Mu'tazila position and distinguishes it from the Maturidi view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the specific point of agreement and the crucial difference between the Mu'tazila and Maturidis regarding the nature of the Qur'an's relationship to Allah's eternal speech.

Core Idea
The Qur'anic expressions indicate divine speech, but do they convey a created text or an eternal attribute?

In this passage, Imam Nasafi reports the view of three prominent Mu'tazila scholars who agree with the Maturidis that the Qur'anic expressions point to speech in the Preserved Tablet. Yet they diverge on what exactly those expressions convey. This lesson unpacks that agreement and the critical difference that marks the Maturidi position.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the scholars
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Note the three Mu'tazila scholars named: Ja'far ibn Harb, Ja'far ibn Mubashshir, and al-Ka'bi.
These are well-known Mu'tazila figures.
2
Notice the common ground
Both sides agree that the speech of Allah is in the Preserved Tablet and that the revealed expressions are indications of that speech.
3
Pinpoint the point of divergence
The Mu'tazila say the expressions convey what is in the Preserved Tablet (the verbal text), while the Maturidis say they convey the attribute that subsists in Allah's essence.
This is the key difference: created text vs. eternal attribute.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this distinction matters: it determines whether the Qur'an is created or eternal, a central debate in Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وجعفر بن حرب وجعفر بن مبشر والكعبي من المعتزلة يقولون بقولنا: إن كلام الله في اللوح المحفوظ والمنزل عبارات دالة عليه، غير أنهم يقولون: إن هذه العبارات مؤدية ما فى اللوح المحفوظ، وهنا الخلاف بيننا وبينهم حيث نقول: هي مؤدية ما قام بالذات من الصفة.
And Ja'far ibn Harb, Ja'far ibn Mubashshir, and al-Ka'bi from the Mu'tazila say with our statement: that the speech of Allah is in the Preserved Tablet, and the revealed expressions are indications of it, except that they say: these expressions convey what is in the Preserved Tablet, and here is the difference between us and them, for we say: they convey what subsists of the attribute in the Essence.
Source pages p. 72

Commentary
This passage is a report of the Mu'tazila position on the nature of the Qur'an. Nasafi begins by naming specific Mu'tazila theologians: Ja'far ibn Harb, Ja'far ibn Mubashshir, and al-Ka'bi. These scholars agree with the Maturidi position in saying that the speech of Allah is in the Preserved Tablet and that the revealed Qur'anic expressions are indications (dāllah) of that speech. However, they differ on what exactly those expressions convey. The Mu'tazila hold that the expressions convey (mu'addiya) the content of the Preserved Tablet—namely, the verbal text. In contrast, the Maturidis hold that the expressions convey the attribute (al-ṣifah) that subsists in the Essence of Allah (qā'im bil-dhāt). The key point of disagreement is whether the Qur'anic speech is an eternal attribute of Allah or a created phenomenon in a locus. The passage sets up the foundational dispute in the theology of divine speech.

Key Terms
اللوح المحفوظ
العبارات
الصفة القائمة بالذات
المعتزلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila and Maturidis are often thought to completely disagree on the Qur'an.
Correction: As seen in this passage, they agree on some points: both affirm that the Qur'an is in the Preserved Tablet and that the revealed text indicates it. The disagreement is on what exactly is conveyed.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, what is the shared point of agreement between the Mu'tazila and the Maturidis regarding the Qur'an?
A) That the Qur'an is created
B) That the Qur'an is an eternal attribute of Allah
C) That the speech of Allah is in the Preserved Tablet and the revealed expressions indicate it
D) That the expressions are identical with the attribute

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila and Maturidis agree that the Qur'anic expressions indicate the speech in the Preserved Tablet, but they differ on whether those expressions convey the verbal text of the Tablet or the eternal attribute of Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?

The Correct Stance on the Qur'an and the Hanbalī View
Nasafi clarifies the Maturidi position on the Qur'an and critiques the Hanbali view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Maturidi distinction between the uncreated attribute of the Qur'an and the created letters and words, and the critique of both the Mu'tazila and Hanbali positions.

Core Idea
The Qur'an's meaning is uncreated, but its physical expression is created.

In this passage, Nasafi clarifies the real point of disagreement about the Qur'an. He contrasts the Mu'tazila and Hanbali extremes with the balanced Maturidi position. Understanding this distinction is key to avoiding confusion in theological discussions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the three groups
Read the Arabic passage once. Then read the translation. Notice that Nasafi mentions the Mu'tazila, the Hanabila, and vague speakers. Identify what each says about the Qur'an.
The Mu'tazila argue about the wrong thing, the Hanbali identify speech with letters/sounds, and some are ambiguous.
2
Distinguish between the uncreated attribute and created letters
Find the sentence where Nasafi states the Maturidi view: the Qur'an is an attribute subsisting in Allah's Essence, uncreated, but the letters and words are created. This is the core distinction.
This avoids both the Mu'tazili error of creation and the Hanbali error of identifying God's speech with physical phenomena.
3
Analyze the critique of the Hanbali position
See how Nasafi refutes the Hanbali claim that the speech of Allah is composed letters and sounds present in copies and tongues, yet uncreated. Understand why this is problematic.
The Hanbali view mixes the uncreated attribute with created physical expressions.
4
Understand the methodological caution
Read the last part about answering ambiguous questions. Nasafi advises not to answer hastily but to examine what the questioner means by 'speech'.
This shows Nasafi's academic honesty and nuanced approach to dialogue.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إن المعتزلة يتكلمون في المسألة في غير محل الخلاف، فنحن نقول: إن القرآن صفة قائمة بذات الباري غير مخلوق، أما الحروف والألفاظ فهي مخلوقة ، وكلامهم على الحنابلة الذين يقولون : إن كلام الله هو الحروف المؤلفة والأصوات، وأنه حال في الألسنة، والمصاحف، ومع هذا فإنه غير مخلوق. ويعرض لكلام من يقول : إن القرآن كلام الله، ولا يزيد أهو مخلوق أو غير مخلوق؟ ويبطله إلا أن أمانة النسفي العلمية تجعله يقول: إن علي كل واحد ألا يجيب عن أحد يسأل في كلام موجه من جهتين إلا بعد التأمل، والتفحص فيما يقوله، فإذا كان يريد بكلامه إرادة الصفة القائمة بالذات فلا ضرر، أما إذا أراد العبارات فهنا يكون الحديث.
Indeed, the Mu'tazila are speaking in the matter about what is not the point of disagreement. For we say: the Qur'an is an attribute subsisting in the Essence of the Creator, uncreated; as for the letters and words, they are created. And his speech against the Hanabila who say: the speech of Allah is the composed letters and sounds, and that it is present in tongues and copies, and despite this it is uncreated. And he presents the statement of one who says: the Qur'an is the speech of Allah, but does not add whether it is created or uncreated? And he refutes it, except that Nasafi's academic honesty makes him say: that it is incumbent upon everyone not to answer someone who asks about speech addressed from two directions except after reflection and examination of what he says. So if by his speech he intends the attribute subsisting in the Essence, then no harm; but if he intends the expressions, then here is the discussion.
Source pages p. 72

Commentary
This passage presents Nasafi's clarification of the real issue in the debate over the Qur'an. He begins by stating that the Mu'tazila are arguing about the wrong thing (fī ghayr maḥall al-khilāf). The correct Maturidi position is that the Qur'an is an attribute (ṣifah) subsisting in the Essence of Allah, not created. The letters and words, however, are created—this distinguishes between the uncreated meaning and the created physical expression. Nasafi then criticizes the Hanabila (literalist traditionists) who identify the speech of Allah directly with composed letters and sounds, claiming that these are present in tongues and copies, yet still uncreated. This is a rejection of the Hanbali anthropomorphic position that equates the divine attribute with physical phenomena. Next, Nasafi presents and refutes the position of those who say 'the Qur'an is the speech of Allah' without specifying whether it is created or not, saying that this ambiguity is unacceptable. However, he adds a methodological caution: out of academic honesty, one should not answer a questioner who asks about 'speech' (kalām) that is directed from two perspectives without first carefully considering what the questioner means. If the speaker intends the attribute subsisting in the Essence, there is no harm in affirming it as uncreated; but if he intends the expressions, then that is where the discussion arises. This shows Nasafi's nuanced approach to theological dialogue.

Key Terms
الصفة القائمة بالذات
الحروف والألفاظ
الحنابلة
الخلاف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some assume the Hanbali position on the Qur'an is identical to the Maturidi because both affirm the Qur'an is uncreated.
Correction: Nasafi shows that the Hanbali identification of God's speech with letters and sounds is a major error; the Maturidi position distinguishes between the uncreated attribute and the created physical expressions.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, what is the correct Maturidi position regarding the Qur'an?
A) The Qur'an is an uncreated attribute of Allah, and the letters and words are also uncreated
B) The Qur'an is a created attribute of Allah
C) The Qur'an is an uncreated attribute of Allah, and the letters and words are created
D) The Qur'an is identical to the sounds and letters

Key Takeaway
The Qur'an is uncreated as an attribute of Allah, but its physical expressions (letters and words) are created.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite the Qur'an, do you sense a difference between the created sounds leaving your lips and the uncreated meaning they convey? How will you cultivate more awe for the divine attribute behind the physical words?
?
Nasafi advises not to answer a religious question without reflecting on the asker's intention. Think of a time you gave a quick answer about Allah. What harm might have come from that, and how will you pause in the future?
?
If you have ever treated a physical mushaf as if it were itself eternal, how does this lesson change your veneration? What tangible step will you take to honor the Qur'an rightly—as both created pages and uncreated truth?

Denying Takwin as an Attribute: Mu'tazila and the Majority View
Author contrasts two denials of takwin as an attribute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that both the Mu'tazila and the majority of theologians (including Ash'ariyya) deny takwin as a distinct attribute, but for different reasons: the Mu'tazila because they deny any attributes of essence, the majority because they identify takwin with the created thing itself.

Core Idea
Two opposite camps both end up denying takwin as a real attribute.

The theological debate on takwin revolves around whether it is a distinct attribute of Allah or merely identical to the act of creation. In this passage, al-Nasafi presents two major positions: the Mu'tazila and Najjariyya who reject all attributes, and the majority (including the Ash'ariyya) who reduce takwin to the created thing itself. Understanding these views is essential for grasping the Maturidi position that follows.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two groups
Read the passage and identify the two groups mentioned: the Mu'tazila/Najjariyya and the majority (al-Qalānisī, Kullābiyya, Ash'ariyya).
The phrase 'عامتهم' likely refers to the majority of theologians, not the Mu'tazila.
2
Note the reasoning of each group
The Mu'tazila and Najjariyya prevent any attribute from subsisting in God's essence, so they deny takwin. The majority say takwin is not a meaning other than the mukawwan; it is identical to it.
This means the majority affirm a form of takwin but deny it as a distinct attribute.
3
Consider the implications
Both positions ultimately deny takwin as a real, distinct attribute of Allah, but from different starting points. This sets up the debate for the Maturidi view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وثانيا: يقول : إن المعتزلة والنجارية يمنعون قيام أي صفة بذات القديم، فهم لا يقولون بالتكوين، ويقول عامتهم مثل قول القلانسي والكلابية، والأشاعرة: إن التكوين ليس بمعنى غير المكون، بل هو عين المكون. أما أبو الهذيل العلاف، وبشر بن المعتمر ، ومعمر، وابن الراوندي، والكرامية فيقولون : إن التكوين معنى وراء المكون ، غير أنهم يقولون هو غير المكون ويقول بشر، وابن الراوندي: إن التكوين حدث لا في محل.
And secondly, he says: Indeed the Mu‘tazila and the Najjāriyya prevent the subsistence of any attribute in the essence of the Eternal; thus they do not affirm takwīn. And the majority of them – like the statement of al-Qalānisī, al-Kullābiyya, and the Ashā‘ira – say that takwīn is not a meaning other than al-mukawwan (the thing brought into existence); rather it is identical to the mukawwan. As for Abū al-Hudhayl al-‘Allāf, Bishr ibn al-Mu‘tamir, Mu‘ammar, Ibn al-Rāwandī, and the Karrāmiyya, they say that takwīn is a meaning beyond the mukawwan, but they say it is other than the mukawwan. And Bishr and Ibn al-Rāwandī say: Takwīn is an event (ḥadath) not in a locus.
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This passage is a classification and statement of various theological positions on takwīn. The author presents three broad camps. First, the Mu‘tazila and Najjāriyya deny that any attribute can subsist in God’s essence; consequently they deny takwīn entirely. Second, the majority of theologians – al-Qalānisī, the Kullābiyya, and the Ash‘ariyya – hold that takwīn is not a distinct meaning separate from al-mukawwan (the thing produced), but is exactly the mukawwan itself; in other words, the act of bringing into existence is not a real attribute. Third, a group including Abū al-Hudhayl al-‘Allāf, Bishr ibn al-Mu‘tamir, Mu‘ammar, Ibn al-Rāwandī, and the Karrāmiyya assert that takwīn is a meaning beyond the mukawwan, but they differ among themselves: Bishr and Ibn al-Rāwandī specify that this takwīn is an event (ḥadath) not subsisting in any locus (lā fī maḥall). This sets the stage for the author’s own Maturidi view which will follow.

Key Terms
المعتزلة
النجارية
القلانسي
الكلابية
الأشاعرة
المكون

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that all non-Maturidis deny takwin as an attribute; actually the Ash'ariyya affirm takwin but identify it with the created thing.
Correction: The Ash'ariyya do not deny takwin, but they do not consider it a distinct attribute separate from the act of creation.

Checkpoint
What do the majority (including Ash'ariyya) say about takwin?
A) Takwin is a distinct attribute subsisting in God's essence.
B) Takwin is an event not in a locus.
C) Takwin is not a meaning other than the mukawwan; it is identical to it.
D) Takwin does not exist at all.

Key Takeaway
Both the Mu'tazila and the majority of theologians end up denying takwin as a distinct attribute, though for different reasons.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If takwin is not a distinct attribute, then is Allah's act of creation merely identical to the thing created? How does that sit with your sense of Allah's eternal, uncreated power? Have you ever caught yourself thinking of Allah's creating as something that happens in time, rather than an expression of His eternal will?
?
The Mu'tazila and Najjariyya deny attributes to protect Allah's oneness. The Ash'ariyya reduce takwin to the creature. Which tendency do you see more in your own heart: a fear of affirming attributes, or a comfort in reducing them to effects? How has that shaped your relationship with Allah's names like al-Khāliq?
?
Imagine that in your prayer you thank Allah for creating you. If takwin is only the result (you), are you thanking Him for something that is identical to you rather than His act? What does this teach you about the importance of properly understanding Allah's attributes—not just as theology, but as worship?

Takwin as a Distinct Meaning: Views of Abu al-Hudhayl and Others
Author lists those who affirm takwin as a distinct meaning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that some theologians, including Abu al-Hudhayl al-'Allaf and Bishr ibn al-Mu'tamir, affirm takwin as a meaning beyond the mukawwan, with Bishr and Ibn al-Rawandi specifying it as an event not in a locus.

Core Idea
A minority of theologians affirm takwin as a real, distinct attribute of Allah.

Not all scholars identified takwin with the created thing. A distinct group, including key Mu'tazili figures and the Karramiyya, argued that takwin is a meaning beyond the mukawwan. This position preserves takwin as a real attribute, though with different nuances. Al-Nasafi presents this view to set up the Maturidi affirmation of takwin as an eternal attribute.

Guided Path
1
List the names mentioned
Identify the five individuals/groups: Abu al-Hudhayl al-'Allaf, Bishr ibn al-Mu'tamir, Muammar, Ibn al-Rawandi, and the Karramiyya.
These figures span both Mu'tazila and other sectarian schools.
2
Understand their common position
All five affirm that takwin is a meaning beyond the mukawwan, i.e., it is a distinct attribute separate from the created thing.
They do not identify takwin with the mukawwan.
3
Note the specific nuance of Bishr and Ibn al-Rawandi
They specify that takwin is a hadath (event) not subsisting in any locus (la fi mahall). This means it is an occurrence without a substrate.
This is a unique description within the group.

?

First Objection: Takwin is an Innovation
Al-Nasafi refutes the claim that only one person held takwin.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Nasafi defends the doctrine of takwin by tracing it to early Hanafi authorities, countering the claim that it is an innovation.

Core Idea
Takwin is not an innovation; it is rooted in the earliest Hanafi teachings.

This lesson examines the first objection raised against the doctrine of takwin – that it was invented by an obscure figure around 400 AH. Al-Nasafi refutes this by pointing to the writings of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad ibn al-Hasan, and al-Tahawi, showing that takwin has always been part of Sunni theology. The passage demonstrates the authority of tradition in theological argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Understand the opponent's claim that takwin was invented by a single obscure individual.
The opponent's claim is that only one man in Marw held this view.
2
Identify refutation strategy
Notice that al-Nasafi flatly denies the claim and lists four major Hanafi authorities who affirmed takwin.
He cites Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad ibn al-Hasan, and al-Tahawi.
3
Examine textual evidence
Read the quotations from al-Fiqh al-Akbar and al-Tahawi's creed that support takwin.
These texts show that takwin was considered eternal and preceded creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم يعرض تشنيعات الخصوم وشبههم ويلخصها في أمرين: الأول: القول بالتكوين لم يقل به أحد سوى رجل بمرو يسمى الزابر شائي نشأ بحد الأربعمائة من الهجرة. ويكذب النسفى هذا القول قائلا إن أبا حنيفة وأبا يوسف. ومحمد بن الحسن والطحاوي قالوا بالتكوين. فقد قال أبو حنيفة في الفقه الأكبر مع شرحه لعلي القاري ٢١، ٢٢ وخالق" بتخليقه والتخليق صفة له في الأزل، وفاعلا بفعله، والفعل صفة له في الأزل" وقال بذلك الطحاوي أيضا في عقيدته السلفية عقيدته السلفية مع شرحها ص ٦٧ ٦٨: وله معنى الربوبية ولا مربوب، ومعنى الخالق، ولا مخلوق، ويعني بالخالق المخرج للشيء من العدم إلى الوجود، ويقول: "وكما أنه محيي الموتى بعدما أحيا استحق هذا الاسم قبل إحيائهم كذلك استحق اسم الخالق قبل إنشائهم".
Then he presents the objections (tashnī‘āt) of the opponents and their doubts and summarizes them in two matters. The first: The statement of takwīn has not been said by anyone except a man in Marw named al-Zābir Shā’ī, who arose around 400 AH. And al-Nasafī denies this statement, saying that Abū Ḥanīfa, Abū Yūsuf, Muḥammad ibn al-Ḥasan, and al-Ṭaḥāwī said takwīn. For Abū Ḥanīfa said in al-Fiqh al-Akbar with its commentary by ‘Alī al-Qārī: 'And Creator (Khāliq) by His creating (takhliq), and takhlīq is an attribute for Him in eternity, and Agent (fā‘il) by His action (fi‘l), and action is an attribute for Him in eternity.' And al-Ṭaḥāwī also said this in his Salafī creed: 'And He has the meaning of lordship (rubūbiyya) without any lorded-over, and the meaning of Creator (khāliq) without any created, and He means by Creator the one who brings things from non-existence to existence.' And he says: 'Just as He is the Reviver of the dead after He revived, He deserved this name before reviving them, thus He deserved the name Creator before creating them.'
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This passage is the presentation of the first objection (shubha) raised by opponents and its refutation. The opponents claim that the doctrine of takwīn was invented by a single obscure individual from Marw named al-Zābir Shā’ī around 400 AH, implying it is an innovation. Al-Nasafī flatly denies (yukadhdhib) this claim and counters by listing four major authorities who affirmed takwīn: Abū Ḥanīfa, Abū Yūsuf, Muḥammad ibn al-Ḥasan, and al-Ṭaḥāwī. He then provides textual evidence: a quotation from Abū Ḥanīfa’s al-Fiqh al-Akbar (as recorded in the commentary by ‘Alī al-Qārī) where Abū Ḥanīfa states that takhlīq (creating) is an attribute of God in eternity, and that God is Agent by His action, which is also eternal. Next he quotes al-Ṭaḥāwī’s creed, where al-Ṭaḥāwī affirms that God has the meaning of Creator without any created object existing, and that He deserves the name 'Creator' before creation, just as He deserves the name 'Reviver' before actually reviving the dead. Thus the doctrine is not a later innovation but part of the classical Sunni heritage.

Key Terms

شبه (shubah)
الزابر شائي (al-Zābir Shā’ī)
أبو حنيفة (Abū Ḥanīfa)
أبو يوسف (Abū Yūsuf)
محمد بن الحسن (Muḥammad ibn al-Ḥasan)
الطحاوي (al-Ṭaḥāwī)
الفقه الأكبر (al-Fiqh al-Akbar)
علي القاري (‘Alī al-Qārī)
العقيدة السلفية (al-‘Aqīda al-Salafiyya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the view of takwin is a late innovation invented by an obscure individual around 400 AH.
Correction: Al-Nasafi shows that takwin was affirmed by the earliest Hanafi authorities like Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad ibn al-Hasan, and al-Tahawi, making it part of the classical Sunni heritage.

Key Takeaway
The doctrine of takwin is not a later innovation but is attested by the earliest Hanafi authorities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

What would it take for you to defend that truth calmly and with evidence, as al-Nasafī did? Identify one such truth and resolve to speak it with wisdom this week.
?

How does God's self-sufficiency before creation challenge your tendency to find identity in accomplishments? Pause and thank God that He is complete without you, and ask Him to free you from the need to prove yourself.

Refuting Mu'tazila Interpretations of Moses' Request
Al-Nasafi refutes two Mu'tazila interpretations of Moses' request.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two Mu'tazila interpretations of Moses' request in Quran 7:143 and how al-Nasafi invalidates them as distortions of the plain meaning.

Core Idea
The Quran's plain meaning is not to be twisted by speculative interpretation.

In this passage, al-Nasafi addresses two attempts by the Mu'tazila to reinterpret the clear words of Prophet Moses' request to see Allah. These reinterpretations aim to avoid affirming the possibility of seeing Allah in the afterlife, but al-Nasafi shows they are baseless. Let us examine the two interpretations and his refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the two interpretations mentioned.
2
Identify the two interpretations
The Mu'tazila claim that Moses either asked for a sign (not vision) or that he asked on behalf of his people to demonstrate impossibility. Spot both.
3
Understand al-Nasafi's response
Al-Nasafi says he invalidates these interpretations with multiple proofs. Reflect on why he rejects them so strongly.
This connects to the Ahl al-Sunna belief that seeing Allah is possible in the afterlife.
4
Consider the broader lesson
Ask yourself: Why is it important to defend the literal meaning of the Quran against re-interpretation? How does this protect faith?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويذكر خلال ذلك تفسيرات المعتزلة لآية ﴿ رَبِّ أَرِنِي أَنظُرْ إِلَيْكَ ﴾ [الأعراف: ١٤٣] من ذلك قولهم: إن موسى عليه السلام لم يطلب الرؤية، وإنما طلب آية يستدل بها على الله، ويفسد النسفي هذا الكلام بأكثر من دليل. ومن ذلك: أن موسى لم يطلب الرؤية لنفسه، وإنما طلبها قومه، لأنهم يطلبونها فأراد بالطلب أن يبين الله لهم استحالتها. ويبطل النسفي هذا الكلام.
And during that he mentions the interpretations of the Muʿtazila regarding the verse 'My Lord, show me [Yourself] that I may look at You' (Q. 7:143). Among them is their saying: Moses, peace be upon him, did not request vision, but rather requested a sign by which he could infer God. Al-Nasafi invalidates this statement with more than one proof. And among them: that Moses did not request vision for himself, but rather his people requested it, because they were asking for it; so he intended by his request that God show them its impossibility. And al-Nasafi refutes this statement.
Source pages p. 80

Commentary
This passage records al-Nasafi's refutation of two Mu'tazilī interpretations of the verse where Moses asks to see God. The first interpretation: Moses did not actually ask to see God; rather, he asked for a miraculous sign (āya) to prove God's existence. The second interpretation: Moses asked on behalf of his people, who were demanding to see God, and he intended by his request to demonstrate the impossibility of seeing God. Al-Nasafi dismisses both interpretations but does not detail his refutations here; he states that he invalidates them with multiple proofs (bi-akthar min dalīl) and that he refutes (yubṭilu) the second interpretation. The passage serves to show that the author engages with opposing views and exposes their weaknesses.

Key Terms
تفسير
آية
استحالة
إبطال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Moses did not actually ask to see Allah; he either asked for a sign or asked for his people.
Correction: Al-Nasafi refutes these claims, affirming that Moses asked to see Allah, and that the plain meaning of the verse is not to be distorted.

Checkpoint
How does al-Nasafi characterize his response to the Mu'tazila interpretations?
A) He accepts one of them.
B) He refutes them with multiple proofs.
C) He ignores them.
D) He only mentions them without comment.

Key Takeaway
Al-Nasafi rejects both Mu'tazila attempts to reinterpret Moses' request, affirming that the verse must be understood in its clear meaning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read a clear Quranic verse that challenges your intellect or preconceived notions, do you immediately try to reinterpret it, or do you submit to its plain meaning? Al-Nasafi's firm rejection of speculative interpretation calls you to examine your own relationship with revelation. Will you submit your reasoning to the Quran, or will you force the Quran to conform to your reasoning?
?
Moses' request to see Allah was a moment of intense spiritual longing. Do you truly desire to see Allah in the Hereafter, or are you content with intellectual acknowledgment? How does the Mu'tazila's reduction of Moses' request to a mere 'sign' reflect a lack of that deep yearning? Ask yourself: am I seeking Allah Himself, or just proofs and arguments?
?
The Mu'tazila claimed to speak on behalf of the people, arguing that Moses asked to show impossibility. Have you ever watered down divine truths to make them more palatable to others? Today, identify one truth from the Quran that you have hesitated to affirm fully out of fear of how others might react, and commit to upholding it without apology.

Refutation of False Views on Human Capacity
Nasafi refutes three erroneous positions on istita'a.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand three erroneous positions on istita'a and why Nasafi refutes them using the nature of accidents.

Core Idea
Istita'a is an accident, not a part of the agent—denying it or misplacing it undermines rational theology.

After establishing that istita'a exists in two forms (the means and the created accident), Nasafi turns to refute three opposing views. Each view misrepresents the relationship between capacity and the human agent. This lesson examines those views and the reasoning that invalidates them.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Start by reading the full Arabic passage and its literal translation. This will give you the raw material before analysis.
Pay attention to the names mentioned.
2
Identify the three false views
The passage lists three groups who err about istita'a. List each one and the specific mistake they make.
The views are: (1) capacity is the agent himself, (2) capacity is only soundness of causes, (3) capacity is part of the agent.
3
Understand the refutation
Nasafi uses a single argument to refute the third view: capacity is an accident (‛araḍ), and an accident cannot be part of a body. Reflect on why this also undermines the other views.
The first two views also fail because they deny or misplace the accidental nature of istita'a.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبعد أن يدلل على وجود الاستطاعة بقسميها يقول : إنه بهذا يبطل قول من يقول لا استطاعة للإنسان وراء ذاته بل مستطيع بنفسه. كما قال النظام وعلى الأسواري، والأصم ، وكذا قول من يقول : إنه لا استطاعة غير سلامة الأسباب والآلات. وكذا قول ضرار، وحفص الفرد بأن الاستطاعة بعض المستطيع، لأن الاستطاعة عرض، والقول بكون العرض بعض الجسم محال.
And after he provides evidence for the existence of istita‛a and its two divisions, he says: By this, the statement of those who say there is no capacity (istita‛a) for a human beyond his essence, rather he is capable (mustaṭī‛) by himself – as al-Naẓẓām, [‛Alā] al-Aswārī, and al-Aṣamm said – is invalidated; likewise the statement of those who say there is no capacity other than soundness of causes and instruments; likewise the statement of Ḍirār and Ḥafṣ al-Fard that capacity is a part of the capable one (al-mustaṭī‛), because capacity is an accident (‛araḍ), and the claim that an accident is part of a body is absurd.
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of three erroneous positions on istita‛a. After establishing the existence of istita‛a in two forms (means and created accident), al-Nasafī declares that this refutes: (1) The Mu‛tazilī view of al-Naẓẓām, al-Aswārī, and al-Aṣamm that a human is capable by his own essence, with no added capacity. (2) The view that only the soundness of causes exists, denying the created accident. (3) The view of Ḍirār and Ḥafṣ al-Fard that capacity is a part of the capable person. The reason given is that capacity is an accident (‛araḍ), and an accident cannot be a part of a body, because accidents are transient attributes, not components of substance. Thus, these views are invalid because they contradict the nature of istita‛a as an accident that is not part of the agent.

Key Terms
مستطيع (mustaṭī‛)
النظام (al-Naẓẓām)
ضرار (Ḍirār)
حفص الفرد (Ḥafṣ al-Fard)
عرض (‛araḍ)
بعض (ba‛ḍ)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think istita‛a is inherent in the human essence or a part of the body.
Correction: Nasafi shows istita‛a is an accident created by Allah, separate from the agent's essence and not a part of the body.

Checkpoint
Why does al-Nasafī reject the view that istita‛a is part of the capable person?
A) Because it would imply infinite regress
B) Because istita‛a is an accident, and an accident cannot be part of a body
C) Because istita‛a is eternal
D) Because the Qur'an denies it

Key Takeaway
Istita'a is an accident created by Allah, not a part of the human essence, and the three false views are refuted by this fact.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you accomplish something difficult, do you attribute the ability to yourself or to Allah? How does this lesson's emphasis on istita'a as a created accident challenge your sense of self-sufficiency? Write a specific resolution to thank Allah before your next task.
?
The view that capacity is part of you makes you feel inherently powerful. Which areas of your life do you operate as if you are self-capable, forgetting that Allah creates your ability moment by moment? What concrete action will you take tomorrow to remember this dependence?
?
If istita'a is a transient accident, not a permanent part of you, then every good deed you do is by Allah's creation of that capacity in you. How does this change the way you approach salah and dhikr? Commit to one change in your worship to reflect this awareness.

The Suitability of Istita'a for Opposites
Nasafi surveys major schools on istita'a's suitability for opposites.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the different theological positions on whether istita'a (human capacity) is valid for both opposite actions, including the nuanced Hanafi view of suitability on substitution (badal).

Core Idea
The suitability of istita'a for opposites is a key dividing line between Mu'tazila, Ash'ariyya, and Hanafi-Maturidi schools.

This passage from al-Nasafī unpacks a crucial question in kalām on human capacity (istita'a): can the same capacity be used for both obedience and disobedience? The author surveys the positions of major schools and then clarifies the nuanced Māturīdī stance, which incorporates elements from both sides.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without pausing. Then read the literal translation. Let the author's own presentation of different positions sink in.
2
Identify the four positions
The passage presents views from the Mu'tazila, Abū Ḥanīfa, Ashā'ira, and Māturīdiyya. Notice how each is described and where they agree or disagree.
Pay attention to the phrase 'على البدل' (on substitution) for Abū Ḥanīfa's view.
3
Understand 'suitability on substitution'
Abū Ḥanīfa's view is that istita'a is suitable for opposites 'on substitution' — the capacity that leads to faith is not suitable for disbelief at that moment, but if it were used for disbelief it would be suitable for that. Grasp this subtle distinction.
4
Locate al-Māturīdī's own leaning
Notice that although the Māturīdiyya as a school took a middle position, al-Nasafī reports that al-Māturīdī himself leaned towards the Ash'arite view that istita'a is not suitable for opposites.
This illustrates that school positions may have internal nuances.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعلى هذا فإن المعتزلة يقولون بأنها صالحة للضدين. ومن يقول بالاستطاعة مع الفعل منهم من قال مثل أبي حنيفة إنها تصلح للضدين على البدل، ومعنى ذلك أن الاستطاعة التي حصل بها الإيمان صلحت له ولا تصلح للكفر إذا اقترنت بالإيمان ولكنها لو اقترنت بالكفر بدلا من الإيمان لصلحت له بدلا من صلاحها للإيمان. أما الأشعرية فإنهم يقولون : إن الاستطاعة لا تصلح للضدين. ويمكن أن يفسر هذا بأن الماتريدية اتفقوا مع الأشاعرة في أن الاستطاعة مع الفعل واتفقوا مع المعتزلة في أنها تصلح للضدين. مع لكن النسفي يقول إنه يظهر أن أبا منصور الماتريدي يميل إلى أنها لا تصلح للضدين وعلى هذا يكون متفقا مع الأشاعرة.
And on this basis, the Mu‛tazila say that it is suitable for opposites. Among those who say that istita‛a is with the act, some – like Abū Ḥanīfa – said that it is suitable for opposites on substitution (‛alā al-badal). The meaning of that is: the istita‛a by which faith is obtained is suitable for it and is not suitable for disbelief if it accompanies faith, but if it accompanied disbelief instead of faith, it would be suitable for it instead of its suitability for faith. As for the Ashā‛ira, they say that istita‛a is not suitable for opposites. And this can be interpreted by saying that the Māturīdiyya agreed with the Ashā‛ira that istita‛a accompanies the act, and agreed with the Mu‛tazila that it is suitable for opposites. However, al-Nasafī says that it appears that Abū Manṣūr al-Māturīdī leans toward it not being suitable for opposites, and on this he is in agreement with the Ashā‛ira.
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage addresses the second question: whether istita‛a is ṣāliḥa lil-ḍiddayn (valid for both opposites). The Mu‛tazila affirm that the second type of istita‛a (power) is suitable for both the act and its opposite. Among those who believe istita‛a accompanies the act, some – like Abū Ḥanīfa – hold a nuanced view: it is suitable for opposites on a substitutive basis. That is, the specific capacity that produces faith is suitable for faith and not for disbelief when actually accompanied by faith; but if it had accompanied disbelief, it would have been suitable for that. The Ash‛arites hold that istita‛a is not suitable for opposites at all (they say it is only for the specific act that occurs). The author then notes that the Māturīdī school appears to agree with the Ash‛arites on accompaniment but with the Mu‛tazila on suitability for opposites – a middle position. However, al-Nasafī reports that al-Māturīdī himself tends to side with the Ash‛arites on this issue, i.e., that it is not suitable for opposites.

Key Terms
صلاحية للضدين (ṣalāḥiyya lil-ḍiddayn)
على البدل (‛alā al-badal)
أبو حنيفة (Abū Ḥanīfa)
الماتريدية (al-Māturīdiyya)
الأشعرية (al-Ash‛ariyya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All Māturīdīs agree with the Mu'tazila that istita'a is suitable for opposites.
Correction: Although the Māturīdiyya school takes a middle position on this issue, al-Nasafī reports that Abū Manṣūr al-Māturīdī himself leaned toward the Ash'arite view that istita'a is not suitable for opposites.

Checkpoint
According to al-Nasafī, what is Abū Manṣūr al-Māturīdī's view on whether istita‛a is suitable for opposites?
A) It is suitable
B) It is not suitable
C) It is suitable only on substitution
D) He had no opinion

Key Takeaway
The debate on istita'a's suitability for opposites reveals the delicate balance in Māturīdī theology between affirming human capacity and preserving divine sovereignty, with al-Māturīdī himself leaning toward the Ash'arite position.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you have the ability to commit a sin, do you recognize that very ability is a gift from Allah that could have been used for obedience? How does that awareness change your next step?
?
If your capacity to believe came from Allah, yet you chose disbelief, what does that tell you about your own responsibility? Will you thank Allah for the capacity He gave you to believe, or will you squander it?
?
Consider a time you attributed your success in worship to your own capability. How does this lesson's distinction between capacity and its direction reshape your gratitude and dependence on Allah?

Validity of Istita'a for Opposites: A Comparison of Schools
Nasafi enumerates and compares positions on istita'a's validity for opposites.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the positions of the Mu'tazila, Abu Hanifa, Ash'aris, and Maturidis on whether istita'a (power) is valid for both opposites, and the nuance of 'on substitution (badal).'

Core Idea
Power is valid for opposites, but only one at a time—the substitution principle clarifies this.

This lesson examines a key point of disagreement among kalam schools: can a single instance of power (istita'a) be valid for both performing an act and its opposite? Al-Nasafi compares the views of the Mu'tazila, Abu Hanifa, the Ash'aris, and the Maturidis, revealing a subtle distinction that safeguards both divine omnipotence and human responsibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the different schools mentioned and the logic of their arguments.
Pay attention to the term 'على البدل' (on substitution) as it is key to understanding Abu Hanifa's position.
2
Identify the positions
List what each school (Mu'tazila, Abu Hanifa, Ash'aris, Maturidis) says about istita'a's validity for opposites. Write them down in your own words.
The Maturidi position is described as a blend; note al-Nasafi's observation about al-Maturidi's personal view.
3
Understand the 'badal' nuance
Explain the concept of 'on substitution' (على البدل) in your own words. Why does Abu Hanifa add this condition? How does it differ from the absolute validity claimed by the Mu'tazila?
This nuance means the power is valid for one opposite at a time, not both simultaneously.
4
Compare al-Nasafi's conclusion
Reflect on al-Nasafi's statement that al-Maturidi tends toward the Ash'ari view. What implications does this have for the Maturidi school's overall position?
This shows that within a school, there can be internal differences of opinion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعلى هذا فإن المعتزلة يقولون بأنها صالحة للضدين. ومن يقول بالاستطاعة مع الفعل منهم من قال مثل أبي حنيفة إنها تصلح للضدين على البدل، ومعنى ذلك أن الاستطاعة التي حصل بها الإيمان صلحت له ولا تصلح للكفر إذا اقترنت بالإيمان ولكنها لو اقترنت بالكفر بدلا من الإيمان لصلحت له بدلا من صلاحها للإيمان. أما الأشعرية فإنهم يقولون : إن الاستطاعة لا تصلح للضدين. ويمكن أن يفسر هذا بأن الماتريدية اتفقوا مع الأشاعرة في أن الاستطاعة مع الفعل واتفقوا مع المعتزلة في أنها تصلح للضدين. لكن النسفي يقول إنه يظهر أن أبا منصور الماتريدي يميل إلى أنها لا تصلح للضدين وعلى هذا يكون متفقا مع الأشاعرة.
And on this basis, the Mu'tazila say that it (istita'a) is valid for both opposites. And among those who say that istita'a is with the act, some like Abu Hanifa said that it is valid for both opposites on substitution, meaning that the istita'a by which faith is obtained is valid for faith and is not valid for unbelief if it accompanies faith, but if it accompanied unbelief instead of faith, it would be valid for it instead of being valid for faith. As for the Ash'aris, they say that istita'a is not valid for both opposites. And this can be explained by saying that the Maturidis agreed with the Ash'aris that istita'a is with the act, and agreed with the Mu'tazila that it is valid for both opposites. But al-Nasafi says that it appears that Abu Mansur al-Maturidi tends toward the view that it is not valid for both opposites, and thus he would be in agreement with the Ash'aris.
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage classifies the positions of different schools on the issue of whether istita'a (in the sense of qudra) is valid for both opposites. The Mu'tazila affirm it is valid for opposites. Among those who hold simultaneity (with the act), some like Abu Hanifa add a nuance: it is valid for opposites on substitution (badal), meaning the power that brought about faith is valid for faith and not for unbelief in that moment, but if it had been connected to unbelief instead, it would have been valid for it. The Ash'aris deny validity for opposites. The Maturidis, al-Nasafi explains, have a mixed position: they agree with the Ash'aris on simultaneity, but with the Mu'tazila on validity for opposites. However, al-Nasafi notes that Abu Mansur al-Maturidi himself seems to lean toward the Ash'ari view of invalidity, thus agreeing with them.

Key Terms
الضدين
على البدل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that 'valid for both opposites' means the same power can produce two opposite acts simultaneously.
Correction: It actually means the power can produce either one in different circumstances, not both at once.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the position of Abu Hanifa regarding istita'a and its validity for opposites?
A) It is valid for opposites absolutely.
B) It is not valid for opposites.
C) It is valid for opposites on substitution (badal).
D) It is valid only for the act it actually produces.

Key Takeaway
The debate over istita'a's validity for opposites highlights the delicate balance between divine power and human agency, with schools differing on whether one power can be valid for two contrary acts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your ability to believe or disbelieve is not a matter of your independent power but a created act attributed to you, how does that reshape your gratitude for your faith? Do you truly thank Allah for every moment of belief as a gift, or do you take your faith for granted, assuming you could have chosen otherwise?
?
The concept of istita'a 'ala al-badal teaches that the same capacity that could have produced disbelief instead produces faith. When you reflect on your own life, are there moments where you recognize that your faith could have been replaced by disbelief had circumstances been different? How does that awareness change your reliance on Allah to preserve your faith?
?
Al-Maturidi's leaning toward the Ash'ari view reveals even theological giants struggled with the tension between human responsibility and divine sovereignty. In your own spiritual journey, where do you feel that tension most acutely? Are you more prone to attribute your successes to your own strength or to Allah's grace? What would it take for you to truly internalize that your very ability to act is a gift?

The False Premise Behind Jabriyya and Qadariyya
Nasafi presents the false premise behind Jabriyya and Qadariyya.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the common false premise that drives both Jabriyya and Qadariyya into extreme positions on human action.

Core Idea
A shared false premise leads to opposite extremes.

Nasafi begins by describing the extreme views of the Jabriyya and Qadariyya on human actions. He then reveals that despite their apparent opposition, both groups agree on a false premise: that one act cannot be under the power of two agents. This premise, he argues, is the root of their error.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three positions
Read the passage and note the positions of Jahm (Jabriyya), the Mu'tazila, and 'Abdullah al-Basri.
Each denies some role to either God or man.
2
Find the shared premise
Look for the sentence where Nasafi states what both groups agree on — the impossibility of two powers on one act.
This premise is presented as false.
3
Understand its implications
Reflect on how this premise leads to either pure compulsion or pure human autonomy.
Nasafi will refute it in the next part.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكلام في خلق أفعال العباد: وبعد ذلك يبدأ فى الحديث في مسألة خلق أفعال العباد فيقول: إن جَهْمًا جعلها الله، وليس للعبد فيها شيء، وإضافتها للعباد مجاز، وهذا هو القول بالجبر. أما المعتزلة : فجعلوها للعباد، وقطعوا تدبير الله عنها بالكلية. وذهب عبد الله البصري إلى أن الله ليس بخالق في الحقيقة بل يوصف بذلك مجازا، وإنما الخالق في الحقيقة هو العبد. ثم يقول : إن الذي دفع الجبرية وهم أصحاب جهم، والقدرية وهم المعتزلة إلى القول بأن العبد خالق لأفعاله الاختيارية اتفاقهم على مقدمة كاذبة وهي: أن دخول مقدور واحد تحت قدرة قادرين محال وذلك كما هو في الشاهد، ويبين فساد قولهم. ويقول : إن مذهب أهل السنة الجماعة : أن الأفعال للخلق، فإنهم بها صاروا عصاة أو مطيعين، وأنها مخلوقة لله، مكتسبة للعبد، وقد نفوا إحالة تعلق قدرتين بمقدور واحد، ونفوا الضرورة عن أفعالهم، ويستفاد من هذا ثبوت العدل ونفي الظلم، وإثبات الفضل لله ، ويستفاد أيضا أن الله موصوف بما وصف به نفسه، كما قال تعالى: خَالِقُ كُلِّ شَيْءٍ [الزمر: ٦٢]. والأشعري يقول : أن ما هو مقدور العبد لا يسمي فعلا، وإنما كسبا كما لا خلقا، وهذا هو وجه المخالفة بين الماتريدية والأشعرية، ويقول: إن قول الأشعري امتناع عما أطلقه الله بقوله تعالى : وافعلوا الخير [الحج: ٧٧]. يسمي وجميع المتكلمين يقولون : أفعال العباد ، بل إن الأشعري نفسه يقول (باب خلق أفعال العباد) وهذا تناقض عنده. ومع هذا فإن النسفي يقرر أن الاختلاف بين الماتريدية، والأشعري اختلاف في العبارة، وليس اختلافًا حقيقيًا.
The discourse on the creation of the acts of servants. After that, he begins to speak on the issue of the creation of the acts of servants and says: Indeed Jahm (ibn Safwan) made them (acts) belong to God, and the servant has nothing in them, and their attribution to the servants is metaphorical, and this is the saying of jabr (compulsion). As for the Mu'tazila, they made them belong to the servants, and they completely cut off God's disposal over them. And 'Abdullah al-Basri held that God is not a creator in reality, but is described as such metaphorically, and the real creator is the servant. Then he says: What drove the Jabriyya (the followers of Jahm) and the Qadariyya (the Mu'tazila) to say that the servant is the creator of his voluntary acts is their agreement on a false premise: that it is impossible for one act of power to fall under the power of two powerful agents, as is the case in the witnessed world. And he shows the falsity of their statement. And he says: The school of Ahl al-Sunna wa al-Jama'a is that acts belong to the creatures, for by them they become disobedient or obedient, and that they are created by God, acquired by the servant, and they denied the impossibility of the attachment of two powers to one act of power, and they denied compulsion over their acts. From this is derived the affirmation of justice and the negation of injustice, and the affirmation of divine grace (fadl), and it is also derived that God is described with what He described Himself with, as He said: 'Creator of all things' (Q. 39:62). And al-Ash'ari says that what is within the power of the servant is not called an act, but rather an acquisition (kasb) and not a creation, and this is the point of difference between the Maturidis and the Ash'aris. And he says: Indeed al-Ash'ari's saying is a refusal of what God has expressed in His saying: 'And do good' (Q. 22:77). He calls all theologians saying 'the acts of servants'; rather, al-Ash'ari himself says 'the chapter on the creation of the acts of servants', and this is a contradiction on his part. Despite this, al-Nasafi affirms that the difference between the Maturidis and al-Ash'ari is a difference in expression, not a real difference.
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage presents the Maturidi doctrine on the creation of human actions. Nasafi first describes the extreme positions: Jabriyya (Jahm) attribute all acts to God, denying human agency; Mu'tazila attribute acts to the servant, cutting off God's control; and 'Abdullah al-Basri (a Mu'tazili) denies God's real creation. Nasafi then explains that both Jabriyya and Qadariyya share a false premise: that one act cannot fall under the power of two agents. He refutes this. The Ahl al-Sunna position is that acts are created by God but acquired (maksaba) by the servant; this preserves justice and negates compulsion, and God is described as He described Himself (e.g., 'Creator of all things'). Nasafi then addresses the Ash'ari view: al-Ash'ari calls the servant's act 'kasb' not 'act', but Nasafi notes that al-Ash'ari himself uses 'creation of acts of servants' in his chapter headings, implying inconsistency. However, Nasafi concludes that the difference is merely verbal, not substantive.

Key Terms
الجبر
القدر
المعتزلة
خلق أفعال العباد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, what common false premise do both Jabriyya and Qadariyya agree on?
A) God is the sole creator of all actions.
B) It is impossible for one act to be under the power of two agents.
C) The servant is completely free in his actions.
D) Acts are not attributable to God.

Key Takeaway
A false premise about impossibility of shared agency leads to extreme doctrines on human action.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you find yourself either blaming God entirely ('it was written') or feeling completely alone in your guilt? How does this passage expose both extremes as stemming from the same false assumption? What would it look like for you to hold both God's sovereignty and your responsibility together without dismissing either?
?
This lesson says that a false premise about shared agency leads groups to opposite errors. Think of an area in your life where you tend to see things as either/or (e.g., God's control vs. your effort). Are you willing to examine whether that either/or itself is a false premise? What would change if you embraced a both/and?
?
Nasafi identifies a mistaken belief that leads astray. What mistaken belief about God's relationship to your daily choices might you be holding onto without realizing it? Will you ask God tonight to show you any false premise in your heart about His role and your role in your life?

The Maturidi Doctrine of Kasb and the Ash'ari Difference
Nasafi presents the Ahl al-Sunna doctrine of kasb and addresses the Ash'ari-Maturidi difference.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Maturidi doctrine of acquisition (kasb) and how Nasafi reconciles the apparent difference with the Ash'ari view.

Core Idea
Acts are created by God and acquired by man, preserving justice and divine attributes.

After refuting the false premise, Nasafi presents the correct view of Ahl al-Sunna. Acts are created by God yet belong to the servant through acquisition (kasb). This view upholds divine justice and negates compulsion. Nasafi then addresses al-Ash'ari's position and argues that the difference between Maturidis and Ash'aris is merely verbal.

Guided Path
1
Read the Ahl al-Sunna position
Identify that acts are created by God and acquired by the servant, and the implications (justice, negation of compulsion, divine attributes).
Note the Quranic proof: 'Creator of all things'.
2
Examine the Ash'ari view
Read how al-Ash'ari calls the servant's action 'kasb' not 'act', and Nasafi's critique using the Quran and al-Ash'ari's own terminology.
Nasafi points out an apparent contradiction.
3
Understand the reconciliation
Notice the final sentence where Nasafi states the difference is only in expression, not in meaning.
This resolves the sectarian dispute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكلام في خلق أفعال العباد: وبعد ذلك يبدأ فى الحديث في مسألة خلق أفعال العباد فيقول: إن جَهْمًا جعلها الله، وليس للعبد فيها شيء، وإضافتها للعباد مجاز، وهذا هو القول بالجبر. أما المعتزلة : فجعلوها للعباد، وقطعوا تدبير الله عنها بالكلية. وذهب عبد الله البصري إلى أن الله ليس بخالق في الحقيقة بل يوصف بذلك مجازا، وإنما الخالق في الحقيقة هو العبد. ثم يقول : إن الذي دفع الجبرية وهم أصحاب جهم، والقدرية وهم المعتزلة إلى القول بأن العبد خالق لأفعاله الاختيارية اتفاقهم على مقدمة كاذبة وهي: أن دخول مقدور واحد تحت قدرة قادرين محال وذلك كما هو في الشاهد، ويبين فساد قولهم. ويقول : إن مذهب أهل السنة الجماعة : أن الأفعال للخلق، فإنهم بها صاروا عصاة أو مطيعين، وأنها مخلوقة لله، مكتسبة للعبد، وقد نفوا إحالة تعلق قدرتين بمقدور واحد، ونفوا الضرورة عن أفعالهم، ويستفاد من هذا ثبوت العدل ونفي الظلم، وإثبات الفضل لله ، ويستفاد أيضا أن الله موصوف بما وصف به نفسه، كما قال تعالى: خَالِقُ كُلِّ شَيْءٍ [الزمر: ٦٢]. والأشعري يقول : أن ما هو مقدور العبد لا يسمي فعلا، وإنما كسبا كما لا خلقا، وهذا هو وجه المخالفة بين الماتريدية والأشعرية، ويقول: إن قول الأشعري امتناع عما أطلقه الله بقوله تعالى : وافعلوا الخير [الحج: ٧٧]. يسمي وجميع المتكلمين يقولون : أفعال العباد ، بل إن الأشعري نفسه يقول (باب خلق أفعال العباد) وهذا تناقض عنده. ومع هذا فإن النسفي يقرر أن الاختلاف بين الماتريدية، والأشعري اختلاف في العبارة، وليس اختلافًا حقيقيًا.
The discourse on the creation of the acts of servants. After that, he begins to speak on the issue of the creation of the acts of servants and says: Indeed Jahm (ibn Safwan) made them (acts) belong to God, and the servant has nothing in them, and their attribution to the servants is metaphorical, and this is the saying of jabr (compulsion). As for the Mu'tazila, they made them belong to the servants, and they completely cut off God's disposal over them. And 'Abdullah al-Basri held that God is not a creator in reality, but is described as such metaphorically, and the real creator is the servant. Then he says: What drove the Jabriyya (the followers of Jahm) and the Qadariyya (the Mu'tazila) to say that the servant is the creator of his voluntary acts is their agreement on a false premise: that it is impossible for one act of power to fall under the power of two powerful agents, as is the case in the witnessed world. And he shows the falsity of their statement. And he says: The school of Ahl al-Sunna wa al-Jama'a is that acts belong to the creatures, for by them they become disobedient or obedient, and that they are created by God, acquired by the servant, and they denied the impossibility of the attachment of two powers to one act of power, and they denied compulsion over their acts. From this is derived the affirmation of justice and the negation of injustice, and the affirmation of divine grace (fadl), and it is also derived that God is described with what He described Himself with, as He said: 'Creator of all things' (Q. 39:62). And al-Ash'ari says that what is within the power of the servant is not called an act, but rather an acquisition (kasb) and not a creation, and this is the point of difference between the Maturidis and the Ash'aris. And he says: Indeed al-Ash'ari's saying is a refusal of what God has expressed in His saying: 'And do good' (Q. 22:77). He calls all theologians saying 'the acts of servants'; rather, al-Ash'ari himself says 'the chapter on the creation of the acts of servants', and this is a contradiction on his part. Despite this, al-Nasafi affirms that the difference between the Maturidis and al-Ash'ari is a difference in expression, not a real difference.
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage presents the Maturidi doctrine on the creation of human actions. Nasafi first describes the extreme positions: Jabriyya (Jahm) attribute all acts to God, denying human agency; Mu'tazila attribute acts to the servant, cutting off God's control; and 'Abdullah al-Basri (a Mu'tazili) denies God's real creation. Nasafi then explains that both Jabriyya and Qadariyya share a false premise: that one act cannot fall under the power of two agents. He refutes this. The Ahl al-Sunna position is that acts are created by God but acquired (maksaba) by the servant; this preserves justice and negates compulsion, and God is described as He described Himself (e.g., 'Creator of all things'). Nasafi then addresses the Ash'ari view: al-Ash'ari calls the servant's act 'kasb' not 'act', but Nasafi notes that al-Ash'ari himself uses 'creation of acts of servants' in his chapter headings, implying inconsistency. However, Nasafi concludes that the difference is merely verbal, not substantive.

Key Terms
الكسب
الماتريدية
الأشعرية
خالق كل شيء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Maturidi and Ash'ari views on human action are fundamentally different and incompatible.
Correction: Nasafi states that the difference is in expression, not in meaning.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, what does al-Ash'ari call the servant's action?
A) Act (fi'l)
B) Creation (khalq)
C) Acquisition (kasb)
D) Compulsion (jabr)

Key Takeaway
The Maturidi-Ash'ari dispute over human action is a matter of terminology, not substance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perform a good deed, do you truly believe that God created that act within you, or do you secretly feel it is your own achievement? How would honestly acknowledging God's creation of your actions change your gratitude and humility?
?
The lesson shows that a major theological dispute boils down to mere wording. Are there any theological debates you hold onto that might actually be about terminology rather than truth? What would it cost you to let go of that attachment?
?
If every act of yours—even your disobedience—is created by God, how do you wrestle with the feeling of moral responsibility? Do you ever use God's sovereignty as an excuse to avoid accountability? Confront the tension in your own heart right now.

The Three Positions on Human Actions: Jabriyya, Mu'tazila, and Abdallah al-Basri
Al-Nasafi enumerates three views on the creation of human acts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three main rival positions on the creation of human actions: Jabriyya, Mu'tazila, and that of Abdallah al-Basri.

Core Idea
Human actions are not fully from Allah nor fully from the servant – the truth lies in between.

This passage presents three starkly different views on who creates human actions. Al-Nasafi reports them before laying out the Maturidi middle path. Understanding these extremes is essential to grasping the Sunni position.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the author's rhythm come through before interpreting.
Notice that al-Nasafi presents these views as preliminary to his own position.
2
Identify the three positions
List the three views: Jahm (Jabriyya), the Mu'tazila, and Abdallah al-Basri. Notice what each says about the roles of Allah and the servant.
These are three extreme positions that the Maturidi view will reconcile.
3
Compare the views on divine and human agency
For each view, ask: Who is the real creator of actions? Allah, the servant, or metaphorically one or the other?
4
Reflect on why these are insufficient
Consider why the Maturidi school would reject each extreme. This sets the stage for the balanced position.
The Maturidi view affirms both divine creation and human acquisition (kasb).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكلام في خلق أفعال العباد: وبعد ذلك يبدأ فى الحديث في مسألة خلق أفعال العباد فيقول: إن جَهْمًا جعلها الله، وليس للعبد فيها شيء، وإضافتها للعباد مجاز، وهذا هو القول بالجبر. أما المعتزلة : فجعلوها للعباد، وقطعوا تدبير الله عنها بالكلية. وذهب عبد الله البصري إلى أن الله ليس بخالق في الحقيقة بل يوصف بذلك مجازا، وإنما الخالق في الحقيقة هو العبد.
Discourse on the creation of the acts of servants: And after that he begins to speak about the issue of the creation of the acts of servants, saying: Verily, Jahm made them belong to Allah, and the servant has no part in them, and their attribution to servants is metaphorical, and this is the doctrine of jabr. As for the Mu'tazila: they made them belong to the servants, and completely cut off Allah's governance over them. And 'Abdallah al-Basri held that Allah is not a creator in reality, but is described as such metaphorically; rather, the genuine creator is the servant.
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage presents three rival views on the creation of human actions. The author, al-Nasafi, begins by stating the issue: the creation of human acts. He then reports the positions. First, Jahm ibn Safwan made all actions belong to Allah alone, with no role for the servant—this is jabr (compulsion). Second, the Mu'tazila made actions belong to the servant entirely, cutting off Allah's arrangement (tadbir) from them. Third, 'Abdallah al-Basri held that Allah is not actually a creator, but only metaphorically; the real creator is the servant. These views set up the need for a middle position.

Key Terms

معتزلة
مجاز
خلق أفعال العباد

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that the Mu'tazila and Abdallah al-Basri hold the same view.
Correction: The Mu'tazila attribute actions to the servant while still allowing metaphorical attribution to Allah? No, they cut off Allah's governance. Abdallah al-Basri explicitly denies that Allah is a creator in reality, making the servant the genuine creator.

Checkpoint

A) The Mu'tazila
B) Jahm and the Jabriyya
C) Abdallah al-Basri
D) The Maturidiyya

Key Takeaway
There are three rival views on human actions: total compulsion, total autonomy, and servant as real creator – but the truth is a balanced middle.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
If you truly believed that every action you take is entirely your creation (as Abdallah al-Basri claimed), would that make you more arrogant or more fearful? How does the middle path of Islam change your sense of accountability before Allah?
?
Think of a specific choice you made today. In that moment, did you act as if Allah's governance was completely cut off, or as if you had no role at all? How can you train your heart to hold both divine power and human responsibility without falling into one of these extremes?

The Verbal Nature of the Maturidi-Ash'ari Disagreement on Kasb
al-Nasafi contrasts the Ash'ari view on kasb and concludes the difference is merely verbal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the apparent conflict between Maturidis and Ash'aris over human action is merely terminological, not doctrinal.

Core Idea
The Maturidi-Ash'ari dispute on kasb is a disagreement in words, not in meaning.

Al-Nasafi examines the claim that al-Ash'ari restricts the term 'fi'l' to God and uses 'kasb' for human action, and finds that even al-Ash'ari himself speaks of 'af'al al-'ibad'. This leads al-Nasafi to conclude that the difference is only verbal. In this lesson, we will trace his argument and see how he reconciles the two positions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic text carefully, then follow the literal translation. Notice the key terms: fi'l, kasb, khalq.
Pay attention to the critical statement about the Quranic verse.
2
Identify the reported position of al-Ash'ari
Find where al-Nasafi presents al-Ash'ari's view: what does he say about the servant's power? How does he distinguish between act and kasb?
3
Trace the critique and resolution
Observe how al-Nasafi points out a contradiction: al-Ash'ari uses 'act' in his own chapter title. Then note his final conclusion that the disagreement is merely verbal.
This is the key takeaway.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والأشعري يقول: أن ما هو مقدور العبد لا يسمى فعلا، وإنما كسبا لا خلقا، وهذا هو وجه المخالفة بين الماتريدية والأشعرية، ويقول: إن قول الأشعري امتناع عما أطلقه الله بقوله تعالى: (وافعلوا الخير) [الحج: 77]. يسمى وجميع المتكلمين يقولون: أفعال العباد، بل إن الأشعري نفسه يقول (باب خلق أفعال العباد) وهذا تناقض عنده. ومع هذا فإن النسفي يقرر أن الاختلاف بين الماتريدية، والأشعري اختلاف في العبارة، وليس اختلافًا حقيقيا.
And al-Ash'ari says: That which is under the power of the servant is not called an act (fi'l), but rather an acquisition (kasb), not a creation (khalq). And this is the aspect of disagreement between the Maturidiyya and the Ash'ariyya. And he says: Verily, the statement of al-Ash'ari is an abstention from what Allah has expressed in His saying, Exalted: 'And do good' (wa if'al al-khayr) [al-Hajj: 77]. It is called (yusamma) and all the mutakallimun say: 'the acts of the servants.' Rather, al-Ash'ari himself says: 'Chapter on the creation of the acts of servants' (bab khalq af'al al-'ibad). And this is a contradiction according to him. Yet despite this, al-Nasafi affirms that the disagreement between the Maturidiyya and al-Ash'ari is a disagreement in expression, not a real disagreement.
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage addresses the apparent conflict between the Maturidi and Ash'ari positions. Al-Nasafi reports that al-Ash'ari refuses to call the servant's act a 'fi'l' (act) and instead uses 'kasb' (acquisition) as distinct from 'khalq' (creation). This is the point of difference. Al-Nasafi then criticizes al-Ash'ari's own usage: al-Ash'ari abstains from calling it an 'act' despite the Qur'anic command 'Wa if'al al-khayr' (And do good). Moreover, all mutakallimun, including al-Ash'ari himself, speak of 'af'al al-'ibad' (acts of servants). This is a contradiction. However, al-Nasafi concludes that the disagreement is merely in terminology, not in substance.

أشعرية

Correction: Al-Nasafi clarifies that the dispute is only about terminology, not about the actual doctrine of divine creation and human acquisition.

D) They deny the Qur'anic command to do good

Key Takeaway

This lesson calls you to examine your own life for such inconsistencies. What is one specific example you can identify today, and what step will you take to align them?
?

The Ahl al-Hadith Argument: God's Creating Evil is Not Evil
Ahl al-Hadith define evil by prohibition and harm.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ahl al-Hadith define evil as that which is prohibited or brings harm, and why this excludes God's acts from being evil.

Core Idea
God is above prohibition and harm, so His creating evil cannot be evil.

Imam al-Nasafi presents the Ahl al-Hadith perspective to refute the Mu'tazila claim. They define evil in two ways: by divine command and by consequence. Both definitions show that God's acts are not evil.

Guided Path
1
Read the Ahl al-Hadith argument
Read the passage carefully and note the two definitions of evil presented.
The argument uses two independent definitions.
2
Identify the definitions
Find the first definition: evil as what is prohibited by command. Then the second: evil as what brings pure harm to the doer.
3
Apply to God
Notice why neither definition applies to God: He has no prohibition upon Him and is not subject to harm.
The conclusion is that creating disbelief is not evil.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم بعد ذلك يبين فساد قول المعتزلة : أن إيجاد القبيح قبيح، فيقول: إن أهل الحديث يقولون : إن القبيح ما ورد النهي عنه، والله سبحانه فيما يفعل ليس عليه نهي، ولم يتجاوز حدا حُدَّ له، ولا رسما رسم له، فلم يكن فيما يفعل سفها، ولا فعله قبيحا، وكما أن القبيح ما يعود به على فاعله الضرر المحض، والله تعالى لا يعود عليه بأي فعل ضرر، فلا يكون خلقه الكفر قبيحا. ويقول مشايخ الماتريدية : كل ما له عاقبة حميدة فهو حكمة، وما ليس كذلك فهو سفه، ولا شيء يعود إلى الله، فلا سَفَه، وكل أفعاله حكمة.
Then after that he explains the falsity of the Mu'tazila statement: that creating evil is evil. He says: The People of Hadith say: Evil is what the prohibition has been revealed against, and Allah the Exalted, in what He does, there is no prohibition upon Him, nor does He exceed a limit prescribed for Him, nor a definition defined for Him. So there is no foolishness in what He does, nor is His act evil. And just as evil is what brings pure harm back upon its doer, and Allah the Exalted is not affected by any act with harm, so His creation of disbelief is not evil. And the shaykhs of the Maturidis say: Everything that has a praiseworthy consequence is wisdom, and what is not like that is foolishness, and nothing returns to Allah [of benefit or harm], so there is no foolishness, and all His acts are wisdom.
Source pages p. 99

Commentary
This passage refutes the Mu'tazila claim that God's act of creating evil (such as disbelief) is itself evil. The author presents two perspectives. First, the view of Ahl al-Hadith: they define evil as that which is prohibited by divine command. Since God is not subject to any prohibition, His acts cannot be evil. Additionally, they define evil as that which brings pure harm to the agent. Since God is not harmed by any act, His creating disbelief does not count as evil. Second, the view of the Maturidi shaykhs: they define evil (or foolishness) as that which has no praiseworthy consequence. Since all of God's acts lead to a praiseworthy outcome (they are all wisdom), none of His acts are foolish or evil. Thus from both definitions, God's creating evil is not evil.

Key Terms
قبيح
النهي
الضرر المحض
أهل الحديث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Ahl al-Hadith argument, why is God's creation of disbelief not evil?
A) Because disbelief is not really evil.
B) Because it is not prohibited by any command upon God.
C) Because it has a good consequence.
D) Because it is a limit prescribed.

Key Takeaway
God's acts are not evil because He is neither prohibited nor harmed by them.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face trials or see evil in the world, do you secretly resent Allah's decree? How does knowing that Allah is beyond harm and prohibition change your heart toward accepting His will? Will you surrender that resentment today?
?
If you define evil by what harms you personally, you are using a human standard. Can you look at your own struggles and see them not as 'evil' but as Allah's action upon you, free from any defect? What would it take for you to trust that His act toward you is never evil?
?
You say you believe Allah is not subject to prohibition, but do you live as if He is? When you question His commands or find them burdensome, you imply a limit on Him. Repent of that arrogance and commit to obeying without objection today.
