The Maturidi Argument: All God's Acts are Wisdom
Maturidi shaykhs define wisdom and foolishness by consequence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Maturidi definition of foolishness as absence of praiseworthy consequence, and why it cannot apply to God.

Core Idea
God's acts are wise because they always have praiseworthy outcomes.

Imam al-Nasafi then presents the Maturidi shaykhs' argument. They define foolishness as that which has no praiseworthy consequence. Since God's acts are all ultimately wise, none are foolish or evil.

Guided Path
1
Read the Maturidi argument
Read the short passage and note the key terms: wisdom (hikma), foolishness (safah), and praiseworthy consequence (aqiba hamida).
2
Understand the definitions
Wisdom is whatever has a praiseworthy consequence; foolishness is what lacks that. Note that 'nothing returns to Allah' means He is not affected by outcomes.
3
Draw the conclusion
Since nothing returns to Allah, no act of His can be foolish, and all His acts are wisdom.
This includes the creation of disbelief, which has a wise purpose.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم بعد ذلك يبين فساد قول المعتزلة : أن إيجاد القبيح قبيح، فيقول: إن أهل الحديث يقولون : إن القبيح ما ورد النهي عنه، والله سبحانه فيما يفعل ليس عليه نهي، ولم يتجاوز حدا حُدَّ له، ولا رسما رسم له، فلم يكن فيما يفعل سفها، ولا فعله قبيحا، وكما أن القبيح ما يعود به على فاعله الضرر المحض، والله تعالى لا يعود عليه بأي فعل ضرر، فلا يكون خلقه الكفر قبيحا. ويقول مشايخ الماتريدية : كل ما له عاقبة حميدة فهو حكمة، وما ليس كذلك فهو سفه، ولا شيء يعود إلى الله، فلا سَفَه، وكل أفعاله حكمة.
Then after that he explains the falsity of the Mu'tazila statement: that creating evil is evil. He says: The People of Hadith say: Evil is what the prohibition has been revealed against, and Allah the Exalted, in what He does, there is no prohibition upon Him, nor does He exceed a limit prescribed for Him, nor a definition defined for Him. So there is no foolishness in what He does, nor is His act evil. And just as evil is what brings pure harm back upon its doer, and Allah the Exalted is not affected by any act with harm, so His creation of disbelief is not evil. And the shaykhs of the Maturidis say: Everything that has a praiseworthy consequence is wisdom, and what is not like that is foolishness, and nothing returns to Allah [of benefit or harm], so there is no foolishness, and all His acts are wisdom.
Source pages p. 99

Commentary
This passage refutes the Mu'tazila claim that God's act of creating evil (such as disbelief) is itself evil. The author presents two perspectives. First, the view of Ahl al-Hadith: they define evil as that which is prohibited by divine command. Since God is not subject to any prohibition, His acts cannot be evil. Additionally, they define evil as that which brings pure harm to the agent. Since God is not harmed by any act, His creating disbelief does not count as evil. Second, the view of the Maturidi shaykhs: they define evil (or foolishness) as that which has no praiseworthy consequence. Since all of God's acts lead to a praiseworthy outcome (they are all wisdom), none of His acts are foolish or evil. Thus from both definitions, God's creating evil is not evil.

Key Terms
حكمة
سفه
عاقبة حميدة
مشايخ الماتريدية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Maturidi shaykhs in this passage, when is an act considered foolish (safah)?
A) When it is prohibited by divine command.
B) When it brings harm to the agent.
C) When it has no praiseworthy consequence.
D) When it is created by God.

Key Takeaway
All of God's acts are wise because they lead to praiseworthy outcomes, even if we do not see it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a painful trial, do you truly believe that this act of God has a praiseworthy outcome, even if you cannot see it? What would change in your heart if you did?
?
The Maturidis argue that nothing returns to Allah from your actions—so your obedience or disobedience does not benefit or harm Him. How does that reshape your motivation to worship, and are you content to worship solely because He is worthy?
?
If every act of God is wise by definition, what does that mean for the moments you have doubted His wisdom in your life? Will you repent of that doubt and commit to trusting His planning?

All Actions Are Created by God: Refuting Shirk and the Mu'tazila
Al-Nasafi asserts God creates all actions, defines two types of shirk, and critiques the Mu'tazila.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two types of shirk and why the Mu'tazila are considered to affirm partners with Allah according to al-Nasafi.

Core Idea
Creatures cannot be partners with God; shirk is either in creation or in worship.

This passage from al-Nasafi's Tabsirat al-Adilla connects the discussion of good and evil to the broader principle that all actions are created by God. Since God creates everything, creatures cannot be partners with their Lord. The author then distinguishes two types of shirk and concludes that the Mu'tazila fall into shirk by denying God's sole creation of actions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the two types of shirk
Read the Arabic passage and its translation, focusing on the two types of shirk described. Notice how each type is defined and exemplified.
The Magians are mentioned as an example of shirk in creation; idol-worshippers for shirk in worship.
2
Understand the logical connection: God creates all actions, so no partners
The author argues that because all actions of creatures are created by Allah, servants cannot be partners with their Lord. Reflect on why this refutes any notion of partnership.
This is a key Maturidi principle: God is the sole creator of all actions, including human acts.
3
Analyze the critique of the Mu'tazila
The passage ends by stating that the Mu'tazila, by affirming that servants create their own actions, have affirmed partners with Allah. Consider how this follows from the earlier definition of shirk in creation.
The Mu'tazila do not see themselves as polytheists; the author is pointing out the logical implication of their doctrine.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويتحدث بإسهاب عن الحسن و القبح مثبتا أن كل ما صدر عن الله سبحانه فهو حسن وهو حكمة، وينطلق بعد ذلك ليقول : إن أفعال الخلق كلها مخلوقة الله، وهذا يؤدي إلى أن العباد ليسوا شركاء لربهم، فإن الشرك نوعان: شرك يثبت فيه المشرك الله شريكا في خلق العالم مثل المجوس وشرك يثبت فيه المشرك شريكا الله تعالى في استحقاق العبادة مثل عبدة الأصنام. وإذا كان الله هو خالق كل شيء، وليس في احتياج إلى أحد، كان وحده المحمود، وكان عين ما هو مقدور الله مقدور العبد بإقداره إياه، وما هو مقدور العبد، مقدور الله بخلقه، وهذا يعني في قول النسفي أن المعتزلة هم الذين أثبتوا شركاء الله.
And he speaks at length about good and evil, affirming that everything that issues from Allah the Exalted is good and wisdom. Then he proceeds to say: Indeed, all the actions of creatures are created by Allah, and this leads to the conclusion that servants are not partners with their Lord. For shirk is of two types: shirk in which the mushrik affirms a partner with Allah in creating the world, like the Magians; and shirk in which the mushrik affirms a partner with Allah the Exalted in being deserving of worship, like idol-worshippers. And since Allah is the Creator of everything, and has no need of anyone, He alone is praised, and that which is within the power of Allah is within the power of the servant only by Allah's enabling him, and that which is within the power of the servant is within the power of Allah by His creation. And this means, in al-Nasafi's view, that the Mu'tazila are those who have affirmed partners with Allah.
Source pages p. 99

Commentary
This passage connects the discussion of good and evil to the broader principle that all actions of creatures are created by God. Since God creates everything, creatures cannot be partners with their Lord. The author then distinguishes two types of shirk: (1) shirk in creation, as held by the Magians who posit a second creator; (2) shirk in worship, as practiced by idol-worshippers. He then argues that because God is the sole creator and independent, He alone deserves praise. Moreover, any power a servant has is granted by God's enabling (iqdār), and any action of the servant is ultimately created by God. The conclusion is that the Mu'tazila, by asserting that servants create their own actions, effectively attribute partners to God in creation — thus they fall into shirk.

Key Terms
حسن
قبيح
خلق
شرك
مجوس

اكتساب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila are not considered polytheists in Islamic theology; they are a rationalist sect.
Correction: While the Mu'tazila do not consider themselves polytheists, al-Nasafi argues that their doctrine of human creation of actions logically implies a partner in creation, which is a form of shirk.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is the second type of shirk mentioned?
A) Shirk in creation like the Magians.
B) Shirk in worship like idol-worshippers.
C) Shirk in actions like the Mu'tazila.
D) Shirk in names and attributes.

Key Takeaway
Because God creates all actions, any claim that servants create their own actions implies a partner in creation, which is a form of shirk.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you succeed in something, do you ever feel that it was entirely your own doing, forgetting that Allah created both your action and the circumstances? How does this passage call you to repentance for that hidden shirk?
?
Examine your daily speech: do you ever say 'I did this' as if you are the ultimate agent, independent of Allah's creation? Will you commit today to saying 'by Allah's will' or 'Allah enabled me' whenever you acknowledge your actions?
?
The Mu'tazila's error was in claiming humans create their own actions. Have you ever, even in your heart, felt that your choices are completely free from Allah's decree? What would it mean for you to truly believe that every breath and every move is created by Allah alone?

Why Mu'tazila Are Worse Than Magians
Al-Nasafi gives two reasons why Mu'tazila are worse than Zoroastrians.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Nasafi's two reasons for judging the Mu'tazila more blameworthy than the Magians (Zoroastrians) in matters of divine attribution.

Core Idea
Mu'tazila spread divine partnership wider than Magians.

In this short but intense passage, al-Nasafi delivers a sharp polemical verdict: the Mu'tazila are in a worse state than the Magians. To see why, we need to unpack his two arguments — one about the number of partners and the other about the intention behind dualism. His aim is to expose what he sees as a deeper theological corruption in Mu'tazilite thought.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once through, then read the literal translation. Let the strength of the comparison sink in.
Notice the phrase 'أشر من المجوس' — a very strong claim.
2
Identify the two reasons
The passage lists two 'وجوه' (aspects). Find the first reason (number of partners) and the second (intention behind dualism).
3
Understand the contrast
For each reason, compare what the Magians do versus what the Mu'tazila do. Why does al-Nasafi see the Mu'tazila as worse?
Think about the scope of partnership: one limited partner vs. many.
4
Reflect on the doctrine
Consider how the Mu'tazilite view of human agency (creating our own actions) leads to the charge of making every living thing a partner.
This connects to the broader debate about free will and divine sovereignty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويشدد النسفي في نعت المعتزلة قائلا: إنهم أشر من المجوس لوجهين: أن المجوس أثبتوا الله شريكا واحدا ، أما المعتزلة فقد جعلوا كل ما فيه حياة شريكا الله، ومن جهة أخرى أن المجوس أرادوا بإثبات الشريك تنزيه الله عما هو سفه، عندهم وأضافوا إلى الله الخير، وإلى أهر من الشر ، أما المعتزلة فقد أضافوا كل ما هو حسن أو قبيح إلى غير الله.
And al-Nasafi intensifies in describing the Mu'tazila, saying: They are worse than the Magians for two aspects: that the Magians affirmed one partner with Allah, while the Mu'tazila have made everything that has life a partner with Allah; and from another aspect, that the Magians, by affirming the partner, intended to exonerate Allah from what is foolishness, according to them, and they attributed good to Allah and evil to Ahriman, while the Mu'tazila have attributed everything that is good or evil to other than Allah.
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This passage presents a polemical comparison between the Mu'tazila and the Magians (Zoroastrians). The author gives two reasons why the Mu'tazila are worse. First, the Magians only affirm one partner for God (i.e., Ahriman, the principle of evil), whereas the Mu'tazila make every living being a partner with God by attributing to them the creation of their own actions. Second, the Magians, by positing a separate source of evil, intended to exonerate God from foolishness or evil — they attribute good to God and evil to Ahriman. The Mu'tazila, on the other hand, attribute both good and evil to other than God (i.e., to human agents). Thus, in the author's view, the Mu'tazila are more blameworthy.

Key Terms
أشر
المجوس
شريك
تنزيه
سفه
أهر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Al-Nasafi is accusing the Mu'tazila of literal polytheism (shirk) for believing human agents create their own actions.
Correction: Al-Nasafi's point is theological: by making creatures independent agents, Mu'tazila logically associate partners with God in bringing about events. It's an argument about divine sovereignty, not literal worship of other gods.

Checkpoint
According to al-Nasafi, what did the Magians intend by affirming a partner?
A) To share worship with another god.
B) To exonerate Allah from what they considered foolishness.
C) To deny all actions of God.
D) To divide creation between two equal gods.

Key Takeaway
Al-Nasafi argues that the Mu'tazila are worse than Magians because their doctrine makes every living being a partner with God and attributes both good and evil to creatures, whereas the Magians limit partnership and exonerate God from evil.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience a setback or a good outcome, do you ever, like the Mu'tazila, attribute it in your heart to your own efforts or to another person? How does al-Nasafi's critique expose the subtle shirk in that inclination? What would it take to truly live out the conviction that every event is from Allah alone?
?
The Magians at least intended to protect God from evil. Have you ever tried to 'protect' God by blaming someone else for a wrong, or by distancing Him from your mistakes? Why is this attempt at tanzih actually more dangerous than simply acknowledging both good and evil come from Him?
?
Consider the second reason: Mu'tazila attribute everything good and evil to creatures. In your daily life, do you take credit for your good deeds or feel resentment toward those who wrong you? How does that resemble the Mu'tazili error? What step can you take today to redirect all praise and blame to Allah alone?

Why the Mu'tazila Are Worse Than the Magians
Nasafi compares Mu'tazila and Magians, giving two reasons.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Nasafi's theological critique that the Mu'tazila are worse than the Magians in associating partners with God, and the two reasons he gives.

Core Idea
Mu'tazila make every living being a partner to God.

In this passage, Nasafi draws a sharp comparison between the Mu'tazila and the Magians (Zoroastrians). He argues that the Mu'tazila, by claiming that humans create their own actions, have actually set up countless partners with God. He then gives two reasons why the Mu'tazila are even worse than the Magians, who at least only affirmed one partner.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once to grasp the overall argument. Notice the premise about divine enabling and creation.
The passage uses the terms 'Majus' for Zoroastrians and 'Ahriman' for the evil principle.
2
Identify the two reasons
Nasafi gives two reasons for his claim. Find them and note how each reason contrasts Mu'tazila with Magians.
Reason one: quantity of partners. Reason two: attribution of good and evil.
3
Reflect on the theological stakes
Consider why Nasafi considers this a serious error. How does the Mu'tazila view of human agency lead to shirk? Connect to the broader Maturidi emphasis on tawhid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المحمود، وكان عين ما هو مقدور الله مقدور العبد بإقداره إياه، وما هو مقدور العبد، مقدور الله بخلقه، وهذا يعني في قول النسفي أن المعتزلة هم الذين أثبتوا شركاء الله. ويشدد النسفي في نعت المعتزلة قائلا: إنهم أشر من المجوس لوجهين: أن المجوس أثبتوا الله شريكا واحدا ، أما المعتزلة فقد جعلوا كل ما فيه حياة شريكا الله، ومن جهة أخرى أن المجوس أرادوا بإثبات الشريك تنزيه الله عما هو سفه، عندهم وأضافوا إلى الله الخير، وإلى أهر من الشر ، أما المعتزلة فقد أضافوا كل ما هو حسن أو قبيح إلى غير الله.
Praiseworthy, and what is within God's power is within the servant's power by His enabling, and what is within the servant's power is within God's power by His creation. This means, in Nasafi's view, that the Mu'tazila are those who affirmed partners to God. And Nasafi emphasizes in describing the Mu'tazila, saying: They are worse than the Magians for two reasons: the Magians affirmed one partner to God, while the Mu'tazila have made every living thing a partner to God; and another reason that the Magians intended by affirming the partner to exonerate God from what they consider foolishness, and they attributed good to God and evil to Ahriman, while the Mu'tazila attributed everything good or evil to other than God.
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This passage is a critique of the Mu'tazila regarding their doctrine of human agency. Nasafi begins by stating a premise: What is within God's power is within the servant's power by God's enabling, and what is within the servant's power is within God's power by His creation. This means that the Mu'tazila, by affirming that the servant creates his own actions, have effectively set up partners with God. He then emphasizes that the Mu'tazila are worse than the Magians (Zoroastrians) for two reasons. First, the Magians only affirmed one partner to God (i.e., Ahriman), whereas the Mu'tazila make every living being a partner to God by claiming they create their own actions. Second, the Magians intended to exonerate God from evil by attributing evil to a separate principle, while the Mu'tazila attribute both good and evil to creatures, thus denying God's sole agency over all actions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Nasafi is equating the Mu'tazila with polytheists (mushrikun) in the same sense as the Magians.
Correction: Nasafi is making a comparative theological critique; he does not declare them outside the fold of Islam but argues that their doctrine of human agency inadvertently leads to a worse form of associating partners with God than that of the Magians.

Key Takeaway
Nasafi argues that the Mu'tazila view on human agency leads to a worse form of shirk than that of the Magians, by multiplying partners and denying God's exclusive agency over all actions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The Mu'tazila attributed both good and evil to creatures. Are there areas in your life where you blame your failures entirely on yourself or others, forgetting that God is the ultimate creator of all that happens? How would your heart change if you truly saw every outcome—good and bad—as from God?
?
Nasafi says the Mu'tazila made every living being a partner to God. In your daily life, do you treat any person or object as if they have independent power over you? Whose approval or fear have you put on par with God's? Commit to one action today that breaks that false partnership.

Maturidi vs Ash'ari: Is Love the Same as Will?
Nasafi notes the Maturidi-Ash'ari disagreement on love and will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the key difference between Maturidi and Ash'ari schools regarding whether God's love and pleasure are identical to His will.

Core Idea
Maturidis distinguish love from will; Ash'aris equate them.

Within Sunni theology, the Maturidi and Ash'ari schools agree on many things, but they disagree on the relationship between divine love and divine will. This short passage by Imam al-Nasafi highlights that disagreement and also explains how each school connects will to knowledge or command. Let's read his words carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the passage once in Arabic, then the translation. Notice the two questions being answered: (1) Are love and pleasure the same as will? (2) What is will aligned with?
2
Identify the two positions
The passage clearly states the Maturidi and Ash'ari positions on the first question. Mark each one.
Maturidis: no; Ash'aris: yes.
3
Understand the second contrast
Nasafi adds that will is in accordance with knowledge (Maturidi view), while the Mu'tazila say it is in accordance with command. Reflect on why this matters.
This connects will to God's knowledge, not His commands.
4
Check your understanding
Take the checkpoint quiz to see if you can identify the Maturidi position correctly.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهنا يذكر اختلافا بين الماتريدية، والأشاعرة وهو هل المحبة والرضا بمعنى الإرادة أم لا؟ يقول الماتريدية: لا، ويقول الأشاعرة: نعم. ويقول : إن الإرادة موافقة للعلم، وعند المعتزلة موافقة للأمر.
And here he mentions a disagreement between the Maturidis and the Ash'aris: whether love (mahabbah) and pleasure (rida) are synonymous with will (irada) or not? The Maturidis say: No, and the Ash'aris say: Yes. And he says: Indeed, the will (irada) is in accordance with knowledge (ilm), while according to the Mu'tazila, it is in accordance with command (amr).
Source pages p. 102

Commentary
This passage highlights a key theological difference between the Maturidi and Ash'ari schools. The question is whether God's love and pleasure are the same as His will. Maturidis maintain they are distinct, while Ash'aris consider them identical. Al-Nasafi then adds a further clarification: will is congruent with knowledge (God wills what He knows), whereas the Mu'tazila claim that will is congruent with command (God wills what He commands). This reinforces the Maturidi position that God's will covers all things, including disobedience, but His love and pleasure do not.

Key Terms
الماتريدية
الأشاعرة
المحبة
الرضا
الإرادة
العلم
الأمر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All Sunni Muslims agree that God's love is exactly the same as His will.
Correction: This passage shows that Maturidis and Ash'aris disagree on this point. Maturidis distinguish love from will, while Ash'aris equate them. Both are Sunni schools.

Checkpoint
In the disagreement between Maturidis and Ash'aris, what is the position of the Maturidis regarding love and will?
A) Love and will are identical
B) Love is a subset of will
C) Love and will are distinct
D) Love is irrelevant to will

Key Takeaway
Maturidis distinguish divine love and pleasure from divine will, while Ash'aris identify them.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience a hardship that is clearly against God's commands, do you still believe that it is His will? How does the Maturidi distinction between will and love help you trust that God's will may not always mean He is pleased with what happens?
?
Reflect on a time you assumed that something good happening meant God loves you, and something bad meant He doesn't. How does this lesson challenge that assumption? Are you willing to accept that God's will encompasses all things, but His love is reserved for what aligns with His pleasure?
?
If God's will is based on His infinite knowledge rather than merely His commands, how does that affect your response to events you cannot understand? Can you surrender to His will without needing to feel His pleasure in every circumstance?

The Qadariyya: Magians of This Nation
Nasafi cites the hadith and identifies Qadariyya as Mu'tazila.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand who the Qadariyya are according to the Prophet's hadith and how al-Nasafi identifies them with the Mu'tazila.

Core Idea
The Qadariyya are the Mu'tazila, identified as the Magians of this nation.

This lesson introduces a powerful hadith that labels the Qadariyya as the Magians of the Muslim community. Al-Nasafi clarifies that the Qadariyya are the Mu'tazila, despite the latter's denial. We will explore the hadith and the initial identification.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadith and its sources
Read the hadith that the Prophet called the Qadariyya the Magians of this nation and note the multiple sources cited by al-Nasafi.
The hadith also instructs not to visit them when sick or attend their funerals.
2
Understand the meaning of 'Magians of this nation'
Consider why the Prophet would compare this group to the Magians (Zoroastrians), a dualistic religion known for believing in two creators.
This sets the stage for the doctrinal comparison.
3
Learn al-Nasafi's identification and the Mu'tazila's counter-claim
Al-Nasafi explicitly says the Qadariyya are the Mu'tazila, but note that the Mu'tazila reject this and define Qadariyya differently.
The debate over the label Qadariyya is central to the discussion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكلام في القدرية
يقول : "القدرية" مجوس هذه الأمة، إن مرضوا فلا تعودوهم، وإن ماتوا فلا تشهدوهم.
ورواه الحاكم في المستدرك ج ص ٨٥ من حديث ابن عمر عنه ، ورواه أبو داود في سننه ٢ ص ٥٢٤ من حديث ابن عمر.
ويذكر أحمد بن حنبل في مسنده ج ٢ ص ٢٧٩ من حديث ابن عمر قال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم: "إن لكل أمة مجوسًا وإن مجوس أمتي المكذبون بالقدر... " فمن هم القدرية؟ يقول النسفي بأنهم المعتزلة. إلا أن المعتزلة ينفون عن أنفسهم ذلك يقولون : إن القدرية هم الذين يقولون : إن القدر خيره وشره من الله. ويذكر النسفي أدلة المعتزلة على قولهم ويناقشها بطريقة منهجية سليمة، ويقول: إن وجه الكشف عن المراد بهذا الاسم هو النظر في المقالات ليظهر الموافق للمجوس في قوله من المخالف لهم. ثم يذكر بعض وجوه المشابهة بين المعتزلة والمجوس في أفكارهما من ذلك مثلا قول المجوس : كان الله ولا شيء معه ثم حدثت منه فكرة ردية فحدث إبليس فخلق الله الخير وخلق إبليس الشر والمعتزلة يقولون: كان الله ولا شيء غيره ثم حدثت إرادته من غير أن كان الله تعالى في حدوثها إرادة أو اختيار وكان بها جميع العالم.. وهنا تكون المشابهة بينهما.
Discussion of the Qadariyya. He (the Prophet) said: 'The Qadariyya are the Magians of this nation; if they become sick, do not visit them, and if they die, do not attend their funeral.' Al-Hakim narrated it in al-Mustadrak, vol. p. 85, from Ibn 'Umar; and Abu Dawud narrated it in his Sunan, vol. 2, p. 524, from Ibn 'Umar. Ahmad ibn Hanbal mentions in his Musnad, vol. 2, p. 279, from Ibn 'Umar that the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) said: 'Every nation has Magians, and the Magians of my nation are those who deny the decree (qadar)...' So who are the Qadariyya? Al-Nasafi says they are the Mu'tazila. However, the Mu'tazila deny that about themselves, saying: The Qadariyya are those who say that the decree, both its good and evil, is from Allah. Al-Nasafi mentions the Mu'tazila's proofs for their position and discusses them in a sound, methodical way. He says: The way to uncover the intended meaning of this name is to examine the doctrines in order to distinguish who agrees with the Magians from who disagrees. Then he mentions some aspects of similarity between the Mu'tazila and the Magians in their ideas. For example, the Magians say: There was God and nothing with Him, then a base thought occurred to Him, so Iblis came into being, and God created good and Iblis created evil. The Mu'tazila say: There was God and nothing other than Him, then His will originated without God having any will or choice in its origination, and through it the entire world came into being. Here lies the similarity between them.
Source pages p. 108

Commentary
This passage is a definition and identification of the Qadariyya sect, along with a comparison to Zoroastrians (Majus).

The passage begins by citing a hadith that the Qadariyya are the 'Magians of this nation,' with recommendations not to visit them when sick or attend their funerals. The author then cites the sources of this hadith from al-Hakim, Abu Dawud, and Ahmad.

Al-Nasafi clarifies that the Qadariyya are the Mu'tazila. However, the Mu'tazila reject this label and instead define Qadariyya as those who attribute both good and evil of the decree to God (i.e., fatalists). Al-Nasafi notes that he will examine their evidences and refute them methodically.

The key point is that the identification of the Qadariyya depends on comparing their views to those of the Magians. Al-Nasafi shows a specific similarity: both groups trace the origin of evil to a secondary principle apart from God's will and choice. The Magians say that a base thought arose from God, leading to Iblis who creates evil. The Mu'tazila say that God's will originated without any prior will or choice on His part, and through that will the world came into existence. In both cases, evil is not directly attributed to God's will, and a form of dualism or compartmentalization is introduced.

Thus, the Mu'tazila are like the Magians because both posit that God is not the sole creator of evil, and that His action is not fully voluntary.

Key Terms
قدرية (Qadariyya)
مجوس (Majus)
تكذيب بالقدر (denial of decree)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila are not Qadariyya because they affirm free will.
Correction: Al-Nasafi argues they are Qadariyya because their doctrine parallels Magian dualism, as will be explained.

Checkpoint
According to the hadith cited by al-Nasafi, what is the Qadariyya's relation to the Magians?
A) They are the same as Magians.
B) They are the Magians of this nation.
C) They are enemies of Magians.
D) They are followers of Magians.

Key Takeaway
The Prophet identified the Qadariyya as the Magians of his nation, and al-Nasafi specifies that they are the Mu'tazila.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Prophet instructed us to avoid the Qadariyya even when they are sick or dying. Do I take the company of those who deny Allah's decree lightly? Whose influence on my faith am I tolerating that I should cut off?
?
Al-Nasafi calls the Mu'tazila 'Qadariyya', but they deny it. Am I ever guilty of redefining my wrong beliefs to avoid accountability? Think of one area where I have justified a flawed understanding. Will I repent and seek the truth?
?
The comparison to Magians highlights a dualistic mindset. Do I ever, in my heart, attribute evil or misfortune to any cause other than Allah? How can I train myself to see all events—good and bad—as solely from Him?

The Similarity Between Mu'tazila and Zoroastrians
Nasafi compares Mu'tazila and Magian doctrines on the origin of will and evil.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Nasafi compares the Mu'tazila's doctrine of the origin of will to the Zoroastrian dualistic belief.

Core Idea
Both Mu'tazila and Magians trace evil to a secondary principle lacking divine will.

This lesson delves into al-Nasafi's detailed comparison between the Mu'tazila and the Magians (Zoroastrians). He highlights a striking parallelism in their explanations for the origin of evil, showing that both groups introduce a form of dualism that compromises God's sole creative agency.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazila and Magian statements
Read the specific doctrines as presented by al-Nasafi for both groups.
Note that both start with 'There was God and nothing with Him...'
2
Identify the similarity in their positions
Both assert that the ultimate cause of evil arises from a secondary process not directly willed by God.
For Magians, it's a base thought; for Mu'tazila, it's an un-willed origination of will.
3
Reflect on why this makes the Mu'tazila like the Magians
In both cases, God is not the direct, fully voluntary creator of evil, leading to a dualistic implication.
This justifies the hadith label 'Magians of this nation'.

Source Text

الكلام في القدرية
يقول : "القدرية" مجوس هذه الأمة، إن مرضوا فلا تعودوهم، وإن ماتوا فلا تشهدوهم.

Discussion of the Qadariyya. He (the Prophet) said: 'The Qadariyya are the Magians of this nation; if they become sick, do not visit them, and if they die, do not attend their funeral.' Ahmad ibn Hanbal mentions in his Musnad, vol. 2, p. 279, from Ibn 'Umar that the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) said: 'Every nation has Magians, and the Magians of my nation are those who deny the decree (qadar)...' However, the Mu'tazila deny that about themselves, saying: The Qadariyya are those who say that the decree, both its good and evil, is from Allah. Al-Nasafi mentions the Mu'tazila's proofs for their position and discusses them in a sound, methodical way. He says: The way to uncover the intended meaning of this name is to examine the doctrines in order to distinguish who agrees with the Magians from who disagrees. Then he mentions some aspects of similarity between the Mu'tazila and the Magians in their ideas. For example, the Magians say: There was God and nothing with Him, then a base thought occurred to Him, so Iblis came into being, and God created good and Iblis created evil. The Mu'tazila say: There was God and nothing other than Him, then His will originated without God having any will or choice in its origination, and through it the entire world came into being. Here lies the similarity between them.

A) Both deny the existence of evil.

D) Both believe in two gods.

Key Takeaway
Al-Nasafi argues that the Mu'tazila's belief in an unwilled origination of God's will makes them similar to the Magians who attribute evil to a separate entity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly accept that God has absolute will and choice in all things, how does that change the way you interpret the existence of evil in your own life? Are you willing to submit to God's will even when you cannot see the wisdom?
?
Al-Nasafi warns against attributing any creation of evil to a source other than God. In what areas of your life do you still implicitly blame circumstances, people, or even yourself as independent causes of your difficulties, rather than seeing God's decree behind them?
?
The Mu'tazila's error was in trying to protect God from evil by separating His will from creation. Do you ever try to 'protect' God by making excuses for Him, rather than humbly accepting His absolute sovereignty over everything, including what you perceive as bad?

The Qadariyya: Magians of This Ummah
Nasafi cites the hadith on Qadariyya and identifies them as Mu'tazila.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Nasafi identifies the Mu'tazila as the Qadariyya and how this refutes their self-definition.

Core Idea
A hadith warns: the Qadariyya are the Magians of this Ummah.

In this passage, Imam al-Nasafi presents a powerful prophetic tradition that denounces the Qadariyya as the 'Magians of this Ummah.' He then identifies this sect as the Mu'tazila, sparking a debate about who truly deserves the label. Understanding this identification is key to grasping the sectarian landscape of early kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadith and its narrations
Start by reading the Arabic text of the hadith and the list of narrators. Notice that multiple major hadith collections (Hakim, Abu Dawud, Ahmad) authenticate it.
This establishes the authority of the hadith.
2
Identify Nasafi's claim
After the hadith, Nasafi explicitly states: the Qadariyya are the Mu'tazila. Mark this as the central claim of the passage.
This is a sectarian identification with significant theological implications.
3
Understand the Mu'tazila denial
The Mu'tazila reject being called Qadariyya. Instead, they define Qadariyya as those who say all predestination (good and evil) is from Allah. Consider why this definition actually describes Ahl al-Sunna, not them.
This reveals a rhetorical reversal: the Mu'tazila try to shift the label, but Nasafi's hadith refutes them.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يقول: "القدرية" مجوس هذه الأمة، إن مرضوا فلا تعودوهم، وإن ماتوا فلا تشهدوهم".
ورواه الحاكم في المستدرك ج ص ٨٥ من حديث ابن عمر عنه ، ورواه أبو داود في سننه ٢ ص ٥٢٤ من حديث ابن عمر.
ويذكر أحمد بن حنبل في مسنده ج ٢ ص ٢٧٩ من حديث ابن عمر قال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم: "إن لكل أمة مجوسًا وإن مجوس أمتي المكذبون بالقدر... " فمن هم القدرية؟ يقول النسفي بأنهم المعتزلة. إلا أن المعتزلة ينفون عن أنفسهم ذلك يقولون : إن القدرية هم الذين يقولون : إن القدر خيره وشره من الله.
He said: 'The Qadariyya are the Magians of this Ummah. If they become sick, do not visit them, and if they die, do not attend their funerals.' And al-Hakim narrated it in al-Mustadrak, vol. 1 p. 85 from the hadith of Ibn 'Umar from him, and Abu Dawud narrated it in his Sunan, vol. 2 p. 524 from the hadith of Ibn 'Umar. And Ahmad ibn Hanbal mentions in his Musnad, vol. 2 p. 279 from the hadith of Ibn 'Umar that the Messenger of Allah, may Allah bless him and give him peace, said: 'Indeed, for every nation there are Magians, and indeed the Magians of my nation are those who deny predestination...' So who are the Qadariyya? Al-Nasafi says they are the Mu'tazila. However, the Mu'tazila deny that of themselves, saying: The Qadariyya are those who say that predestination, both its good and its evil, is from Allah.
Source pages p. 108

Commentary
This passage presents a hadith from the Prophet (peace be upon him) equating the Qadariyya with the Magians (Zoroastrians) of this Ummah, and then provides citations from major hadith collections. The author, Imam al-Nasafi, then identifies the Qadariyya as the Mu'tazila. He notes that the Mu'tazila themselves reject this label, instead defining the Qadariyya as those who affirm that all predestination (good and evil) comes from God—which is actually the position of Ahl al-Sunna. This sets up the debate: Nasafi's claim is that the Mu'tazila are the true Qadariyya, despite their denial. The passage includes multiple narrations to authenticate the hadith.

Key Terms
القدرية
مجوس
المعتزلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila claimed that the Qadariyya are those who affirm that all predestination (good and evil) comes from Allah.
Correction: Nasafi shows that the Qadariyya are actually the Mu'tazila themselves, based on the hadith's condemnation.

Checkpoint
Who does Nasafi identify as the Qadariyya?
A) The Magians
B) The Mu'tazila
C) Those who deny predestination
D) Those who affirm predestination from God

Key Takeaway
The hadith's warning and Nasafi's identification define the Qadariyya sect as the Mu'tazila, refuting their denial of this label.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever distanced yourself from someone because of their beliefs about predestination? Did you consider the hadith's instruction to not visit or attend funerals? What does that say about your heart's alignment with the truth?
?
When you read that the Mu'tazila tried to deflect the label, did you recognize any area in your own life where you twist the truth to avoid a negative label? How can you embrace the truth even when it comes with a harsh warning?
?
Are you willing to accept that your own views on Qadr might align with those condemned in this hadith? Take a moment to honestly assess your belief: do you truly affirm that everything is from Allah, or do you find yourself denying His control in some aspects of your life?

Nasafi's Method: Comparing Mu'tazila to Magians
Nasafi outlines his method and gives the first similarity in origin of will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi systematically compares the Mu'tazila to the Magians and why the first similarity lies in the origin of will.

Core Idea
Comparing doctrines reveals hidden dualism.

In this passage, al-Nasafi explains his method for identifying the Qadariyya: by comparing their doctrines to those of the Magians. He then presents the first point of similarity — both groups posit a source of will or evil that is not from God's eternal will. This introduces a dualistic element that Nasafi will criticize.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get the overall flow of Nasafi's argument.
Notice that he first states his method, then gives an example.
2
Identify Nasafi's method
Find the sentence where Nasafi describes his method: 'the way to reveal the intended meaning...' and understand that he compares doctrines to see who agrees with the Magians.
This shows a systematic theological comparison.
3
Find the first similarity
Compare the Magian belief about the origin of evil (from Iblis) with the Mu'tazila belief about the origin of will (without prior divine will). Notice the common element: both introduce a causation not directly from God's eternal will.
This is the core of the similarity Nasafi points out.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويذكر النسفي أدلة المعتزلة على قولهم ويناقشها بطريقة منهجية سليمة، ويقول: إن وجه الكشف عن المراد بهذا الاسم هو النظر في المقالات ليظهر الموافق للمجوس في قوله من المخالف لهم. ثم يذكر بعض وجوه المشابهة بين المعتزلة والمجوس في أفكارهما من ذلك مثلا قول المجوس : كان الله ولا شيء معه ثم حدثت منه فكرة ردية فحدث إبليس فخلق الله الخير وخلق إبليس الشر والمعتزلة يقولون: كان الله ولا شيء غيره ثم حدثت إرادته من غير أن كان الله تعالى في حدوثها إرادة أو اختيار وكان بها جميع العالم.. وهنا تكون المشابهة بينهما.
And al-Nasafi mentions the proofs of the Mu'tazila for their statement and discusses them in a sound methodological way, and says: The method of revealing the intended meaning of this name is to examine the doctrines in order to show who agrees with the Magians in his statement from among those who disagree with them. Then he mentions some aspects of similarity between the Mu'tazila and the Magians in their ideas, for example: the statement of the Magians: 'There was God and nothing with Him, then an evil thought occurred from Him, so Iblis came into being, and God created good and Iblis created evil.' And the Mu'tazila say: 'There was God and nothing other than Him, then His will occurred without God Almighty having will or choice in its occurrence, and through it the entire world came into being...' And here lies the similarity between them.
Source pages p. 108

Commentary
This passage explains al-Nasafi's systematic method: to identify the Mu'tazila as Qadariyya by comparing their doctrines to those of the Magians. He first states that the way to uncover the referent of the name 'Qadariyya' is to examine the respective doctrines and see who aligns with the Magians. Then he presents the first similarity: both groups posit an origination of will or evil from a source other than God's eternal will and choice. The Magians say Iblis originated from a bad thought and created evil; the Mu'tazila say God's will occurred without prior will or choice, and through that will the world came into being—thus they attribute efficient causation to a will that is not willed by God. This introduces a dualistic element.

Key Terms
الإرادة
المجوس
المعتزلة
المشابهة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) God created it

D) It is eternal

Key Takeaway
Nasafi uses doctrinal comparison to expose the dualistic implications of Mu'tazila theology.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you examine your own understanding of why evil exists, do you ever unconsciously assume that some events arise from a source other than Allah's perfect will and wisdom? How does the similarity Nasafi exposes force you to examine hidden assumptions in your heart? Will you ask Allah to purify your belief in His sole sovereignty?
?
Nasafi first presents the Mu'tazila's proofs before criticizing. Do you approach opposing views with that same fairness? Or do you dismiss them without understanding? How can you adopt Nasafi's method of fair comparison in your own discussions about differing beliefs?
?
If attributing the origin of will to anything other than Allah's eternal will is akin to dualism, what does that mean for how you view your own choices? Are there moments where you feel your actions are independent of Allah's will? How does this lesson call you to repent and reaffirm that 'there is no will except by Allah'?

Belief in Multiple Creators: Mu'tazila and Magians
Nasafi states the first similarity: both groups affirm multiple creators.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Mu'tazila and Magians both affirm multiple creators, and why Nasafi considers this a shared doctrine.

Core Idea
Attributing creative power to creatures implicitly affirms multiple creators.

Nasafi continues his comparison between the Mu'tazila and Magians by highlighting another striking parallel: both groups effectively believe in more than one creator. While the Magians explicitly posit a dualistic principle (Yazdan and Ahriman), the Mu'tazila do so implicitly by assigning to humans the power to create their own actions. This lesson examines that claim.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the source passage carefully. Notice the parallel phrasing: 'and likewise the Mu'tazila' is repeated for both claims. This passage is clearly making a comparative point.
2
Understand the Magian position
The Magians believed in two creators: one good (Yazdan) and one evil (Ahriman). This is a clear dualism. Nasafi uses this as a foil.
The Mu'tazila do not explicitly say there are two gods, but their theology leads to a similar effect.
3
Analyze the Mu'tazila position
The Mu'tazila hold that humans are the creators of their own actions (especially evil ones), because God cannot do evil. This effectively gives humans a creative power independent of God, thus implying multiple creators.
Nasafi's argument is that any being that creates something independently is, in that respect, a creator alongside God.
4
Reflect on the theological implications
Consider why Nasafi finds this similarity significant. It strengthens his argument that the Mu'tazila are closer to dualism than to true monotheism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمجوس جعلوا الله تعالى وغيره خالقين للعالم وكذا المعتزلة.
والمجوس يأبون دخول ما هو قبيح تحت قدرة الله وكذا المعتزلة.
And the Magians made God Almighty and other than Him creators of the world, and likewise the Mu'tazila. And the Magians refuse the inclusion of what is ugly under the power of God, and likewise the Mu'tazila.
Source pages p. 108

Commentary
This passage presents two further points of similarity between the Magians and the Mu'tazila. First, both affirm multiple creators: the Magians attribute some creation to Iblis, and the Mu'tazila attribute acts of humans to the humans themselves, thus making each human a creator of his own actions. Second, both deny that evil or ugly acts fall under God's power: the Magians exclude evil from God's creation, and the Mu'tazila deny that God creates evil actions, attributing them to human free will. Nasafi considers these points as evidence that the Mu'tazila share core doctrines with the dualist Magians.

Key Terms
خالقين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila are pure monotheists because they affirm tawhid.
Correction: Nasafi argues that by attributing creative power to humans, they implicitly introduce multiple creators, contradicting pure monotheism.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, what do the Magians and Mu'tazila share regarding creation?
A) They both deny creation entirely
B) They both affirm a single creator
C) They both affirm multiple creators
D) They both believe God creates everything

Key Takeaway
Belief in human creative power leads to the same error as explicit dualism: multiple creators.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever feel that your own choices and actions are 'yours' in a way that competes with Allah's absolute creation? In what moments do you sense a desire to claim independent power over your deeds?
?
If someone accused you of implicitly affirming multiple creators by the way you speak about your own achievements, how would you defend the oneness of Allah's creative act? Is your heart truly convinced that every outcome comes from Him alone?
?
Read the source again: 'and likewise the Mu'tazila.' Does it unsettle you that a respected theological school could be accused of the same error as explicit dualists? What does this warn you about the danger of even subtle deviations in belief?

Denying God's Power Over Evil: Mu'tazila and Magians
Nasafi states the second similarity: both groups deny God's power over evil.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will grasp how both the Magians and Mu'tazila deny that evil actions fall under God's power, and why this undermines divine omnipotence.

Core Idea
Limiting God's power to only good things denies His absolute omnipotence.

The second parallel Nasafi draws is even more direct: both the Magians and the Mu'tazila refuse to accept that evil or ugly acts (al-qabih) are under God's power. The Magians attribute evil to a separate principle, while the Mu'tazila say humans create their own evil acts to preserve God's justice. Nasafi sees this restriction on God's power as a serious deviation.

Guided Path
1
Read the second part of the passage
Read the Arabic: 'والمجوس يأبون دخول ما هو قبيح تحت قدرة الله وكذا المعتزلة.' Note the verb 'يأبون' (they refuse), which implies active denial.
2
Understand the Magian view on evil
Magians believe evil originates from an evil creator (Ahriman), not from the good God. Therefore, evil is outside God's power by nature.
This is a dualistic solution to the problem of evil.
3
Understand the Mu'tazila view on evil
Mu'tazila argue that God, being just and good, cannot do evil. So evil acts must be created by humans. Thus God's power does not extend to evil actions.
This is their way to preserve God's justice and avoid attributing evil to Him.
4
Recognize the common denial
Both groups end up placing evil outside the scope of God's power, making God less than omnipotent. Nasafi considers this a shared heresy.
For Sunni orthodoxy, God's power encompasses all things, including evil, even if He is not pleased with it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمجوس جعلوا الله تعالى وغيره خالقين للعالم وكذا المعتزلة.
والمجوس يأبون دخول ما هو قبيح تحت قدرة الله وكذا المعتزلة.
And the Magians made God Almighty and other than Him creators of the world, and likewise the Mu'tazila. And the Magians refuse the inclusion of what is ugly under the power of God, and likewise the Mu'tazila.
Source pages p. 108

Commentary
This passage presents two further points of similarity between the Magians and the Mu'tazila. First, both affirm multiple creators: the Magians attribute some creation to Iblis, and the Mu'tazila attribute acts of humans to the humans themselves, thus making each human a creator of his own actions. Second, both deny that evil or ugly acts fall under God's power: the Magians exclude evil from God's creation, and the Mu'tazila deny that God creates evil actions, attributing them to human free will. Nasafi considers these points as evidence that the Mu'tazila share core doctrines with the dualist Magians.

Key Terms

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What do the Magians and Mu'tazila refuse regarding God's power?
A) That God has power over good
B) That God has power over evil
C) That God has power over anything
D) That God's power is eternal

Key Takeaway
Denying God's power over evil, even for the sake of His justice, undermines His absolute omnipotence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face evil or suffering in your life, do you ever unconsciously limit God's power, thinking He either cannot stop it or cannot use it for good? How does this lesson confront that tendency, and what will you do to repent of it?
?
The Mu'tazila denied God's power over evil to protect His justice. In what areas of your life do you try to 'protect' God's reputation by restricting His sovereignty? Are you willing to surrender that control today, even if it means accepting that He is sovereign over things you don't understand?
?
If God's power truly encompasses all things, including the ugly and painful, what does that demand of you regarding the hardships you have experienced? Will you commit to seeing them as within His sovereign plan, or will you continue to resist His rule over those parts of your life?

Nasafi's Preference for Magians over Mu'tazila
Nasafi enumerates two respects in which Magians are better than Mu'tazila.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two specific arguments Nasafi uses to claim that Magians are less blameworthy than Mu'tazila, and the theological reasoning behind each.

Core Idea
Two wrongs don't make a right, but Nasafi uses comparison to expose Mu'tazila errors.

Nasafi continues his critique of the Mu'tazila by comparing them to the Magians. While both groups are misguided, Nasafi argues that the Magians are actually better in two specific respects. This lesson unpacks those two points and shows how Nasafi uses them to highlight the more severe theological errors of the Mu'tazila.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to get the gist.
2
Identify the two comparisons
Notice the two points: (A) number of partners, (B) attribution of good and evil. Mark them in your mind.
3
Understand the reasoning
For each point, think about why Nasafi considers the Magian position less harmful. The key is that the Mu'tazila multiply partners and impugn God's power.
4
Reflect on the rhetorical strategy
Consider why Nasafi would compare Mu'tazila to Magians. What effect does this have? This is a rhetorical device to show that even a polytheistic sect is better in some ways.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهو في عرضه لوجوه التشابه بين المجوس والمعتزلة يجعل المجوس أحسن من المعتزلة نأخذ ذلك من هذه العبارات:
أ- المجوس أثبتوا الله شريكا واحدا أما المعتزلة فبقولهم : إن الإنسان يخلق فعل نفسه؛ قد جعلوا كل إنسان شريكا الله أي أنهم أثبتوا شركاء.
ب المجوس جعلوا الله خالق الخيرات أما الشرور فخالقها الشيطان، أما المعتزلة فقد نسبوا أحسن المحاسن لغيره، وجعلوا الله غير قادر على خلق فعل أصلح مما فعل بل جعلوه مجبورا بإيجابهم الأصلح عليه سبحانه.
And in his presentation of the aspects of similarity between the Magians and the Mu'tazila, he makes the Magians better than the Mu'tazila. We take that from these statements:
A) The Magians affirmed for God one partner, whereas the Mu'tazila, by their saying that man creates his own action, made every human a partner of God, i.e., they affirmed multiple partners.
B) The Magians made God the creator of good things, whereas the creator of evils is Satan. As for the Mu'tazila, they attributed the best of good things to other than Him, and made God incapable of creating an act that is better than what He did; rather they made Him compelled by their imposing the 'most beneficial' upon Him, exalted be He.
Source pages p. 108

Commentary
In this passage, Nasafi goes a step further and asserts that the Magians are actually better than the Mu'tazila in two specific ways. First, the Magians only associate one partner with God (Iblis), while the Mu'tazila, by granting each human the power to create his own actions, effectively make every human a partner of God—multiplying shirk. Second, the Magians at least attribute the creation of good to God and evil to Satan, preserving God's goodness. In contrast, the Mu'tazila attribute the best of good actions to human effort rather than God, and they claim that God is obligated to do what is best (al-aslah) for His servants, thereby limiting His freedom and implying He is compelled—which Nasafi sees as a worse form of blasphemy.

Key Terms
شريك
خالق
أصلح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think Nasafi is praising Magians or claiming they are correct.
Correction: Nasafi uses this comparison as hyperbole to condemn Mu'tazila more severely. He is not endorsing Magian beliefs; rather, he shows that even a polytheistic group is better in these respects than the Mu'tazila.

Checkpoint
How many partners do the Magians affirm for God according to Nasafi?
A) Many
B) One
C) None
D) Two

Key Takeaway
Nasafi's comparison of Mu'tazila to Magians reveals that in two key respects — multiplicity of partners and attribution of good — the Mu'tazila are actually worse off.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever compared yourself to someone you consider worse to justify your own faults? How does Nasafi's comparison of the Mu'tazila to Magians force you to reexamine your own heart and actions? Will you commit to measuring yourself against the standard of revelation rather than the failings of others?
?
When you pray for something good and it doesn't happen, do you secretly think God could have done better? In what specific area of your life do you struggle to trust that God's choice is truly the most beneficial, even when it contradicts your desires? What would it mean for you to surrender that area to His will today?
?
Nasafi argues that the Mu'tazila, by imposing 'the most beneficial' on God, actually strip Him of His sovereignty. Where in your own understanding of God do you impose your own expectations or conditions? Are you prepared to worship a God who is absolutely free, even if His freedom means your comfort is not guaranteed?

Sectarian Views on the Major Sinner
Author summarizes views of Murji'a, Mu'tazila, Khawarij, and Hasan al-Basri on the major sinner.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the differing positions of early Islamic sects on the status and fate of a Muslim who commits a major sin.

Core Idea
The major sinner's fate was a defining controversy that split early Islamic theology.

This passage offers a concise survey of how four major groups—Murji'a, Mu'tazila, Khawarij, and Hasan al-Basri—viewed the Muslim who commits a major sin. Each position reflects deeper theological commitments about faith, works, and God's justice. As you read, notice how each school's view on this single issue reveals its broader approach to theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the four views
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Identify the positions of the Murji'a, Mu'tazila, Khawarij, and Hasan al-Basri. Note how each group defines the major sinner's status.
The author begins by referencing the Battle of Siffin to contextualize the Murji'a stance.
2
Compare the Murji'a and Ahl al-Sunna
The author says the Murji'a are like Ahl al-Sunna in placing the major sinner under Allah's will. Why might these two groups be similar here? Consider their shared emphasis on God's prerogative in judgment.
Common misunderstanding: some conflate Murji'a and Ahl al-Sunna, but the author distinguishes them.
3
Examine the Mu'tazila middle position
The Mu'tazila call the major sinner a fasiq and consign him to a 'station between the two stations.' Reflect on how this middle ground differs from both the Murji'a and the Khawarij.
This concept (manzila bayn al-manzilatayn) is a hallmark of Mu'tazila theology.
4
Contrast the Khawarij and Hasan al-Basri
The Khawarij declare the major sinner a disbeliever, while Hasan al-Basri calls him a hypocrite. Both are more severe than the others. Consider why Hasan's label might be distinct from outright disbelief.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأحسب أن هذا الكلام لو قارنته بموقفهم من الفريقين المتقاتلين في صفين وأن رأيهم أنهما مؤمنان إلا أن أمرهما في الآخرة إلى الله عز وجل يعذب من يشاء ويغفر لمن يشاء ومن هنا فإني أخرج بنتيجة تخالف النسفي، وتقول: إن المرجئة في هذا مثل أهل السنة يجعلون مرتكب الكبيرة في الآخرة بين مشيئة الله. والمعتزلة يسمونه فاسقا وحكمه أنه في منزلة بين المنزلتين، ولو مات قبل التوبة خلد في النار ولم ينفعه إيمانه. أما الخوارج فواضح أنهم يكفرونه ويقولون بأنه مخلد في النار، ويذكر الحسن البصري أن مرتكب الكبيرة منافق.
And I think that if you compare this statement with their position regarding the two fighting parties at Siffin, and that their view is that both are believers, except that their affair in the Hereafter is up to Allah Almighty—He punishes whom He wills and forgives whom He wills. From here I conclude a result differing from al-Nasafi, saying: Indeed, the Murji'a in this are like Ahl al-Sunna: they place the committer of a major sin in the Hereafter under the will of Allah. The Mu'tazila call him a sinner (fasiq) and his ruling is that he is in a station between the two stations (manzila bayn al-manzilatayn), and if he dies before repentance, he will be eternally in the Fire and his faith will not benefit him. As for the Khawarij, it is clear that they declare him a disbeliever and say that he is eternally in the Fire. And al-Hasan al-Basri mentions that the committer of a major sin is a hypocrite (munafiq).
Source pages p. 111

Commentary
This passage is a survey of the positions of different Islamic sects on the fate of a Muslim who commits a major sin without repentance. The author begins by noting that if one examines the stance of the Murji'a regarding the fighting parties at the Battle of Siffin—where both sides were considered believers—one finds that they leave their final judgement to Allah. He then contrasts this with his own view, which he says differs from al-Nasafi. The Murji'a and Ahl al-Sunna both place the major sinner under Allah's will. The Mu'tazila label such a person a fasiq and consign him to a station between belief and disbelief; if he dies unrepentant, he is eternally in Hell. The Khawarij go further, declaring him a disbeliever. Finally, Hasan al-Basri is reported to have called the major sinner a hypocrite. This overview sets the stage for al-Nasafi's defence of the Ahl al-Sunna position.

Key Terms
مرتكب الكبيرة
المرجئة
أهل السنة
المعتزلة
فاسق
منزلة بين المنزلتين
الخوارج
منافق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Murji'a and Ahl al-Sunna hold identical positions on the major sinner.
Correction: While both groups place the major sinner under Allah's will, the author distinguishes them: the Murji'a are like Ahl al-Sunna in this respect, but they are not identical schools. Ahl al-Sunna maintain a more nuanced view of faith and works.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which group holds that the committer of a major sin is in a 'station between the two stations'?
A) Murji'a
B) Ahl al-Sunna
C) Mu'tazila
D) Khawarij

Key Takeaway
The four positions on the major sinner—ranging from leaving his fate to Allah (Murji'a/Ahl al-Sunna) to declaring him a disbeliever (Khawarij) or a hypocrite (Hasan al-Basri)—illustrate the core theological divisions of early Islam.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you were to die tonight with a major sin unrepented, which of these positions would be true for you? And what does that tell you about your need for repentance now?
?
The Khawarij declared the major sinner a disbeliever. Is there any sin in your life that you treat as though it has removed your faith? How does this lesson challenge that assumption?
?
Hasan al-Basri said the major sinner is a hypocrite. Do you ever find yourself living like a hypocrite—outwardly Muslim but inwardly comfortable with sin? What would it take to close that gap today?

Nasafi's Takfir and the Author's Rebuttal
Nasafi's takfir of Mu'tazila and Khawarij and the author's initial rebuttal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author of Tabsirat al-Adilla disagrees with Nasafi's declaration of disbelief against the Mu'tazila and Khawarij, based on the definition of faith as tasdiq.

Core Idea
Faith is affirmation; those who affirm cannot be absolute disbelievers.

In this passage, the author presents a key disagreement with Imam al-Nasafi over the status of the Mu'tazila and Khawarij. Nasafi argues they are disbelievers because they despair of God's mercy, but the author counters that faith, defined as tasdiq, is present in both groups. Let's examine Nasafi's evidence and the author's rebuttal.

Guided Path
1
Read Nasafi's argument
Carefully read the first part where Nasafi declares the Mu'tazila and Khawarij disbelievers and cites Quran 12:87. Notice his reasoning.
The verse is about despairing of God's mercy, but the author will challenge its application.
2
Identify the author's disagreement
The author explicitly says 'I disagree' and gives a reason: according to Ahl al-Sunna, faith is tasdiq, and both groups possess it.
Tasdiq means heartfelt affirmation, not just verbal confession.
3
Understand the implication
If faith is tasdiq, then having it excludes someone from being a true kafir in the sense of apostasy. The author is protecting the Islamic status of these groups.
This sets up the charitable reinterpretation in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والنسفي يكفر الخوارج والمعتزلة لقولهم في مرتكب الكبيرة، مستدلا على ذلك بأنهم يئسوا من رحمة الله، وقد قال تعالى: ﴿ إِنَّهُ لَا يَأْيَنَسُ مِن زَوْعِ اللَّهِ إِلَّا الْقَوْمُ الكَفِرُونَ ﴾ [يوسف: ۸۷] ويدير حول كلامه هذا نقاشا عقليا ليؤكد رأيه في كفر المعتزلة والخوارج. وأخالف الإمام النسفي في رأيه في هذا فإنه على رأي أهل السنة - والنسفي منهم - إن الإيمان هو التصديق وهو موجود لدى المعتزلة والخوارج، حتى لو اختلفنا في تعريف الإيمان، ورائي في ذلك أن المعتزلة كان جل همهم الدفاع عن الإسلام ورد الضربات والشطحات العقلية الموجهة إليه من الفلسفات الأخرى بل والديانات الأخرى. ولقد كان هدف الخوارج من تكفير مرتكب الكبيرة هو أن يلتزم المسلمون بكل ما جاء به القرآن والسنة المطهرة، فهم فقط شددوا لأنهم رأوا الخروج عن العقيدة. وهم زلوا حينما كفروا الصحابة الأعلام - رضوان الله عليهم - لكني أحتسبها لهم سقطة عصبية، وأحسب أن جيلا طاهرا من المسلمين يأتي لينقي المجرى الطاهر من كل الشوائب التي علقت به معطيا كل اتجاه إسلامي حقه دون تكفير. وقول الله سبحانه وتعالى خير شاهد على ما أراه: إِنَّ اللَّهَ لَا يَغْفِرُ أَن يُشْرَكَ بِهِ وَيَغْفِرُ مَا دُونَ ذَلِكَ لِمَن يَشَاءُ [النساء: ١١٦] ويمكنني حمل تكفير النسفي للمعتزلة والخوارج في أنه لا يريد كفر الملة أو المعتقد وإنما كفر النعمة الذي هو كفر العصيان.
And al-Nasafi declares the Khawarij and the Mu'tazila as disbelievers due to their statement about the committer of a major sin, adducing that they have despaired of the mercy of Allah, and He said: 'Indeed, none despairs of the relief of Allah except the disbelieving people' [Yusuf 87]. And he conducts a rational discussion around this statement to affirm his view on the disbelief of the Mu'tazila and the Khawarij. And I disagree with Imam al-Nasafi in his opinion on this; for according to the view of Ahl al-Sunna—and al-Nasafi is among them—indeed faith is affirmation (tasdiq), and it is present among the Mu'tazila and the Khawarij, even if we differ in the definition of faith. My view in this is that the Mu'tazila's main concern was defending Islam and repelling the intellectual blows and excesses directed against it from other philosophies and even other religions. And the goal of the Khawarij in declaring the committer of a major sin a disbeliever was that Muslims should adhere to everything brought by the Qur'an and the purified Sunna; they only became strict because they saw deviation from the creed. And they slipped when they declared the prominent Companions—may Allah be pleased with them—disbelievers, but I consider that for them a mistake of zeal. And I think that a pure generation of Muslims will come to purify the pure course from all the impurities that have clung to it, giving every Islamic direction its due without declaring disbelief. And the saying of Allah Almighty is the best witness to my view: 'Indeed, Allah does not forgive associating partners with Him, and He forgives what is less than that for whom He wills' [al-Nisa' 116]. And I can interpret al-Nasafi's declaration of disbelief of the Mu'tazila and Khawarij as meaning that he does not intend disbelief in the religion or creed (kufr al-milla), but rather disbelief of the blessing (kufr al-ni'ma), which is the disbelief of disobedience.
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage first presents al-Nasafi's strong stance: he declares the Mu'tazila and Khawarij disbelievers because of their view on the major sinner, citing Quran 12:87 that only disbelievers despair of God's mercy. The author then disagrees with al-Nasafi. He argues that both Mu'tazila and Khawarij possess tasdiq (affirmation), which is the essence of faith according to Ahl al-Sunna, so they cannot be real disbelievers. He offers a charitable interpretation: the Mu'tazila were defending Islam against external philosophies, and the Khawarij were zealous in enforcing Islamic law, though they erred in declaring the Companions disbelievers. He suggests that a future pure generation will reconcile these schools without takfir. Finally, he interprets al-Nasafi's takfir as kufr al-ni'ma (disbelief of blessing, i.e., ingratitude) rather than kufr al-milla (disbelief in the religion), citing Quran 4:116 to show that sins less than shirk may be forgiven. This lesson presents a major objection to Nasafi's position and a conciliatory conclusion.

Key Terms
كفر
يئس من رحمة الله
الإيمان هو التصديق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that anyone who commits a major sin or has a wrong belief is automatically a disbeliever.
Correction: The author shows that even if a group holds erroneous views, as long as they affirm the truth of Islam (tasdiq), they are not true kafirs. Takfir must consider the presence of essential faith.

Checkpoint
What is the basis of the author's disagreement with Nasafi's takfir?
A) Nasafi misinterpreted the Quranic verse.
B) The author believes that both groups have tasdiq (affirmation).
C) The author thinks takfir is never permissible.
D) The author disagrees because Nasafi is not a reliable scholar.

Key Takeaway
The presence of tasdiq (affirmation) prevents takfir, even if one holds erroneous views on major sins.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a Muslim who holds a belief you consider wrong, do you ever feel a judgment rising in your heart against their faith? How does the author's insistence on tasdiq as the core of faith challenge that impulse—and what will you do the next time that judgment surfaces?
?
The author openly disagrees with a respected scholar like al-Nasafi. Is there a scholar or teacher you admire whose view on a certain matter you have blindly accepted without checking it against the principles of Ahl al-Sunna? Are you willing to re-examine that view, even if it means disagreeing?
?
If the essence of faith is inner affirmation, what does that mean for how you treat Muslims who fall into major sins? Does your treatment of them reflect the author's position—that they still have iman—or do you secretly consider them outside the fold? What concrete change will you make in how you speak about or interact with them?
