Imamate Established by Election: Nasafi's Refutation of Nass
Nasafi argues that imamate is by election and refutes textual designation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Nasafi's argument that imamate is established by the election of the just, and why claims of a textual designation (nass) are refuted.

Core Idea
Imamate is a public matter decided by election, not by inheritance or hidden text.

In this passage, Nasafi addresses a core controversy: how is the leader of the Muslim community appointed? He rejects both hereditary claims and secret prophetic designations, grounding the imamate in the choice of the community's righteous members. Let's examine his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two questions
Read the passage and notice that Nasafi answers two questions: 'By whom is the imamate established?' and 'By what means?'. This structure frames his argument.
2
Understand the positive proof for election
Nasafi states imamate is established by the choice of the just and righteous. He argues it is a public affair, so election is the natural method.
This refutes any claim of private designation.
3
Examine the refuted groups
The Rawandiyya claim inheritance or nass for al-Abbas; the Jarudiyya claim a nass for 'Ali by description then inheritance. Note how Nasafi rejects both.
These are the two main alternatives to election.
4
Analyze the historical argument
Nasafi points out that the Companions, despite their piety, chose the imam without opposition. If a nass existed, it would be known and the designated person would have asserted it. Since neither happened, no nass exists.
This is an argument from silence and historical observation.
5
Reflect on 'Ali's role
Nasafi notes 'Ali's bravery and integrity. If there had been a nass for him, he would have defended it for the truth, not ambition. His silence further proves no nass.
This refutes any claim that 'Ali was designated but remained silent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويجيب النسفي عن السؤال التالي: بمن تثبت الإمامة؟ فيقول : تثبت باختيار أهل العدالة والصلاح، خلافا للراوندية القائلين بالوراثة، أو بنص من الرسول على العباس عمه. وخلافا للجارودية القائلين بأن النبي النص على خلافة على بالوصف، ثم ورثها ابنيه، ثم إنها على الميراث في هذين البطنين. ويسأل النسفي: بأي طريق تثبت؟ ويقول : تثبت بالاختيار لأنها من الأمور العامة، ولو كان فيها نص لاشتهر ولكن لم يحدث هذا بدليل أن الصحابة مع جلالة قدرهم، وتمسكهم بالشريعة اختاروا إمامهم، ولم يعارضهم أحد، وكيف يتصور أن يعرض الصحابة عن النص وهم القمة في الجلال والبعد عن الهوى، وقد أمروا بالمعروف ونهوا عن المنكر، وقد اصطفاهم الرسول عليه السلام لصحبته. ثم لو كان النص ثابتا على أحد لادعاه، وأقام الحجة على القوم، وخاصم من لم يتبعه، وحيث لم يحدث ذلك، إذن فلا نص. وعلى شجاع لا تأخذه في الله لومة لائم، ولا يمكن أن يترك الحق لضعف، فهو الموصوف بالصلابة في الدين والبسالة، فلو كان هناك نص عليه لدافع عن ذلك تنفيذا للنص ، لا طمعا في الإمامة، لكنه لم يحدث.
And al-Nasafi answers the following question: By whom is the imamate established? He says: It is established by the choice of the people of justice and righteousness, contrary to the Rawandiyya who say by inheritance, or by a text (nass) from the Messenger concerning al-Abbas his uncle. And contrary to the Jarudiyya who say that the Prophet made a textual designation for the caliphate of 'Ali by description, then it was inherited by his two sons, then it is on inheritance in these two lineages. And al-Nasafi asks: By what means is it established? And he says: It is established by choice because it is among public affairs, and if there had been a text concerning it, it would have become widely known, but this did not happen, as evidence that the Companions, despite their great status and adherence to the Shari'a, chose their imam, and no one opposed them. And how can it be imagined that the Companions would turn away from a text while they are the pinnacle of dignity and distance from whims, and they enjoined good and forbade evil, and the Messenger chose them for his companionship? Then, if a text had been fixed upon someone, he would have claimed it and established the proof against the people and disputed with those who did not follow him. And since that did not happen, then there is no text. And 'Ali was brave, not fearing blame in Allah, and could not abandon truth due to weakness; he was described as firm in religion and valorous. So if there had been a text upon him, he would have defended that in implementation of the text, not out of desire for the imamate, but it did not happen.
Source pages p. 118

Commentary
This passage is an argumentative response on how the imamate is established. Nasafi first answers the question 'By whom is it established?' He states it is by the choice of the just and righteous people, rejecting the Rawandiyya who claim inheritance or nass for al-Abbas, and the Jarudiyya who claim a nass by description for 'Ali followed by inheritance in his lineage. Then he addresses the means: it is established by election (ikhtiyar) because imamate is a public matter; if there were a nass, it would be widely known. He points to the historical fact that the Companions, despite their integrity, elected their imam without opposition. He argues it is inconceivable that they would ignore a clear text. He further argues that if a nass existed for a specific person, that person would have asserted it and argued against dissenters. Since that did not happen, there is no nass. He specifically mentions 'Ali's courage and righteousness: had there been a nass for him, he would have defended it, but he did not. This refutes both the idea of inheritance and the idea of explicit designation.

Key Terms
اختيار
نص
راوندية
جارودية
إمامة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some believe that the imamate must be established by an explicit divine text (nass) designating a specific person.
Correction: Nasafi shows that such a text would have been widely known and claimed by the designated person; the absence of such claims from the Prophet's companions, especially 'Ali, proves no nass exists. Therefore, imamate is established by election.

Checkpoint
What evidence does Nasafi give to refute the claim of a textual designation (nass) for imamate?
A) The Qur'an explicitly prohibits nass
B) The Companions would not have ignored a nass, and no claimant came forward
C) The Ash'aris deny nass
D) The Ummah has consensus against nass

Key Takeaway
Nasafi shows that imamate is established by the election of the just, and the absence of any historical claim of nass proves no such designation existed.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Nasafi argues that if a text existed, it would be publicly known and claimed. Think of a belief you hold that you base on 'hidden knowledge' or private revelation rather than public evidence. Is that belief truly from Allah, or is it your own desire? How would you defend it if challenged like Nasafi challenges the nass?
?
The Companions chose their leader without a text, relying on consultation and justice. Are there areas in your life where you expect God to send you a clear sign instead of using the intellect and community He gave you? What decision are you postponing because you're waiting for a 'text'?
?
Nasafi says Ali would have defended a nass if it existed—he was brave and not afraid of blame. Ask yourself: If you knew the truth and were certain of it, would you have the courage to speak up, even if it brought criticism? Where is your silence a proof of what you don't believe?

How an Imam is Chosen: Ash'ari's View
Nasafi states al-Ash'ari's method for electing an imam.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari position that a single qualified mujtahid can elect an imam without requiring consensus, and why this was the Companions' practice.

Core Idea
Imamate can be contracted by one qualified person; consensus is not required.

Nasafi sets out al-Ash'ari's methodology for selecting a new imam at the death of the previous one. He explains that a single mujtahid, after confirming suitability, can contract the imamate, and no further consensus is needed. This is grounded in the Companions' own urgency to establish leadership without delay.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on Ash'ari's view
Read the Arabic passage describing al-Ash'ari's opinion. Focus on who performs the ijtihad and the role of consensus.
The passage contrasts with the need for ijma'.
2
Understand the reasoning
Nasafi justifies the Ash'ari view by citing the Companions' practice: they prioritized the contract (عقد) over consensus (إجماع) to avoid delay in an urgent matter.
The imamate is a necessity for the community.
3
Consider the implication
Reflect on why this view might be controversial. What does it say about the role of the community in leadership selection?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويتحدث النسفي طويلا، عارضا كل ما يمكن أن يعترض به على كلامه، ويبرر إسهابه في هذا الموضوع قائلا: "إن العصر الذي يعيش فيه ينتشر فيه الإلحاد، والقوم لا يطعنون في الإمامة فقط بل في كل أركان الدين." ثم بعد ذلك يتحدث عن الكيفية التي يتم بها اختيار الإمام فيقول : إن الأشعري يرى أن واحدا من أهل الاجتهاد لو اجتهد في زمان موت إمام، ورأى أسباب الصلاحية في أحد ،ثابتة، فعقد له الإمامة انعقدت له، وليس لغيره بعد ذلك أن يعارضه ولا حاجة للإجماع، لما فيه من تأخير أمر هام، تحتاج المجتمعات إليه وهو الإمامة كما أن الصحابة - رضوان الله عليهم - قالوا بوجوب العقد أولا ثم يأتي بعد ذلك دور الإجماع. الكلام في إمامة أبي بكر الصديق : ثم يتحدث عن أول خليفة للمسلمين بعد الرسول عليه السلام فيقول: إن أبا بكر بعد رسول الله عليه السلام كان إماما حقا تجب له الطاعة، وكانت كل الأدلة تؤكد أنه الخليفة الأول للرسول، فقد استجمع شرائط صحة الخلافة، استجماعه لها فقد مع عة من الخلافات بعد وفاته ، كان الفضل الأكبر في فضها لأبي بكر، حدثت مجمو[؟] وهذه[؟] يؤكد أنه خليفة الرسول . ويذكر النسفي منها قوله : من كان يعبد محمداً، فإن محمداً قد مات... بعد إنكار عمر لذلك ومنها اختلاف القوم في مكان الدفن، وقول أبي بكر ، روايةً عن الرسول عليه السلام: "الأنبياء يدفنون حيث يقبضون". ومنها - اختلافهم في إنفاذ جيش أسامة ، لكن أبا بكر أخرج الجيش.
And al-Nasafi speaks at length, presenting every possible objection to his speech, and justifies his elaboration on this topic saying: 'The era in which he lives is spreading atheism, and the people do not attack only the imamate but all the pillars of religion.' Then after that he speaks about the manner in which the imam is chosen and says: Indeed al-Ash'ari holds that if one of the people of ijtihad makes ijtihad at the time of the death of an imam and sees the causes of suitability in someone as established, and then he contracts the imamate for him, it is contracted for him, and no one else after that may oppose him, and there is no need for consensus (ijma'), because it would delay an important matter that communities need – namely the imamate – just as the Companions said that the contract is obligatory first, then the role of consensus comes after. The discussion on the imamate of Abu Bakr al-Siddiq: Then he speaks about the first caliph of the Muslims after the Messenger and says: Indeed Abu Bakr after the Messenger of Allah was a true imam to whom obedience is obligatory, and all the evidence confirmed that he is the first caliph of the Messenger. He had gathered the conditions for the validity of the caliphate. [After his death] there occurred a group of disputes, and the greatest credit for resolving them belonged to Abu Bakr. [This] confirms that he is the caliph of the Messenger. And al-Nasafi mentions among them his saying: 'Whoever used to worship Muhammad, indeed Muhammad has died...' after 'Umar's denial of that, and among them the people's disagreement over the place of burial, and Abu Bakr's statement, by narration from the Messenger: 'The prophets are buried where they die.' And among them – their disagreement over dispatching the army of Usama, but Abu Bakr dispatched the army.
Source pages p. 119

Commentary
This passage covers two topics. First, Nasafi explains the method of selecting an imam according to al-Ash'ari: a single qualified mujtahid can contract the imamate for a suitable person at the time of the previous imam's death, without needing consensus, to avoid delay. He compares this to the Companions' practice of prioritizing the contract over consensus. Second, he discusses the imamate of Abu Bakr, presenting him as the rightful first caliph. He states that Abu Bakr met all conditions of valid caliphate and that his leadership resolved disputes after the Prophet's death. He gives three specific proofs: his famous statement 'Whoever worshipped Muhammad...' that settled the crisis over the Prophet's death; his knowledge that prophets are buried where they die, resolving the burial location; and his insistence on dispatching Usama's army despite opposition from some Companions. These demonstrate Abu Bakr's authority and knowledge.

Key Terms
اجتهاد
إجماع
عقد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the entire community must agree on the imam before he can lead.
Correction: Nasafi explains that according to al-Ash'ari, a single qualified mujtahid can elect the imam, and consensus follows later, as with Abu Bakr.

Checkpoint
According to al-Ash'ari in this passage, who can contract the imamate?
A) The entire community
B) A single qualified mujtahid
C) A council of scholars
D) The outgoing imam

Key Takeaway
A single qualified mujtahid can validly contract the imamate without prior consensus, following the Companions' practice.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When faced with an important decision, do you tend to wait for everyone else to agree before acting, even when the right path is clear? How does the urgency of the imamate in this lesson expose your own reluctance to take decisive action? What is one area of your life where you will commit to acting on your conviction today instead of waiting for consensus?
?
The Companions prioritized the contract over consensus. In your relationships, what 'contract'—a commitment, promise, or duty—have you been postponing because you fear others might not back you? What is stopping you from taking that step now?
?
Al-Ash'ari grants a single qualified person the authority to appoint an imam. Do you trust your own judgment when you see a genuine need in your community, or do you hide behind group opinion? How can you cultivate the courage to lead decisively in a situation that requires immediate action?

Proofs of Abu Bakr's Rightful Caliphate
Nasafi lists three proofs for Abu Bakr's caliphate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will know three specific proofs Nasafi uses to establish Abu Bakr's authority as the first caliph.

Core Idea
Abu Bakr's knowledge and decisive actions prove his rightful caliphate.

After the Prophet's death, disputes arose. Nasafi presents three events where Abu Bakr's knowledge and leadership resolved critical issues: his statement on the Prophet's mortality, his knowledge of prophetic burial, and his insistence on dispatching Usama's army. These demonstrate his rightful authority.

Guided Path
1
Read the proofs for Abu Bakr
Read the Arabic passage that lists the three proofs of Abu Bakr's caliphate. Identify each one.
The proofs are narrative events.
2
Examine the first proof
When the Prophet died, some were in denial. Abu Bakr recited: 'Whoever worshipped Muhammad, Muhammad is dead...' This showed his knowledge and steadied the community.
This is after 'Umar's initial denial.
3
Examine the second proof
Companions disputed where to bury the Prophet. Abu Bakr narrated the hadith: 'Prophets are buried where they die.' This resolved the issue.
4
Examine the third proof
Some opposed sending Usama's army after the Prophet's death. Abu Bakr insisted and dispatched it, showing his commitment to the Prophet's command.

Ash'ari View on Selecting an Imam
Nasafi reports the Ash'ari position that one qualified person can contract imamate without consensus.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari position on how an imam is selected and the role of a single qualified person (mujtahid) in contracting the imamate.

Core Idea
One qualified person can contract the imamate; consensus comes after.

This lesson presents the Ash'ari view on the method of selecting an imam. Nasafi reports that according to al-Ash'ari, a single qualified person (mujtahid) can contract the imamate for a suitable candidate without requiring consensus. The reasoning is practical: delaying imamate would harm the community. The Companions also prioritized the initial contract over consensus.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once and then the translation. Notice how al-Ash'ari's position is presented: a single mujtahid, at the death of an imam, can contract the imamate for a suitable person.
2
Identify the key elements
Look for the qualified person (mujtahid), the conditions for contracting, and the rejection of the need for consensus. Note the reason given: delay would harm the community.
3
Reflect on the analogy with the Companions
The passage ends by comparing this view to the practice of the Companions, who insisted on the contract first and then consensus. Think about how this supports al-Ash'ari's argument.
This is not a historical claim but a doctrinal precedent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إن الأشعري يرى أن واحدا من أهل الاجتهاد لو اجتهد في زمان موت إمام، ورأى أسباب الصلاحية في أحد،ثابتة، فعقد له الإمامة انعقدت له، وليس لغيره بعد ذلك أن يعارضه ولا حاجة للإجماع، لما فيه من تأخير أمر هام، تحتاج المجتمعات إليه وهو الإمامة كما أن الصحابة - رضوان الله عليهم - قالوا بوجوب العقد أولا ثم يأتي بعد ذلك دور الإجماع.
Al-Ash'ari holds that if one of the people of independent judgment (ijtihad) exerts effort at the time of an imam's death and sees the conditions of suitability in someone, established, then contracts the imamate for him, it is contracted, and no one else may oppose him after that; there is no need for consensus (ijma'), because that would delay an important matter which societies need, namely the imamate, just as the Companions—may God be pleased with them—said that the contract is obligatory first, then after that comes the role of consensus.
Source pages p. 119

Commentary
This passage reports the Ash'ari view on the method of selecting an imam. It is a legal-doctrinal position within the broader discussion of imamate (leadership). The author states that according to al-Ash'ari, a single qualified person (mujtahid) can, upon the death of the previous imam, assess the suitability of a candidate and formally contract the imamate for him. Once this contract is made, it is binding and no one else may object; there is no requirement for the consensus of all qualified electors. The reasoning is that requiring consensus would cause undue delay in an urgent matter that the community needs—the imamate. The author then notes that this is analogous to the practice of the Companions, who first insisted on the contract (bay'ah) and only later considered consensus. Thus, the Ash'ari position is presented as pragmatic and rooted in the precedent of the early community.

Key Terms
الإمامة
أهل الاجتهاد
الإجماع
العقد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Ash'ari view disregards consensus entirely.
Correction: The Ash'ari view does not reject consensus; it prioritizes the initial contract by a qualified person and allows consensus to follow later, as seen in the analogy with the Companions.

Checkpoint
According to the Ash'ari view reported by Nasafi, who can contract the imamate for a new imam?
A) The entire community
B) A single qualified person (mujtahid)
C) Only the outgoing imam
D) The majority of the people of binding and loosing

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari position allows a single qualified person to contract the imamate, prioritizing urgent leadership over delayed consensus.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see an urgent need in your community, do you wait for unanimous agreement before acting, or do you take initiative? How does al-Ash'ari's prioritization of the contract challenge your own tendency to delay?
?
Think of a time you withheld support from a capable leader because not everyone agreed. Could the urgency of the imamate here cause you to reconsider? What would you do differently now?
?
How do you balance speed and collective input in your own decisions? What is one area of your life where you can apply the principle of acting decisively when the conditions are right, trusting that consensus can follow?

Evidences for Abu Bakr's Caliphate
Nasafi lists actions and a Qur'anic verse as evidence for Abu Bakr's rightful caliphate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the key evidence cited by Imam Nasafi for the caliphate of Abu Bakr, including his actions, a Qur'anic verse, and consensus.

Core Idea
Abu Bakr's caliphate is proven by evidence that combines action, scripture, and community consensus.

Imam Nasafi presents a range of arguments affirming that Abu Bakr was the rightful first caliph. These include his calm response to the Prophet's death, his knowledge of prophetic burial, his decisive sending of Usama's army, his firm stance on zakat, a supporting Quranic verse, and the eventual consensus. This lesson explores each piece of evidence and its significance.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Nasafi uses a cumulative style, listing several pieces of evidence.
2
Identify each piece of evidence
The passage enumerates several distinct proofs: the statement about the Prophet's death, the burial tradition, the dispatch of Usama's army, the stance on zakat, the Quranic verse, and the consensus. List them in order.
3
Reflect on the role of consensus
Consider why Nasafi ends with consensus (ijma'). Consensus is a strong proof in Sunni theology. Think about how it ties the other evidences together.
Consensus represents the community's agreement, validating the caliphate.
4
Test your understanding
Answer the checkpoint question to check if you can distinguish the evidences mentioned from those not mentioned.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم يتحدث عن أول خليفة للمسلمين بعد الرسول عليه السلام فيقول: إن أبا بكر بعد رسول الله عليه السلام كان إماما حقا تجب له الطاعة، وكانت كل الأدلة تؤكد أنه الخليفة الأول للرسول، فقد استجمع شرائط صحة الخلافة، استجماعه لها فقد مع كثرة من الخلافات بعد وفاته، كان الفضل الأكبر في فضها لأبي بكر، ويذكر النسفي منها قوله: من كان يعبد محمداً، فإن محمداً قد مات... بعد إنكار عمر لذلك ومنها اختلاف القوم في مكان الدفن، وقول أبي بكر، روايةً عن الرسول عليه السلام: "الأنبياء يدفنون حيث يقبضون". ومنها - اختلافهم في إنفاذ جيش أسامة، لكن أبا بكر أخرج الجيش. ومنها اختلافهم في الصدقات وقوله: والله لو منعوني عناقا كانوا يدفعونه لرسول الله لقاتلتهم عليه. ويذكر النسفي الدليل من القرآن في قوله تعالى: {قُل لِّلْمُخَلَّفِينَ مِنَ الْأَعْرَابِ سَتُدْعَوْنَ إِلَى قَوْمٍ أُولِي بَأْسٍ شَدِيدٍ تُقَاتِلُونَهُمْ أَوْ يُسْلِمُونَ} [الفتح: ١٦]. ثم يذكر أن الإجماع انعقد على خلافة أبي بكر، وهو من الأدلة القوية على صحة خلافته.
Then he discusses the first caliph of the Muslims after the Messenger—peace be upon him—saying: Abu Bakr after the Messenger of God—peace be upon him—was a rightful imam whose obedience was obligatory, and all evidence confirmed that he was the first caliph of the Messenger. He had gathered the conditions for the validity of the caliphate; his gathering of them, along with the many disputes after his death, the greatest credit for resolving them goes to Abu Bakr. Al-Nasafi mentions among them: his saying: 'Whoever worshipped Muhammad, Muhammad has died...' after Umar's denial of that, and among them: the people's disagreement over the burial place, and Abu Bakr's statement—narrated from the Messenger—peace be upon him: 'The prophets are buried where they die.' And among them: their disagreement over dispatching the army of Usama, but Abu Bakr sent out the army. And among them: their disagreement over the charity (zakat) and his saying: 'By God, if they withheld from me a young she-goat that they used to give to the Messenger of God, I would fight them for it.' Al-Nasafi mentions the proof from the Qur'an in His saying: {Say to those who remained behind of the Bedouins, 'You will be called against a people of severe might; you will fight them or they will submit'} [al-Fath: 16]. Then he mentions that consensus (ijma') was established on Abu Bakr's caliphate, which is among the strong evidence for the validity of his caliphate.
Source pages p. 119

Commentary
This passage presents the evidence for Abu Bakr's rightful caliphate. It is a proof-based argument (istidlal). The author first affirms that Abu Bakr was a rightful imam with obligatory obedience. Then he enumerates several pieces of evidence: (1) His famous statement at the death of the Prophet to calm the people; (2) His knowledge of the burial place of prophets; (3) His resolve to send Usama's army despite opposition; (4) His firm stance on zakat, threatening to fight those who withheld it—this demonstrates his commitment to the religion's obligations. (5) A Qur'anic verse (al-Fath 48:16) is cited as indicating jihad against those who oppose, which supports his actions. (6) Finally, the establishment of consensus (ijma') on his caliphate is presented as a strong proof. The author structures the evidence as both rational and textual, showing that Abu Bakr's actions and the community's agreement validate his leadership.

Key Terms
خلافة
أبو بكر
إجماع
الصدقات
جيش أسامة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The evidence for Abu Bakr's caliphate is only based on force and coercion.
Correction: Nasafi includes Prophetic tradition (burial, statement), decisive action (army, zakat), a Quranic verse, and consensus, showing a multi-faceted proof.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT mentioned as evidence for Abu Bakr's caliphate in this passage?
A) His statement about the death of the Prophet
B) The consensus (ijma') of the community
C) The Qur'anic verse about jihad
D) His lineage from Quraysh

Key Takeaway
The caliphate of Abu Bakr is supported by multiple forms of evidence, including prophetic tradition, decisive action, Quranic support, and consensus.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a dispute in your community—whether about leadership or a religious matter—do you actively seek out evidence from the Quran, Sunnah, and consensus, or do you rely on your own opinion? What would Abu Bakr's example on using multiple proofs teach you about being thorough?
?
The consensus of the companions was a decisive proof for Abu Bakr's caliphate. Are you connected to the mainstream Muslim community today, or do you distance yourself from the collective? What step can you take this week to strengthen your bond with the Ummah?
?
Abu Bakr refused to compromise on the obligation of zakat, even at the cost of war. Is there an Islamic obligation you are neglecting or compromising on because of fear of people's reactions? What would it look like for you to be as firm as Abu Bakr in upholding that command?

Refutation of Objections to Abu Bakr's Imamate
Nasafi enumerates objections and announces their refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the main objections raised against Abu Bakr's caliphate and how the author prepares to refute them by listing them and citing prophetic and historical evidence.

Core Idea
Objections to a caliph's legitimacy are answered by referring to prophetic testimony and historical facts.

In this passage, the Maturidi theologian Nasafi sets the stage for defending Abu Bakr's caliphate by listing the most common criticisms made by Shia and other groups. By naming these objections, he prepares his reader to see the counter-arguments that follow, grounding the discussion in scripture (hadith) and early Islamic history.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objections
Read the passage and note each objection mentioned. How many are there?
Look for phrases starting with 'منها'.
2
Understand the two prophetic hadiths
The two hadiths—'Man kuntu mawlahu' and the Harun-Musa analogy—are key Shia proofs. Understand why they are used as objections.
3
Recognize the refutation strategy
The author states he refutes these objections. Notice that he also brings positive evidence about Abu Bakr's qualities.
The refutation itself is not in this passage; it's a preview.
4
Reflect on the role of Fadak and share
The objections about Fadak and the family share relate to Abu Bakr's economic decisions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم يناقش مجموعة من الاعتراضات منها: أن عليا توقف سنة أو أربعة أشهر، ومنها : إن عليا سارع إلى الإجماع تقيَّة، ومنها أن خلافا من البعض حدث ولم يشتهر، ومنها قوله عليه السلام: "من كنت مولاه فعلي مولاه". ومنها قوله عليه السلام لعلي: "أما ترضى أن تكون مني بمنزلة هارون من موسى إلا أنه لا نبي بعد". ويرد على هذه الاعتراضات بما يدحضها، ويثبت صحة خلافة الصديق له، وفي هذا المقام يتحدث طويلا عن صفات أبي بكر، وعما قاله الرسول عليه السلام في حقه. ثم يناقش اعتراضات الروافض بأخذه فَدَكَ من فاطمة، وبإلغائه سهم ذوي القربى، ويتحدث عن الفتوحات التي تمت في عصر الخليفة الأول.
Then he discusses a set of objections, among them: that Ali delayed for a year or four months, and among them: that Ali rushed to consensus under duress (taqiyya), and among them: that some opposition occurred but was not widely known, and among them: his saying—peace be upon him: 'Whoever I am his master (mawla), Ali is his master.' And among them: his saying—peace be upon him to Ali: 'Are you not pleased that you are to me as Harun was to Musa, except that there is no prophet after me?' And he refutes these objections with what disproves them, and establishes the validity of the caliphate of al-Siddiq (Abu Bakr). On this occasion, he speaks at length about the qualities of Abu Bakr and what the Messenger—peace be upon him—said about him. Then he discusses the objections of the Rawafid (rejectionists) regarding his taking of Fadak from Fatima and his abolition of the share for near relatives, and he speaks about the conquests that took place during the first caliph's era.
Source pages p. 119

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer section. The author, Nasafi, lists five specific objections raised against Abu Bakr's caliphate. The objections are: (1) Ali allegedly delayed pledging allegiance for a year or four months; (2) Ali's pledge was under coercion (taqiyya); (3) Some opposition occurred but was not publicly known; (4) The hadith 'Man kuntu mawlahu fa-'Ali mawlahu' (of Ghadir Khumm) is used to argue Ali's priority; (5) The hadith of position ('You are to me as Harun was to Musa') implying Ali should succeed. The author then states that he refutes these objections with arguments that disprove them, thereby establishing the validity of Abu Bakr's caliphate. He also mentions that he discusses Abu Bakr's qualities and Prophetic praise, and then addresses the Rafidi objections concerning Fadak (the land that Abu Bakr took from Fatima) and the abolition of the share for near relatives (which was a change in distribution). Finally, he mentions the conquests of Abu Bakr's time. The passage serves to preempt and dismiss sectarian arguments against the first caliph.

The Three Proofs of Umar's Caliphate
Nasafi affirms Umar's caliphate with three supports

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the threefold basis for affirming Umar ibn al-Khattab's caliphate according to al-Nasafi.

Core Idea
Umar's caliphate is proven by conditions, Prophetic praise, and conquests.

Al-Nasafi presents three pillars that establish Umar's rightful place as the second caliph: his fulfillment of leadership conditions, the Prophet's own commendations, and the immense conquests achieved under his rule. This passage grounds the legitimacy of his imamate in both textual and historical evidence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once and its translation. Notice that Nasafi uses three distinct supporting points.
The passage is a summary of a longer discussion.
2

3
Understand the purpose
Each element serves to legitimize Umar's caliphate. Conditions show qualification, hadiths show divine/grand approval, conquests show historical success.
These three together form a comprehensive proof.
4
Reflect on the misconception
Consider the common misunderstanding that Umar's caliphate was merely political power. The passage refutes this by providing multiple layers of evidence.

The author says he details these conditions and also cites hadiths praising Umar, and then mentions the military conquests achieved during Umar's reign. This serves to legitimize Umar's succession as part of the established orthodox caliphate. The passage is summary-like, preparing for a more detailed discussion presumably following in the text.

Imamate of Uthman ibn Affan
Nasafi explains Uthman's qualification and the process of his election through Abd al-Rahman ibn Awf and consensus.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi argues for the legitimacy of Uthman's caliphate based on his qualifications, the role of Abd al-Rahman ibn Awf, and the support of consensus.

Core Idea
Uthman's caliphate was legitimate because a qualified elector from the shura chose him, and the community agreed.

Nasafi defends the legitimacy of Uthman's imamate by pointing to two key factors: Uthman's personal qualifications and the proper procedure of his election. The passage explains that all conditions for imamate were met in Uthman, and that Abd al-Rahman ibn Awf—a respected member of the shura council appointed by Umar—formally contracted the caliphate for him. This was then ratified by the consensus of the community, making Uthman's leadership both qualified and consensual.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and English carefully to get the gist of Nasafi's argument.
2
Identify the two pillars of legitimacy
Notice how Nasafi first mentions Uthman's personal qualifications (شرائط الإمامة), then the process: appointment by a qualified person (عبد الرحمن بن عوف) and the resulting consensus (إجماع).
3
Reflect on why this matters in kalam
Think about how this argument counters accusations of illegitimacy by grounding Uthman's caliphate in both individual merit and collective approval.
This connects to the broader Sunni defense of the first four caliphs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكلام في خلافة عثمان : ويتحدث الإمام النسفي عن خلافة عثمان ، فيبين أن جميع الشروط الخاصة بالإمامة، توفرت فيه، ثم إن عبد الرحمن بن عوف، وهو أهل للعقد قد اشتغل بالعقد له، ولأنه أحد أهل الشورى، وقد انضم إلى موقف عبد الرحمن إجماع
Discussion on the caliphate of Uthman: Imam al-Nasafi speaks about the caliphate of Uthman, and he explains that all the conditions specific to the imamate were fulfilled in him; then, indeed, Abd al-Rahman ibn Awf—he being one qualified for the contract—undertook the contract for him, and because he was one of the people of the shura (consultative council), and the consensus (ijma') joined the position of Abd al-Rahman.
Source pages p. 120

Commentary
This passage presents the legitimacy of Uthman's caliphate. It is a proof based on the fulfillment of conditions and a proper selection process. The author states that Uthman possessed all the necessary qualities for the imamate. Then he highlights that Abd al-Rahman ibn Awf, who was qualified to contract the imamate, took on the responsibility of selecting Uthman. Since Abd al-Rahman was a member of the shura (the council appointed by Umar), his decision carried weight. Finally, the passage notes that consensus (ijma') aligned with Abd al-Rahman's choice, thereby solidifying Uthman's legitimacy. The argument follows the Ash'ari framework where a single qualified person can contract, and consensus confirms.

Nasafi's Defense of Abu Bakr's Caliphate
Nasafi enumerates evidence and Shia objections for Abu Bakr's caliphate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the key evidences Nasafi uses to support Abu Bakr's caliphate and the main Shia objections he addresses.

Core Idea
Consensus and Quranic proof validate Abu Bakr's caliphate against Shia objections.

This passage is a concise summary of Nasafi's argument for Abu Bakr's caliphate. It presents the primary evidence—the Prophet's hadith, a Quranic verse, and the consensus of the Companions—and then lists five common Shia objections that Nasafi refutes. Understanding this summary gives you the framework of the classical Sunni defense.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage for overview
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get a sense of the flow: evidence first, then objections, then additional issues.
Notice that the evidence is presented briefly, while the objections are enumerated.
2
Identify the three evidences
Find the three pieces of evidence: the hadith about fighting over sadaqat, the Quran verse from Surah al-Fath, and the consensus (ijma). Reflect on why each is considered strong.
The hadith shows Abu Bakr's resolve; the Quran implies continued jihad after the Prophet; consensus is a communal proof.
3
List the five Shia objections
The passage lists five objections: Ali's delay, taqiyya, minor opposition, hadith of mawla, hadith of manzila. Write them down in order.
These are the standard Shia arguments that Nasafi refutes in detail elsewhere.
4
Reflect on the Fadak and share issue
The passage mentions objections about Fadak and the abolition of the share of relatives. Consider why these were controversial.
These relate to financial policies of Abu Bakr that Shia criticize.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها اختلافهم في الصدقات وقوله لله والله لو منعونی عناقا كانوا يدفعونه لرسول الله لقاتلتهم عليه). ويذكر النسفي الدليل من القرآن في قوله تعالى: ﴿ قُل لِّلْمُخَلَّفِينَ مِنَ الْأَعْرَابِ ستُدْعَوْنَ إِلَى قَوْمٍ أُولِي بَأْسٍ شَدِيدٍ تُقَتَتِلُونَهُمْ أَوْ يُسْلِمُونَ ﴾ [الفتح: ١٦]. ثم يذكر أن الإجماع انعقد على خلافة أبي بكر، وهو من الأدلة القوية على صحة خلافته، ثم يناقش مجموعة من الاعتراضات منها: أن عليا توقف سنة أو أربعة أشهر، ومنها : إن عليا سارع إلى الإجماع تقيَّة، ومنها أن خلافا من البعض حدث ولم يشتهر ، ومنها قوله عليه السلام: "من كنت مولاه فعلي مولاه". ومنها قوله عليه السلام لعلي أما" ترضى أن تكون مني بمنزلة هارون من موسى إلا أنه لا نبي بعد". ويرد على هذه الاعتراضات بما يدحضها، ويثبت صحة خلافة الصديق له، وفي هذا المقام يتحدث طويلا عن صفات أبي بكر، وعما قاله الرسول عليه السلام في حقه. ثم يناقش اعتراضات الروافض بأخذه فَدَكَ من فاطمة، وبإلغائه سهم ذوي القربى، ويتحدث عن الفتوحات التي تمت في عصر الخليفة الأول.
And among them is their disagreement about the charities and his saying: 'By Allah, if they withhold from me a young goat which they used to give to the Messenger of Allah, I would fight them for it.' And al-Nasafi mentions the evidence from the Quran in His statement: 'Say to those who remained behind of the Bedouins, "You will be called to a people of severe might; you will fight them or they will submit."' [Al-Fath:16]. Then he mentions that consensus was concluded on the caliphate of Abu Bakr, and it is among the strong evidences for the correctness of his caliphate. Then he discusses a group of objections, including: that Ali delayed for a year or four months; and that Ali hastened to the consensus out of dissimulation (taqiyya); and that opposition occurred from some and did not become famous; and his saying (peace be upon him): 'Whoever I am his mawla, Ali is his mawla'; and his saying (peace be upon him) to Ali: 'Are you not pleased to be to me in the position of Aaron to Moses, except that there is no prophet after me?' And he responds to these objections with what refutes them, and establishes the correctness of the caliphate of al-Siddiq. And in this context he speaks at length about the qualities of Abu Bakr and what the Messenger (peace be upon him) said about him. Then he discusses the objections of the Rafidah (Shia) regarding his taking of Fadak from Fatimah and his abolition of the share of the relatives (dhawi al-qurba), and he speaks about the conquests that occurred during the era of the first caliph.
Source pages p. 120

Commentary
This passage is a summary of Nasafi's defense of Abu Bakr's caliphate. It begins by mentioning one source of disagreement (the issue of sadaqat) and cites the Prophet's hadith about fighting those who withhold it, which Abu Bakr used. Then he provides Quranic evidence from Surah al-Fath, which calls the Bedouins to fight a people of severe might, implying that jihad continues after the Prophet. He states that ijma (consensus) was achieved on Abu Bakr's caliphate, which is a strong proof. Then he lists five Shia objections: (1) Ali delayed his pledge for a year or four months; (2) Ali's pledge was out of taqiyya (fear); (3) some opposition existed but was not widespread; (4) the hadith of Ghadir (man kuntu mawlahu); (5) the hadith of position (manzila). Nasafi refutes these without detailing the refutations in this summary. He then mentions that he discusses Abu Bakr's virtues and the Prophet's praise. Finally, he addresses Shia objections regarding Fadak (the land taken from Fatimah) and the abolition of the share of relatives, and mentions the conquests of Abu Bakr's era.

Nasafi refutes the objection that Ali acted out of fear (taqiyya). Have you ever hidden your faith or compromised your beliefs due to social pressure or fear? How can you cultivate the courage of Abu Bakr, who stood firm even when others opposed him?
?

Uthman's Valid Caliphate
Nasafi affirms Uthman's qualifications and the consensus supporting his caliphate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Uthman's caliphate was legitimate based on his qualifications, the contract by a qualified person, and the consensus of the Companions.

Core Idea
Uthman's caliphate is validated by consensus, which yields certain knowledge.

Nasafi begins by affirming that Uthman possessed all necessary conditions for the imamate. He then highlights the role of Abd al-Rahman ibn Awf, a qualified member of the shura, in contracting the caliphate. Crucially, the consensus of the Companions joined this decision, and consensus is a definitive proof in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditions for imamate
Read the passage and note that Nasafi states all conditions were fulfilled in Uthman. Consider what those conditions might be from kalam tradition.
2
Understand the role of shura and 'Abd al-Rahman
The passage mentions 'Abd al-Rahman ibn 'Awf as qualified for the contract and being part of the shura. Recognize that his involvement adds legitimacy.
3
Grasp the authority of consensus
Nasafi emphasizes that the consensus (ijma) of the Companions joined 'Abd al-Rahman's position, and that consensus is a proof leading to certain knowledge. This is a key theological principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكلام في خلافة عثمان : ويتحدث الإمام النسفي عن خلافة عثمان ، فيبين أن جميع الشروط الخاصة بالإمامة، توفرت فيه، ثم إن عبد الرحمن بن عوف، وهو أهل للعقد قد اشتغل بالعقد له، ولأنه أحد أهل الشورى، وقد انضم إلى موقف عبد الرحمن إجماع الصحابة عليه، والإجماع دليل موجب للعلم. كما يتحدث عن المطاعن التي وجهت لعثمان ، ومنها : أنه عفا عن عبد الله ابن عمر الذي قتل الهرمزان ومنها : أنه رد الحكم بن العاص طريد رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم ويناقش كل المطاعن ليردها، ثم يذكر أن عثمان من الذين بشرهم الرسول بالجنة وأن الرسول عليه السلام قال فيه : عثمان أخي ورفيقي بالجنة"، والحديث رواه الإمام أحمد في مسنده ۱۲ من حديث زيد بن أسلم عن أبيه. وقوله عليه السلام: "ألا" أستحي ممن تستحي منه ملائكة السماء" والحديث صحيح رواه مسلم في صحيحه جـ ١٥ ص ١٦٨ من حديث عائشة - رضي - الله عنها. ثم يتحدث النسفي بعد ذلك عن سبب قتل عثمان، ويبين أنه قتل ظلما وعدوانا، وأن كل ما ادعاه الأعداء لا أساس له ، لأن الصحابة هم الذين ولوه على أمورهم، وهم الذين أعانوه على كل تصرفاته، ولا يعقل أن يولوه وهو ظالم، وأن يسكتوا على الظلم وهم الذي أمر بالمعروف ونهوا عن المنكر.
Discussion on the caliphate of Uthman: Imam al-Nasafi speaks about the caliphate of Uthman, explaining that all the conditions specific to the imamate were fulfilled in him. Then, 'Abd al-Rahman ibn 'Awf, who is qualified for the contract (al-‘aqd), engaged in contracting it for him, and because he was one of the people of the shura (consultation), and the consensus of the Companions joined the position of 'Abd al-Rahman, and consensus is a proof that necessitates certain knowledge. He also speaks about the accusations (al-matā‘in) that were directed at Uthman, including: that he pardoned 'Abdullah ibn 'Umar who killed al-Hurmuzan; and that he returned al-Hakam ibn al-‘As, the exile of the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him). He discusses all the accusations to refute them. Then he mentions that Uthman is among those whom the Messenger gave glad tidings of Paradise, and that the Messenger (peace be upon him) said about him: 'Uthman is my brother and my companion in Paradise' – this hadith is narrated by Imam Ahmad in his Musnad from Zayd ibn Aslam from his father. And his saying (peace be upon him): 'Should I not feel shy of one from whom the angels of heaven feel shy?' – this hadith is authentic, narrated by Muslim in his Sahih from 'A'ishah. Then al-Nasafi speaks after that about the reason for the murder of Uthman, and explains that he was killed unjustly and aggressively, and that all that the enemies claimed has no basis, because the Companions are the ones who appointed him over their affairs and they assisted him in all his actions, and it is not reasonable that they would appoint him while he was unjust and remain silent about injustice while they are the ones who enjoined good and forbade evil.
Source pages p. 121

Commentary
This passage is a defense of Uthman's caliphate. Nasafi begins by stating that Uthman fulfilled all conditions of imamate. He then highlights that the selection was carried out by 'Abd al-Rahman ibn 'Awf, who was qualified, and that the consensus of the Companions joined him, making it a proof of certain knowledge. He then addresses specific accusations: pardoning 'Abdullah ibn 'Umar for killing al-Hurmuzan (a Persian commander), and returning al-Hakam ibn al-‘As (who had been exiled by the Prophet). He refutes these allegations. He then provides two hadiths: one promising Uthman Paradise, and another showing the Prophet's shyness from him. Finally, he discusses Uthman's murder, asserting that it was unjust, that the accusations were baseless, and that the Companions would not have appointed an unjust leader nor tolerated injustice, given their commitment to enjoining good and forbidding evil.

Key Terms
شورى
إجماع
عقد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Uthman's caliphate was illegitimate or based on nepotism.
Correction: Nasafi demonstrates that Uthman was chosen by a qualified person ('Abd al-Rahman ibn 'Awf) and that the Companions unanimously agreed, which constitutes a definitive proof.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, what proves Uthman's caliphate to be certain knowledge?
A) The Prophet's dream
B) Consensus of the Companions
C) Uthman's wealth
D) The Quran

Key Takeaway
Uthman's caliphate is established through his qualifications, the proper contract by a shura member, and the consensus of the Companions, which constitutes certain knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly accept that the consensus of the Companions on Uthman's caliphate gives you certain knowledge, or do you harbor doubts that you have not examined? If you have any uncertainty, what is its source, and are you willing to submit to the evidence of ijma?
?
When you think about Uthman's caliphate, do you feel a sense of settled belief, or do you allow later sectarian narratives to create unease in your heart? Examine your own heart: is there any trace of rejection of the consensus that Nasafi affirms?
?
The passage says consensus yields certain knowledge. In your life, do you treat the ijma of the scholars on matters of creed with that same certainty, or do you pick and choose based on personal preference? Commit today to studying and submitting to the consensus of the Ahl al-Sunnah.

Refuting Objections Against Uthman
Nasafi refutes accusations against Uthman and defends his murder.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the main objections against Uthman and how Nasafi refutes them using hadith and rational argument.

Core Idea
The accusations against Uthman are baseless; he was a promised dweller of Paradise and was killed unjustly.

Nasafi addresses specific accusations leveled against Uthman, such as pardoning a murderer and returning an exile. He refutes these by citing prophetic hadiths that promise Uthman Paradise and show the Prophet's high regard for him. Finally, he explains that Uthman's murder was tyrannical, and the Companions would not have tolerated injustice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the accusations against Uthman
Read the passage and list the two specific accusations mentioned: pardoning 'Abdullah ibn 'Umar for killing al-Hurmuzan, and returning al-Hakam ibn al-'As who was exiled by the Prophet.
2
See how Nasafi refutes them
Nasafi 'discusses all accusations to refute them.' Note that he does not provide explicit counter-arguments here; instead he moves to hadith and the murder context. Reflect on the rhetorical strategy.
3
Examine the hadiths presented
Two hadiths are cited: one promising Uthman Paradise, another stating the Prophet feels shy of him. These establish Uthman's high status and undermine accusations.
4
Understand the defense of Uthman's murder
Nasafi argues that Uthman was killed unjustly and that the accusations have no basis because the Companions appointed him and would not have remained silent if he were unjust.

Source Text

الكلام في خلافة عثمان : ويتحدث الإمام النسفي عن خلافة عثمان ، فيبين أن جميع الشروط الخاصة بالإمامة، توفرت فيه، ثم إن عبد الرحمن بن عوف، وهو أهل للعقد قد اشتغل بالعقد له، ولأنه أحد أهل الشورى، وقد انضم إلى موقف عبد الرحمن إجماع الصحابة عليه، والإجماع دليل موجب للعلم. كما يتحدث عن المطاعن التي وجهت لعثمان ، ومنها : أنه عفا عن عبد الله ابن عمر الذي قتل الهرمزان ومنها : أنه رد الحكم بن العاص طريد رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم ويناقش كل المطاعن ليردها، ثم يذكر أن عثمان من الذين بشرهم الرسول بالجنة وأن الرسول عليه السلام قال فيه : عثمان أخي ورفيقي بالجنة"، والحديث رواه الإمام أحمد في مسنده ۱۲ من حديث زيد بن أسلم عن أبيه. وقوله عليه السلام: "ألا" أستحي ممن تستحي منه ملائكة السماء" والحديث صحيح رواه مسلم في صحيحه جـ ١٥ ص ١٦٨ من حديث عائشة - رضي - الله عنها. ثم يتحدث النسفي بعد ذلك عن سبب قتل عثمان، ويبين أنه قتل ظلما وعدوانا، وأن كل ما ادعاه الأعداء لا أساس له ، لأن الصحابة هم الذين ولوه على أمورهم، وهم الذين أعانوه على كل تصرفاته، ولا يعقل أن يولوه وهو ظالم، وأن يسكتوا على الظلم وهم الذي أمر بالمعروف ونهوا عن المنكر.

?

?
The Prophet (peace be upon him) said the angels feel shy of Uthman. How deep is your reverence for the Companions? Do you carry that respect in your heart, or have you allowed worldly narratives to diminish their status? Commit today to learning more about their virtues and defending their honor.

Nasafi's Defense of Ali's Imamate and Consensus
Nasafi affirms Ali's qualifications and defends the consensus on his caliphate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi argues that Ali possessed all conditions for imamate, that his bay'ah was valid despite initial refusal, and that denying the consensus on his caliphate amounts to accusing the Prophet and Companions of falsehood.

Core Idea
Denying Ali's imamate denies the consensus of the ummah.

Nasafi begins his discussion on Ali's imamate by affirming that Ali possessed every qualification required for leadership. He then addresses the manner of his election, explaining Ali's initial reluctance and eventual acceptance for unity. Most importantly, he argues that the Companions' consensus on Ali must be accepted, as rejecting it undermines the reliability of the Prophet's community.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on Ali's qualifications
Read the first part where Nasafi states that all conditions for imamate are fulfilled in Ali, and that doubt is injustice. Notice the strong language.
2
Examine the bay'ah account
Nasafi describes Ali's initial refusal and then acceptance for unity. Consider why this is important for legitimacy.
Ali's reluctance shows humility, not lack of qualification.
3
Understand the consensus argument
Nasafi argues that denying consensus on Ali is like accusing the Prophet and Companions of falsehood. Reflect on the gravity of this claim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكلام في إمامة علي - كرم الله وجهه ويتحدث النسفي عن إمامة علي بن أبي طالب، فيذكر أن كل الشروط التي توفرت للأئمة قبله متوفرة فيه، ومن شك في ذلك فهو الظالم. ثم يتحدث عن الكيفية التي تمت بها مبايعة علي بالخلافة، وكيف رفضها علي أولا، ثم استجاب لرأي الجماعة ،ثانيا، حفاظا على وحدة الأمة. ويناقش الذين يقولون : إنه لم يحدث إجماع في اختيار علي بن أبي طالب، رادا كلامهم، مثبتا أن كل من يقول بعدم الإجماع مكذب للرسول صلى الله عليه وسلم، وللصحابة. ويتحدث بالتفصيل عن موقعة الجمل، وصفين، وقضية التحكيم، مناقشا أولئك الذين خطأوا عليا في ذلك، مبينا أن عليا كان على حق في كل تصرف صدر عنه، ثم يقول أثناء حديثه في الأمور الثلاثة : إنه لا يمكن تسمية الطرف المضاد لعلي بالفسق أو الظلم لأنهم اجتهدوا في التأويل فأخطاوا، ولم يكونوا هواة ترأس، أو
Discussion on the imamate of Ali (may Allah honor his face): Al-Nasafi speaks about the imamate of Ali ibn Abi Talib, mentioning that all the conditions that were fulfilled for the imams before him are fulfilled in him, and whoever doubts that is the unjust one. Then he speaks about the manner in which the pledge of allegiance (bay'ah) to Ali for the caliphate took place, and how Ali first refused it, then responded to the opinion of the group (al-jama'ah) secondly, in order to preserve the unity of the nation. He discusses those who say that no consensus occurred in the selection of Ali ibn Abi Talib, refuting their words, asserting that everyone who denies the consensus is accusing the Messenger (peace be upon him) and the Companions of falsehood. He speaks in detail about the Battle of the Camel, Siffin, and the arbitration issue, discussing those who criticized Ali in that, showing that Ali was right in every action he took. Then he says during his discussion of these three matters: 'It is not possible to label the opposing party to Ali as immoral (fisq) or unjust (zulm), because they exerted effort in interpretation (ijtihad) and erred, and they were not seekers of leadership...'
Source pages p. 121

Commentary
This passage defends Ali's imamate. Nasafi first states that Ali had all the conditions of the imamate, and doubts indicate injustice. He then describes Ali's initial refusal of the caliphate, then acceptance for unity. He argues that there was consensus on Ali, and denying it is to accuse the Prophet and Companions of falsehood. He then discusses the three major events (Battle of the Camel, Siffin, and arbitration), defending Ali's correctness. Notably, he concludes that those who opposed Ali were not sinful but mujtahidun who erred, distinguishing between intentional wrongdoing and mistaken ijtihad.

Key Terms
إجماع
ظلم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) They are excused
B) They are unjust
C) They are sinners
D) They are disbelievers

Key Takeaway
Nasafi affirms Ali's imamate by asserting his qualifications, explaining his bay'ah, and arguing that consensus on his caliphate is binding.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider the consensus of the early Muslims on matters of leadership, do you accept it fully, or do you harbor doubts that align you with those who 'accuse the Prophet and Companions of falsehood'? What is the state of your heart regarding the sahaba?
?
Ali initially refused the caliphate for the sake of unity. Are you willing to set aside your personal ambitions or preferences when the unity of the Muslim community is at stake, or do you insist on your own way even at the cost of division?
?
Nasafi says that doubting Ali's imamate is injustice. Examine your own beliefs: do you truly submit to the consensus of the ummah, or do you allow sectarian narratives to undermine your trust in the Prophet's companions? What step will you take today to align your conviction with the truth as presented in this lesson?

Nasafi's View on the Opponents of Ali
Nasafi defends Ali's actions and excuses his opponents as mistaken mujtahidun.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Nasafi's justification of Ali's actions in the battles of Camel, Siffin, and arbitration, and his ruling that the opposing party were mujtahidun who erred, not sinful.

Core Idea
Opponents of Ali were mistaken ijtihad, not sin.

Nasafi addresses the three major conflicts during Ali's caliphate: the Battle of the Camel, the Battle of Siffin, and the arbitration. He defends Ali's correctness in each. Then he makes a crucial theological point: the opposing party cannot be labeled as sinful or unjust because they exerted their best effort in interpretation and simply erred, not out of desire for leadership.

Guided Path
1
Read about the three events
Nasafi mentions the Battle of the Camel, Siffin, and arbitration. Note that he defends Ali's actions in all.
2
Identify Nasafi's key ruling on opponents
Nasafi explicitly says they are not fisq or zulm because they made ijtihad and erred. This is a nuanced position.
3
Reflect on the distinction
Consider why this distinction matters for Sunni theology regarding the Companions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Defense of Uthman Against Specific Accusations
Nasafi refutes accusations and cites prophetic hadiths about Uthman.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi refutes accusations against Uthman by citing hadiths affirming his high rank and divine promise.

Core Idea
Uthman's merits are proven by authentic hadiths that refute all accusations.

Nasafi addresses specific accusations against Uthman, particularly the pardon of Abdullah ibn Umar and the return of al-Hakam ibn al-As. He then counters these by citing hadiths that testify to Uthman's place in Paradise and the Prophet's respect for him. This lesson presents these scriptural proofs.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice how Nasafi first lists the accusations and then presents prophetic hadiths.
The passage uses a concise style typical of theological refutations.
2
Identify the two accusations
The passage mentions two specific accusations: the pardon of Abdullah ibn Umar for killing al-Hurmuzan, and the return of al-Hakam ibn al-As. Understand the charges.
These accusations are from critics of Uthman.
3
Recognize the two hadiths
Nasafi cites two hadiths: 'Uthman is my brother and my companion in Paradise' and 'Shall I not feel shy of one whom the angels of heaven feel shy?' Note the sources: Ahmad and Muslim.
These hadiths affirm Uthman's high status and imply his innocence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كما يتحدث عن المطاعن التي وجهت لعثمان، ومنها: أنه عفا عن عبد الله ابن عمر الذي قتل الهرمزان، ومنها: أنه رد الحكم بن العاص طريد رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم، ويناقش كل المطاعن ليردها، ثم يذكر أن عثمان من الذين بشرهم الرسول بالجنة، وأن الرسول عليه السلام قال فيه: "عثمان أخي ورفيقي في الجنة"، والحديث رواه الإمام أحمد في مسنده من حديث زيد بن أسلم عن أبيه. وقوله عليه السلام: "ألا أستحي ممن تستحي منه ملائكة السماء"، والحديث صحيح رواه مسلم في صحيحه من حديث عائشة رضي الله عنها.
As he also discusses the accusations leveled against Uthman, among them: that he pardoned Abdullah ibn Umar who killed al-Hurmuzan, and among them: that he returned al-Hakam ibn al-'As, the exile of the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him), and he discusses all the accusations to refute them, then he mentions that Uthman is among those whom the Messenger gave glad tidings of Paradise, and that the Messenger (peace be upon him) said about him: 'Uthman is my brother and my companion in Paradise,' and the hadith was narrated by Imam Ahmad in his Musnad from Zayd ibn Aslam from his father. And his saying (peace be upon him): 'Shall I not feel shy of one whom the angels of heaven feel shy?' and the hadith is authentic, narrated by Muslim in his Sahih from 'A'isha (may Allah be pleased with her).
Source pages p. 121

Commentary
This passage presents Nasafi's defense of Uthman against specific accusations. He lists two accusations: that Uthman pardoned Abdullah ibn Umar for killing al-Hurmuzan, and that he returned al-Hakam ibn al-'As who was exiled by the Prophet. Nasafi states that he discusses and refutes all such accusations. Then he provides positive evidence: the Prophet promised Uthman Paradise and stated 'Uthman is my brother and my companion in Paradise,' narrated by Ahmad. Also, the Prophet said he feels shy of Uthman because the angels feel shy of him, a hadith from Muslim. These hadiths affirm Uthman's high rank, implying that such a person cannot be unjust. The passage thus uses scriptural proofs to counter the attacks.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the hadiths about Uthman's merits are weak.
Correction: Nasafi confirms they are authentic: the first is from Ahmad's Musnad, the second from Muslim's Sahih.

D) 'Uthman is the guardian of the believers' and 'The throne shakes at the death of Uthman'

Key Takeaway
Uthman's innocence is supported by authentic hadiths that affirm his high rank in Paradise and the Prophet's respect for him.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear accusations against a righteous person like Uthman, do you rush to believe them or do you seek authentic evidence as Nasafi did? How can you train yourself to defend the honor of the pious with proofs?
?
The Prophet felt shy of Uthman. Are there people in your life—parents, scholars, or elders—whom you treat with such deep respect that you would feel ashamed to wrong them? If not, what must change in your heart?
?
This lesson defends Uthman using two authentic hadiths. Are there other believers whom you have wronged with your tongue or heart based on rumors? Will you seek forgiveness and replace assumptions with the truth from authentic sources?

Nasafi's Defense of the Sahaba's Innocence
Nasafi defends the Sahaba's innocence regarding Uthman's killing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Nasafi's argument that the Sahaba could not have been complicit in Uthman's unjust killing because they appointed him and would not tolerate injustice.

Core Idea
The Sahaba's support for Uthman proves his righteousness and their innocence.

Nasafi addresses a controversial historical event: the killing of Uthman. Critics have accused the Sahaba of either appointing an unjust ruler or remaining silent. Nasafi counters by highlighting the Sahaba's active role in enjoining good and forbidding evil, making their continued support a proof of Uthman's justice.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get the gist of Nasafi's argument.
Notice the logical flow: premise, premise, conclusion.
2
Identify the two premises
Nasafi builds his argument on two facts: the Sahaba appointed Uthman, and they are the people who enjoin good and forbid evil.
These premises are used to derive the conclusion that accusations are baseless.
3
Connect to the conclusion
From these premises, Nasafi argues it is unreasonable that the Sahaba would appoint a tyrant or tolerate injustice, so Uthman must have been just and the Sahaba innocent.
4
Reflect on the misconception
Consider the common claim that the Sahaba were complicit. Nasafi's reasoning shows this is incompatible with their character and actions.
Use the misconception field to guide reflection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم يتحدث النسفي بعد ذلك عن سبب قتل عثمان، ويبين أنه قتل ظلما وعدوانا، وأن كل ما ادعاه الأعداء لا أساس له، لأن الصحابة هم الذين ولوه على أمورهم، وهم الذين أعانوه على كل تصرفاته، ولا يعقل أن يولوه وهو ظالم، وأن يسكتوا على الظلم وهم الذين أمروا بالمعروف ونهوا عن المنكر.
Then al-Nasafi speaks after that about the reason for the killing of Uthman, and he explains that he was killed unjustly and aggressively, and that everything that the enemies claimed has no foundation, because the Companions are the ones who appointed him over their affairs, and they are the ones who assisted him in all his actions, and it is not reasonable that they would appoint him while he is unjust, and that they would remain silent about injustice while they are the ones who enjoin good and forbid evil.
Source pages p. 121

Commentary
This passage is an argument defending the Sahaba's role in Uthman's caliphate. Nasafi states that Uthman was killed unjustly and all enemy claims are baseless. The key proof is that the Sahaba appointed Uthman and supported his actions; it is inconceivable that they would appoint a tyrant or remain silent if he were unjust, especially since they were the very people commanded to enjoin good and forbid evil. Thus, their continued support shows Uthman was righteous, and the accusations are false.

Key Takeaway
Nasafi argues that the Sahaba's appointment and support of Uthman, coupled with their duty to enjoin good, proves his innocence and their non-complicity.

?

Imamate of Ali: Qualifications and Bay'ah
Nasafi lists Ali's qualifications and describes his bay'ah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Nasafi affirms Ali's imamate through his qualifications and the manner of his bay'ah, emphasizing Ali's initial reluctance and acceptance for unity.

Core Idea
Ali's imamate is affirmed by his perfect qualifications and his humble acceptance for unity.

Al-Nasafi presents a concise case for Ali's imamate, focusing on two aspects: his inherent qualifications and the historical circumstances of his bay'ah. This passage clarifies that Ali possessed all the conditions of the previous caliphs and that he accepted the caliphate only after initial refusal, acting to preserve the unity of the Muslim community.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing it. Then read the translation. Notice the two-part structure: qualifications and bay'ah.
2
Examine the claim about qualifications
Nasafi asserts that all conditions present in previous imams are present in Ali. He states that anyone who doubts this is unjust. Reflect on why this claim is significant.
This is a strong affirmation of Ali's worthiness.
3
Understand the bay'ah narrative
Nasafi describes how Ali first refused the caliphate, then accepted following the opinion of the community to preserve unity. Note the sequence: refusal then acceptance for unity.
This emphasizes Ali's reluctance and his prioritization of Muslim unity.
4
Connect to broader significance
Consider how this passage defends Ali's imamate by addressing both his qualifications and his conduct during the bay'ah. It counters claims that he sought power.
The passage serves as a concise defense of Ali's caliphate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويتحدث النسفي عن إمامة علي بن أبي طالب، فيذكر أن كل الشروط التي توفرت للأئمة قبله متوفرة فيه، ومن شك في ذلك فهو الظالم. ثم يتحدث عن الكيفية التي تمت بها مبايعة علي بالخلافة، وكيف رفضها علي أولا، ثم استجاب لرأي الجماعة، ثانيا، حفاظا على وحدة الأمة.
And al-Nasafi speaks about the imamate of 'Ali ibn Abi Talib, and he mentions that all the conditions that were available for the imams before him are available in him, and whoever doubts that is the unjust one. Then he speaks about the manner in which the pledge of allegiance to 'Ali for the caliphate was carried out, and how 'Ali refused it first, then he responded to the opinion of the group, secondly, in order to preserve the unity of the ummah.
Source pages p. 121

Commentary
This passage defines the imamate of Ali. Nasafi states that Ali possessed all the qualifications of previous caliphs; anyone who doubts this is unjust. He then describes the bay'ah: Ali initially declined the caliphate but later accepted following the consensus of the community, acting to preserve unity. This highlights Ali's reluctance but ultimate acceptance for the greater good.

?

Defending Ali’s Actions and Excusing His Opponents
Nasafi defends Ali and excuses opponents as erring mujtahids.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Nasafi’s defense of Ali’s correctness in the battles of Jamal and Siffin and the arbitration, and his nuanced position that the opposing party were mujtahids who erred but are not to be labeled as sinful or unjust.

Core Idea
Ali was right; his opponents were erring mujtahids, not sinners.

This passage addresses a key historical-theological debate: were Ali’s actions in the early civil wars correct, and how should we judge his opponents? Nasafi takes a balanced stance—affirming Ali’s correctness while excusing the opposing party as sincere but mistaken. His reasoning avoids both extreme condemnation and uncritical acceptance.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp Nasafi’s overall argument before analyzing details.
2
Identify the three historical events
Notice that Nasafi specifically mentions the Battle of the Camel, Siffin, and the arbitration. These are the key events where Ali’s actions are debated.
These events are central to Sunni-Shi’a polemics.
3
Understand Nasafi’s defense of Ali
See that Nasafi asserts Ali was correct in every action. This is a strong theological claim.
It implies that Ali’s ijtihad was correct.
4
Grasp the nuance regarding opponents
Nasafi says the opposing party cannot be called sinful or unjust because they performed ijtihad and erred. They were not motivated by power or wealth.
This is a key principle: error in ijtihad does not make one a sinner.
5
Reflect on the implications
Consider how this position avoids sectarian extremes while maintaining Ali’s superiority. It allows for coexistence of different views on historical figures.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويناقش الذين يقولون: إنه لم يحدث إجماع في اختيار علي بن أبي طالب، رادا كلامهم، مثبتا أن كل من يقول بعدم الإجماع مكذب للرسول صلى الله عليه وسلم، وللصحابة. ويتحدث بالتفصيل عن موقعة الجمل، وصفين، وقضية التحكيم، مناقشا أولئك الذين خطأوا عليا في ذلك، مبينا أن عليا كان على حق في كل تصرف صدر عنه، ثم يقول أثناء حديثه في الأمور الثلاثة: إنه لا يمكن تسمية الطرف المضاد لعلي بالفسق أو الظلم لأنهم اجتهدوا في التأويل فأخطاوا، ولم يكونوا هواة ترأس، أو استيلاء على السلطة والمال.
And he discusses those who say that there was no consensus in the selection of 'Ali ibn Abi Talib, refuting their speech, affirming that whoever says there was no consensus is accusing the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) and the Companions of falsehood. And he speaks in detail about the Battle of the Camel, Siffin, and the issue of arbitration, discussing those who deemed 'Ali wrong in that, showing that 'Ali was correct in every action that issued from him, then he says during his discussion of these three matters: It is not possible to label the opposing party to 'Ali as immoral or unjust because they exerted ijtihad in interpretation and erred, and they were not seekers of leadership or seizure of power and wealth.
Source pages p. 121

Commentary
This passage refutes claims that there was no consensus on Ali's election, arguing that denying it is a rejection of the Prophet and the Sahaba. Nasafi then addresses the battles of Jamal, Siffin, and the arbitration. He defends Ali, stating he was right in all his actions. Crucially, Nasafi explains that the opposing party cannot be called sinful (fisq) or unjust (zulm) because they were mujtahids who made an error in interpretation. They were not motivated by desire for power or wealth. Thus, they are excused as mistaken but not sinful. This is a nuanced position: Ali is correct, but opponents are not condemned as sinners.

الفسق
الظلم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some sectarians believe that Ali’s opponents in the battles and arbitration were sinful or unjust.
Correction: Nasafi argues that they were mujtahids who erred in interpretation, not seekers of power, and thus are not to be labeled as sinners or unjust.

Checkpoint

A) Because they repented after the battles.
B) Because they were acting on sincere ijtihad and erred, not seeking power or wealth.
C) Because they were forced to fight.
D) Because Ali forgave them.

Key Takeaway
Nasafi defends Ali's correctness in the battles and arbitration while excusing his opponents as erring mujtahids, avoiding sectarian extremes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
Nasafi insists that Ali's opponents were not seeking power. Have you ever assumed the worst about someone's motives in a dispute? How can this lesson help you check your own assumptions about others' intentions?

Defense of All Sahaba and Permissibility of Ta'zir
Nasafi defends all Sahaba rationally and permits ta'zir without accusing them of sin.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Nasafi's rational and transmitted defense of all Companions, including Muawiyah and Talha, and his ruling that ta'zir does not imply fisq or injustice.

Core Idea
All Sahaba are innocent of accusations; ta'zir never implies sin.

In this passage, Imam Nasafi addresses two intertwined issues: the absolute innocence of all Companions from historical accusations, and the legal principle that discretionary punishment (ta'zir) can be applied without labeling anyone as immoral or unjust. He uses both rational argument and transmitted evidence to support his position.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Let the compact style of the author sink in.
Notice the phrase 'دفاعا عقليا مدعما بالاستدلال بالنقل' – he combines reason and transmitted sources.
2
Understand the core claim about Sahaba
Identify that Nasafi declares all Sahaba, including Muawiyah and Talha, innocent of everything attributed to them. Why is this significant?
This counters sectarian accusations and upholds the collective honor of the Companions.
3
Grasp the ta'zir ruling
Nasafi states that the ruler may resort to ta'zir, but this never implies fisq or zulm. Understand that ta'zir is a disciplinary measure, not a moral judgment.
This preserves the reputations of Sahaba while allowing necessary governance.
4
Link the two parts
Reflect on how the defense of Sahaba and the ta'zir ruling fit together: the ruler can punish actions without condemning the person's moral character.
The passage shows a balanced approach – upholding honor while maintaining order.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويدافع دفاعا عقليا مدعما بالاستدلال بالنقل عن جميع الصحابة بما فيهم معاوية وطلحة، مثبتا أنهم برءاء من كل ما نسب إليهم، وللحاكم أن يلجأ إلى التعزير وليس في هذا أبدا نسبة الفسق أو الظلم.
And he defends with a rational defense supported by proof from transmitted sources about all the Companions, including Mu'awiyah and Talhah, affirming that they are innocent of everything that was attributed to them, and the ruler may resort to discretionary punishment (ta'zir), and there is never in this any imputation of immorality (fisq) or injustice (zulm).
Source pages p. 122

Commentary
This passage is a comprehensive defense of all Sahaba, specifically naming Muawiyah and Talha (and implicitly others). Nasafi uses both rational and transmitted proofs to declare them innocent of all accusations. He then adds a legal point: if a ruler needs to discipline someone involved in these conflicts, he may use ta'zir (discretionary punishment) without that implying the person is guilty of fisq or zulm. This preserves the reputations of the Sahaba while allowing for necessary governance actions.

الظلم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ta'zir (discretionary punishment) always implies that the person is guilty of a sin.
Correction: Nasafi clarifies that ta'zir is permissible without any imputation of fisq or injustice, so it does not imply moral guilt or sin.

Checkpoint

B) Ta'zir is permissible but does not imply fisq or zulm.
C) They must be declared sinful first.
D) Only Ali could punish them.

Key Takeaway
Nasafi affirms the innocence of all Sahaba through rational and textual proofs, and permits ta'zir as a governance measure without any imputation of sin or injustice.
