Sunni Consensus on the Best of the Companions
Nasafi states Sunni consensus and enumerates opposing views.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sunni position on the ranking of the caliphs after the Prophet and the sects that opposed it.

Core Idea
Ahl al-Sunna agrees: Abu Bakr is the best after the Prophet.

Nasafi begins a pivotal section on the virtues of the Rightly-Guided Caliphs by establishing the consensus of Ahl al-Sunna. He asserts that Abu Bakr is the most excellent of the Companions, and then identifies several groups who hold contrary views. This lesson examines that consensus and the opposing positions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation. Let the author's own words set the stage.
Notice the strong word 'agreed' (أجمع) which indicates consensus.
2
Identify the Sunni claim
Find the sentence where Nasafi states the Sunni position on the best companion after the Prophet. Note who is named.
3
List the opposing groups
The passage names several sects that oppose the Sunni view. Write them down: Rawafid, Imamiyya, Jarudiyya, Jaririyya, Ya'qubiyya, and the Mu'tazila with al-Ka'bi.
Some groups are Shiite, others are Mu'tazilite.
4
Reflect on the significance
Why is it important for Nasafi to state this consensus? Consider that these debates were not merely academic but reflected deep theological and political divides.
This sets the stage for proofs in the following passages.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويتحدث النسفي عن فضل الخلفاء الراشدين الأربعة فيبدأ قائلا: أجمع أهل السنة والجماعة على أن أفضل هذه الأمة بعد الرسول صلى الله عليه وسلم أبو بكر، خلافا لقول الروافض بأفضلية علي، وخلافا لقول الإمامية: إن من سوى علي وابنيه وفاطمة ونفر يسير من الصحابة ارتدوا بعد وفاة النبي. وخلافا لما قاله الجارودية، والجريرية، واليعقوبية، والكثير من المعتزلة، وعلى رأسهم الكعبي بأن عليا أفضل الصحابة.
And al-Nasafi speaks about the virtue of the four Rightly-Guided Caliphs, beginning by saying: The people of the Sunnah and the community (Ahl al-Sunna wa al-Jama'ah) have agreed that the best of this ummah after the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) is Abu Bakr, contrary to the saying of the Rawafid that 'Ali is best, and contrary to the saying of the Imamiyyah that besides 'Ali, his two sons, Fatimah, and a few Companions, all others apostatized after the death of the Prophet. And contrary to what was said by al-Jarudiyyah, al-Jaririyyah, al-Ya'qubiyyah, and many of the Mu'tazila, headed by al-Ka'bi, that 'Ali is the best of the Companions.
Source pages p. 122

Commentary
This passage presents the Sunni position on the ranking of the caliphs. Nasafi begins by stating the consensus of Ahl al-Sunna wa al-Jama'ah that Abu Bakr is the best after the Prophet. He then lists opposing views: the Rawafid (extreme Shia) say Ali is best; the Imamiyya (Twelver Shia) claim all except Ali, his sons, Fatima, and a few Companions apostatized; and several sects (Jarudiyya, Jaririyya, Ya'qubiyya, many Mu'tazila) also favor Ali over Abu Bakr. This sets up the need for proofs in subsequent passages.

Correction: Nasafi asserts that Ahl al-Sunna have agreed, and he refutes the opposing claims by naming the dissenting groups.

Checkpoint

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Nasafi presents this consensus as a settled matter. Is there any area of your belief where you have allowed sectarian arguments or popular opinions to create uncertainty? What concrete action will you take this week to ground yourself in the authentic teachings of Ahl al-Sunna?

Merits of Abu Bakr and Umar
Nasafi enumerates reasons for Abu Bakr's superiority and Umar's second rank.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the proofs from Nasafi for the superiority of Abu Bakr and the second rank of Umar, and why these positions are defended in Sunni theology.

Core Idea
The merits of the first two caliphs are established by explicit signs and the success of their leadership.

Nasafi presents the key arguments for the Sunni position on caliphal ranks. He recounts the virtues that elevate Abu Bakr above all after the Prophet, then lists Umar's achievements that secure his place as second. This lesson unpacks those arguments and the evidence cited from the Companions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice how Nasafi structures his argument: first Abu Bakr's superiority and refutation, then Umar's virtues, then a report and conquests.
2
Identify the arguments for Abu Bakr
Find the phrases that establish Abu Bakr as 'the best of the ummah' and note the refutation of those who deny his superiority over Ali.
Nasafi says 'the things that made al-Siddiq the best' and that he elaborates in refuting denial.
3
Understand Umar's virtues and title
Look for why Umar is called al-Faruq (the distinguisher) and what accomplishments are attributed to his caliphate.
The passage states: 'through him He manifested His religion and distinguished between truth and falsehood'.
4
Reflect on the Companion consensus
Consider the report about Abu Bakr's statement and the fact that no Companion objected. What does this imply for Sunni theology?
This is presented as evidence of agreement on Abu Bakr's rank.
5
Synthesize the overall argument
Combine the evidence: Abu Bakr's explicit superiority, Umar's title and conquests, and the silence of the Companions. How does this defend the caliphal rank order?

ويعدد النسفي الأمور التي جعلت الصديق أفضل الأمة بعد الرسول صلى الله عليه وسلم، ويفصل في الرد على من أنكر أفضلية الصديق على علي – رضي الله عنهما. ثم يتحدث عن فضل عمر، فيعدد الأمور التي تضع عمر في المرتبة الثانية بعد أبي بكر، فبه أظهر الله دينه وبه فرق بين الحق والباطل، ولهذا سمي الفاروق. ثم إن الصحابة سمعوا أبا بكر وهو يقول: "لو سألني الله تعالى يوم القيامة من وليت عليهم؟ لقلت: خير أهلك" ولم ينكر أحد ذلك، وببركة إمامته افتتحت بلاد العجم، وهدى الله ما لا يحصى من الناس.
And al-Nasafi enumerates the things that made al-Siddiq the best of the ummah after the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him), and he elaborates in refuting those who denied the superiority of al-Siddiq over 'Ali (may Allah be pleased with both). Then he speaks about the virtue of 'Umar, enumerating the things that place 'Umar in the second rank after Abu Bakr: through him Allah manifested His religion and through him He distinguished between truth and falsehood, and for this he was called al-Faruq. Then indeed the Companions heard Abu Bakr saying: 'If Allah, the Exalted, were to ask me on the Day of Resurrection whom I appointed over them, I would say: The best of your people,' and no one denied that. And by the blessing of his imamate, the lands of the non-Arabs were conquered, and Allah guided countless people.
Source pages p. 122

Commentary
This passage provides the proofs for Abu Bakr's superiority. Nasafi mentions unspecified 'things' that made him best, and says he elaborates in refuting those who deny his superiority over Ali. Then he discusses Umar's virtues: through him Allah made Islam manifest and distinguished truth from falsehood, earning him the title al-Faruq. Nasafi cites a report that the Companions heard Abu Bakr say if Allah were to ask him about his appointments, he would say 'I appointed the best of your people,' and no one objected, implying agreement. Additionally, the conquests of non-Arab lands and massive guidance of people occurred under Umar's caliphate, showing its blessedness.

Key Terms

عمر
الفاروق

بركة الإمامة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some claim Umar was harsh and that his caliphate lacked divine favor.
Correction: Nasafi emphasizes Umar's role in spreading truth and justice, evidenced by the title al-Faruq and the conquests that brought guidance to countless people.

Checkpoint
What title does Nasafi say Umar earned and why?
A) 'Amir al-Mu'minin because he ruled justly.
B) al-Faruq because he distinguished truth from falsehood.
C) Dhu al-Nurayn because he married two daughters of the Prophet.
D) al-Siddiq because he verified the truth.

Key Takeaway
Nasafi defends the Sunni rank order of the caliphs by citing the Companions' consensus and the tangible blessings of their leadership.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you evaluate leaders—whether in your community, your country, or your own life—do you measure them by the success of their worldly achievements or by how clearly they distinguish truth from falsehood? Name one leader you follow and ask yourself: is their guidance drawing you closer to the truth that Umar embodied? If not, what will you change today?
?
Abu Bakr's statement, 'the best of your people,' was not contradicted by the Companions. If you were to say something similar about your own choice of whom to follow, would those around you silently agree or object? What does your inner circle say about your commitment to placing the best of Allah's creation above your own desires?
?
Umar was called al-Faruq because he made truth and falsehood distinct. In your daily interactions—at work, with family, online—do you actively separate truth from falsehood, or do you compromise for convenience? Write down one specific situation today where you will consciously choose truth over falsehood, even if it is uncomfortable.

Uthman's Rank and First Merit
Nasafi declares Uthman's rank after Abu Bakr and Umar and states a merit.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Nasafi's position that Uthman is third in rank after Abu Bakr and Umar, the dissenting view of some hadith scholars, and one of Uthman's major merits.

Core Idea
Uthman is third after Abu Bakr and Umar in virtue.

In this passage, Nasafi defends the mainstream Sunni view that Uthman holds the third highest rank among the Companions, after Abu Bakr and Umar. He notes that a few hadith scholars disagreed and placed Ali above Uthman. To support his position, he begins to list Uthman's merits, starting with his generous spending for the religion. This lesson examines the ranking claim and the first evidence Nasafi offers.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that Nasafi states his position first, then mentions the dissenting scholars, then lists a merit.
The passage is brief but forms a complete unit.
2
Identify the ranking and the dissenting view
Nasafi says Uthman is after Abu Bakr and Umar. Who disagrees? Al-Husayn ibn al-Fadl al-Bajali and Muhammad ibn Ishaq ibn Khuzaymah prefer Ali over Uthman.
3
Analyze the first merit mentioned
Nasafi begins enumerating Uthman's merits. The first one is the abundant wealth he spent to support the religion. Think about how this refutes the dissenting view.
This merit is used as evidence for Uthman's high rank.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويتحدث عن فضل عثمان، مبينا أنه يأتي في المرتبة بعد أبي بكر وعمر، خلافا لقول الحسين بن الفضل البجلي، ومحمد بن إسحاق بن خزيمة من أهل الحديث فإنهما يقدمان عليا على عثمان. ويعدد مناقب عثمان: ومنها - الأموال الكثيرة التي صرفها في نصرة الدين.

And he enumerates the merits of 'Uthman: among them - the abundant wealth he spent in support of the religion.

Commentary
This passage addresses the ranking of Uthman among the four caliphs. Nasafi states his view that Uthman is third after Abu Bakr and Umar (and thus fourth overall, with Ali fourth? Actually the text says 'after Abu Bakr and Umar', implying Uthman is third, and Ali fourth according to the common view he adopts. However, he notes a dissenting view from two hadith scholars: al-Husayn ibn al-Fadl al-Bajali and Muhammad ibn Ishaq ibn Khuzaymah, who prefer Ali over Uthman. Nasafi then begins listing Uthman's merits, starting with his generous spending for the religion. The passage cuts off, but the method is clear: he provides counter-evidence to those who lower Uthman's rank.

Sunni Consensus on Abu Bakr's Superiority and Refutation of Sects
Commentary states Sunni consensus on Abu Bakr's rank and refutes sects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sunni consensus on the superiority of Abu Bakr and identify the main sects that oppose this view.

Core Idea
The Sunni consensus is that Abu Bakr is the best of the Ummah after the Prophet, contrary to several Shia and Mu'tazili sects.

This passage from al-Nasafi's work establishes the foundational Sunni belief regarding the ranking of the Companions. It first highlights al-Nasafi's thorough defense of all Companions, then declares the consensus that Abu Bakr is the most excellent after the Prophet, and identifies the specific groups who dissent. Understanding this consensus is key to distinguishing Sunni orthodoxy from sectarian positions.

Guided Path
1
Recognize al-Nasafi's approach
Notice how al-Nasafi defends all Companions, including Muawiya and Talhah, using rational and textual arguments. This sets the stage for the consensus.
The commentator praises this as the best treatment of the topic.
2
Identify the Sunni consensus
The central claim is that Ahl al-Sunna wal-Jama'ah agree that the best of the Ummah after the Prophet is Abu Bakr. Mark this as the orthodox position.
3
List the opposing groups
Find the Rawafid (who prefer Ali), the Imamiyya (who claim many Companions apostatized), and the Jarudiyya, Jaririyya, Ya'qubiyya, and many Mu'tazila (headed by al-Ka'bi) who hold Ali as the best.
These groups are explicitly named in the passage.
4
Understand the refutation
Al-Nasafi enumerates reasons for Abu Bakr's superiority and refutes those who deny it. Reflect on how this reinforces the Sunni position.
The passage indicates that al-Nasafi elaborates on this refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويدافع دفاعا عقليا مدعما بالاستدلال بالنقل عن جميع الصحابة بما فيهم معاوية وطلحة، مثبتا أنهم برءاء من كل ما نسب إليهم، وللحاكم أن يلجأ إلى التعزير وليس في هذا أبدا نسبة الفسق أو الظلم. وأحسبني أقول: إن خير ما قرأت في هذا الموضوع هو كلام النسفي الذي ينزه أصحاب رسول الله عليه السلام عن كل المطاعن. ويتحدث النسفي عن فضل الخلفاء الراشدين الأربعة فيبدأ قائلاً: أجمع أهل السنة والجماعة على أن أفضل هذه الأمة بعد الرسول صلى الله عليه وسلم أبو بكر، خلافا لقول الروافض بأفضلية علي، وخلافا لقول الإمامية: إن من سوى علي وابنيه وفاطمة ونفر يسير من الصحابة ارتدوا بعد وفاة النبي. وخلافا لما قاله الجارودية، والجريرية، واليعقوبية، والكثير من المعتزلة، وعلى رأسهم الكعبي بأن عليا أفضل الصحابة. ويعدد النسفي الأمور التي جعلت الصديق أفضل الأمة بعد الرسول صلى الله عليه وسلم، ويفصل في الرد على من أنكر أفضلية الصديق على علي – رضي الله عنهما.
He defends with a rational defense supported by transmitted evidence concerning all the Companions, including Muawiya and Talhah, affirming that they are innocent of everything attributed to them, and the ruler may resort to discretionary punishment, and in this there is never an attribution of corruption or injustice. And I think I say: Indeed, the best I have read on this topic is the speech of al-Nasafi, who absolves the Companions of the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, from all accusations. And al-Nasafi discusses the virtue of the four Rightly Guided Caliphs, beginning by saying: The people of the Sunnah and the community are agreed that the best of this nation after the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, is Abu Bakr, contrary to the statement of the Rawafid about the superiority of Ali, and contrary to the statement of the Imamiyya that those other than Ali, his two sons, Fatima, and a few of the Companions apostatized after the death of the Prophet. And contrary to what was said by al-Jarudiyya, al-Jaririyya, al-Ya'qubiyya, and many of the Mu'tazila, headed by al-Ka'bi, that Ali is the best of the Companions. And al-Nasafi enumerates the matters that made al-Siddiq the best of the nation after the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, and elaborates on refuting those who deny the superiority of al-Siddiq over Ali, may Allah be pleased with them both.
Source pages p. 122

Commentary
This passage is a commentary praising al-Nasafi's treatment of the Companions and then states the Sunni consensus on the superiority of Abu Bakr. It begins by noting that al-Nasafi defends all Companions rationally and with textual evidence, particularly mentioning Muawiya and Talhah, showing they are innocent of accusations. The commentator expresses that this is the best he has read on the topic. Then al-Nasafi is quoted as starting his discussion of the four caliphs by declaring the consensus of Ahl al-Sunna that the best after the Prophet is Abu Bakr. He refutes the Rawafid (Shia) who prefer Ali, the Imamiyya who claim many Companions apostatized, and various sects like al-Jarudiyya, al-Jaririyya, al-Ya'qubiyya, and many Mu'tazila including al-Ka'bi who hold Ali as best. Finally, al-Nasafi enumerates the reasons for Abu Bakr's superiority and refutes those who deny it. The passage thus establishes the orthodox position and identifies the opposing groups.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Sunni consensus on Abu Bakr's superiority implies denigration of Ali or that only the first three caliphs are considered virtuous.
Correction: The passage shows that the consensus affirms Abu Bakr as the best, but does not degrade Ali; rather, it refutes those who elevate Ali above Abu Bakr, while still honoring all Companions.

D) All of the above

Key Takeaway
The Sunni consensus is that Abu Bakr is the most excellent of the Companions after the Prophet, a position defended rationally and textually by al-Nasafi against various Shia and Mu'tazili sects.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Are you quick to dismiss the views of those who disagree with the Sunni consensus on the Companions? How does knowing that al-Nasafi engaged rationally with each sect challenge you to approach disagreements with patience and evidence?
?
If someone you respect holds that Ali is superior to Abu Bakr, would you have the courage to gently correct them using the consensus and reasoning from this lesson? What would that require of you?

Conclusion of Tabsirat al-Adilla: Praise of Ali and Prophetic Virtues
Nasafi concludes his discussion on imamate with praise of Ali and ends the book.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the virtues of Ali mentioned in the conclusion of Tabsirat al-Adilla and the significance of the prophetic reports cited.

Core Idea
The Prophet's actions and reports affirm Ali's high status.

In the concluding passage of his great work Tabsirat al-Adilla, Imam al-Nasafi summarizes key virtues of Ali ibn Abi Talib by recalling two prophetic traditions: the Pledge of Ridwan and the angels' shyness. He then affirms that Ali was the best of his time during his caliphate. This lesson explores these final remarks and their theological significance.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the two distinct parts: the prophetic reports and the author's conclusion.
2
Understand the Pledge of Ridwan
Reflect on the event where the Prophet placed his hand for Uthman. What does this imply about Ali's status?
3
Grasp the author's final assertion
Nasafi states Ali was the best of his time. Consider the qualities he lists (knowledge, piety, etc.) and why this matters in the context of imamate discussions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإقامة النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم إحدى يديه مقام يد عثمان في بيعة الرضوان، والإخبار بأن الملائكة تستحي منه. ويختتم النسفي حديثه في الإمامة عن فضل علي، وأنه أفضل أهل زمانه في خلافته لعلمه وورعه، واجتهاده، وشجاعته. ذلك وبعد ينهي وبعون الله الإمام النسفي كتابه الكبير "تبصرة الأدلة". والحمد لله رب العالمين الذي أعانني ووفقني.
And the Prophet's (peace be upon him) placing one of his hands in the place of the hand of 'Uthman in the Pledge of Ridwan, and the report that the angels are shy of him. And al-Nasafi concludes his discussion on the imamate concerning the virtue of 'Ali, and that he was the best of his time during his caliphate due to his knowledge, piety, diligence, and bravery. That and afterwards, with the help of Allah, Imam al-Nasafi finishes his great book 'Tabsirat al-Adilla'. And praise be to Allah, Lord of the worlds, Who helped me and granted me success.
Source pages p. 123

Commentary
This passage is the concluding remarks of the book. It begins by recalling two prophetic virtues: the Prophet's action during the Pledge of Ridwan, where he placed his own hand in place of 'Uthman's hand (since 'Uthman was absent), and the report that the angels are shy of the Prophet. Then the author states that he concludes his discussion on the imamate by mentioning the virtue of 'Ali, affirming that 'Ali was the best of his time during his caliphate due to his knowledge, piety, diligence, and bravery. Finally, al-Nasafi announces the completion of his great work 'Tabsirat al-Adilla' with Allah's help, and he praises Allah for granting him assistance and success.

Practical Refutation of Sophists
Prescription of physical punishment to refute sophists.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author uses a practical, experiential argument to refute those who deny the reality of sensory experiences.

Core Idea
Pain forces the sophist to admit what they deny in words.

The author presents a striking practical argument. Instead of merely debating, he proposes imposing physical suffering on deniers of reality. When they cry out, their own actions betray their claimed position. This lesson examines that method and its underlying logic.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the passage slowly, noting the author's direct tone. Then read the translation.
2
Analyze the author's strategy
The author prescribes punishment. Why does he think this will work? Notice the sequence: punish, then when they complain, tell them their pain is unreal.
3
Reflect on the dialectical point
Consider how this argument exposes the sophist's inconsistency. Their actions reveal they do believe in the reality of pain.
This is a powerful ad hominem argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مناقشة منكري الحقائق والعلوم: ولكن ينبغي أن يعاقبوا بقطع الجوارح، والضرب المبرح، ومنع الطعام والشراب فإذا استغاثوا أو ضجروا وطلبوا الطعام والشراب، قيل لهم : لا حقيقة للقطع والضرب والجوع والعطش، إنما ذلك كله حسبان وظن منكم، وهو في الحقيقة إيصال الراحة إليكم، وإنعام عليكم، إلى أن يتركوا العناد، ويقروا.
Refutation of the deniers of realities: But they should be punished by cutting of limbs, severe beating, and preventing food and drink; so when they cry for help or become distressed and ask for food and drink, it is said to them: There is no reality to the cutting, beating, hunger, and thirst; all of that is only calculation and conjecture from you, and in reality it is bringing comfort to you and kindness to you, until they abandon obstinacy and confess.
Source pages p. 141

Commentary
This passage presents a practical refutation of those who deny realities (al-ḥaqāʾiq). The author says: They should be punished by cutting off limbs, severe beating, and withholding food and drink. When they cry out for help or become distressed and ask for food and drink, they are told: There is no reality to the cutting, beating, hunger, and thirst; all that is merely opinion and conjecture from you. In reality, it is bringing comfort to you and kindness to you. This continues until they abandon obstinacy and confess. The point is to show that the sophist's position is self-refuting because in practice they act as if pain and pleasure are real. By forcing them to experience pain, they are compelled to admit the reality of sensations.

Key Terms
الحقائق
العناد
الجوارح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think this passage advocates torturing all intellectual opponents.
Correction: It is specifically a dialectical response to those who deny all sensory reality, using their own reactions to prove inconsistency.

Checkpoint
How does the author propose to refute the deniers of realities in practice?
A) By logical argument.
B) By physical punishment and deprivation until they admit.
C) By ignoring them.
D) By excommunication.

Key Takeaway
The denial of sensory reality is self-refuting because one's actions necessarily affirm what words reject.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Where in your life do you say you believe something but your reactions show otherwise? Think of a trial you complain about—does that complaint reveal what you truly believe about its reality? How will you align your actions with your beliefs starting today?
?
The sophist's denial crumbles under a simple cut. What small test would expose your own inconsistency? What claim about faith or patience do you fail to act on? Commit to one concrete change in how you respond to difficulty.
?
Imagine you told someone in pain that their suffering is 'actually comfort'—how would you expect them to react? This is what the teacher does to the sophist. Are there times you dismiss others' pain as 'not real'? How does this lesson challenge you to take their experience seriously?

The Self-Contradiction of Denying Realities
Nasafi proposes a practical refutation of the deniers of realities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi exposes the self-contradiction of those who deny realities by proposing a practical test.

Core Idea
Denying realities is self-defeating when put to the test.

Nasafi addresses the deniers of realities—sophists who claim nothing is real. He proposes a radical practical response: punish them physically, and when they complain, tell them it's all an illusion. This lesson shows how their very reaction proves they believe in realities.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on punishment
Read the Arabic carefully. Note the proposed punishment: amputation, beating, deprivation.
This is a thought experiment.
2
Identify the self-contradiction
When the deniers cry out in pain, they reveal they believe pain is real. Their actions contradict their words.
3
Understand the method
Nasafi forces them to acknowledge realities by making them experience what they deny.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مناقشة منكري الحقائق والعلوم ولكن ينبغي أن يعاقبوا بقطع الجوارح، والضرب المبرح، ومنع الطعام والشراب فإذا استغاثوا أو ضجروا وطلبوا الطعام والشراب، قيل لهم : لا حقيقة للقطع والضرب والجوع والعطش، إنما ذلك كله حسبان وظن منكم، وهو في الحقيقة إيصال الراحة إليكم ، وإنعام عليكم، إلى أن يتركوا العناد، ويقروا بالحقائق، ثم هم بإنكارهم العلوم والحقائق مقرون أن لا حقيقة لقولهم ومذهبهم، وأنهم لا يعلمون صحة مذهبهم، وبطلان قول خصومهم، ومن أقر ببطلان مذهبه، كفى خصمه مؤنة مجادلته، وكذا بقاؤه إلى هذه المدة دليل أنه يعرف الحقائق إذ لو لم يكن عالما بأسباب البقاء فاجتلبها، وبأسباب الهلاك فاجتنبها لما تصور بقاؤه، بل تلف بأوهى مدة، فإن من لم يتناول الأغذية، ولم يلبس الثياب الدافعة لمضرة الحر والبرد، ولم يتحرز عن اقتحام النيران المضطرمة، وإسقاط نفسه في الأمكنة المرتفعة ومقاربة الأفاعي الناهشة والعقارب اللاذعة لتلف من ساعته، فدل بقاؤهم إلى هذه المدة على علمهم بحقائق الأشياء، وكذا قولهم: لا حقيقة للأشياء تحقيق منهم لنفي الحقائق، فكانوا مبطلين مقالتهم بنفس مقالتهم.
Discussion of the Deniers of Realities and Sciences: But they should be punished by amputation of limbs, severe beating, and deprivation of food and drink. So when they cry out or become distressed and ask for food and drink, it should be said to them: 'There is no reality to amputation, beating, hunger, and thirst; all that is merely supposition and opinion from you, and in reality it is conveying comfort to you and favor upon you,' until they abandon stubbornness and acknowledge realities. Then, by their denial of sciences and realities, they admit that there is no reality to their statement and doctrine, and that they do not know the correctness of their doctrine and the falsehood of the statement of their opponents. And whoever admits the falsehood of his doctrine, his opponent is spared the trouble of debating him. Likewise, his survival until this time is evidence that he knows realities, for if he were not knowledgeable of the causes of survival and thus sought them, and of the causes of destruction and thus avoided them, his survival would be inconceivable; rather, he would have perished in the weakest moment. For one who does not consume food, does not wear clothing that repels the harm of heat and cold, does not guard against throwing himself into blazing fires, dropping himself from high places, and approaching venomous snakes and stinging scorpions, would perish instantly. Thus their survival until this time indicates their knowledge of the realities of things. Likewise, their statement 'There is no reality to things' is a confirmation from them of the negation of realities, so they invalidate their own statement by their very statement.
Source pages p. 141

Commentary
This passage refutes those who deny the existence of realities and sciences. It begins by stating the appropriate punishment for such deniers: they should be disciplined by physical punishment and deprivation until they acknowledge realities. Then it reveals their self-contradiction: by denying realities, they admit that their own statement and doctrine have no reality, and they cannot know the truth of their own position. The author then presents a practical proof: their very survival demonstrates that they know realities, because they seek what preserves life and avoid what harms it. If they truly did not know realities, they would not be able to sustain themselves. Finally, the passage points out that even their denial statement ('there is no reality') is itself a statement with reality, which undermines their position.

Key Terms
حقائق
إنكار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does Nasafi suggest as a punishment for deniers of realities?
A) Ignoring them
B) Physical punishment and deprivation
C) Exiling them
D) Long debates

Key Takeaway
The denier's own reaction to pain proves they believe in realities, exposing their self-contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you experienced pain or discomfort and instinctively treated it as real, even while outwardly claiming it didn't matter? What does that reveal about your true beliefs? How can you close the gap between your words and your actions?
?
If someone you respect denied an obvious truth, how would you respond? Are you ever yourself guilty of ignoring reality to avoid discomfort? What concrete step can you take today to align your life with the truth, even when it hurts?
?
This argument uses physical suffering to expose inconsistency. Consider your own life: is there a belief you hold that you don't act on? What would it take for you to live consistently with what you claim to believe?

The Survival Argument for Knowledge of Realities
Nasafi provides a pragmatic argument from survival and a self-referential refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi uses the fact of survival to argue that even deniers of realities must know the true causes of preservation and harm.

Core Idea
Survival itself proves knowledge of realities.

Nasafi continues his refutation by pointing to the undeniable fact that the deniers have survived until now. Their survival requires that they seek what preserves life and avoid what destroys it—actions that presuppose knowledge of real causes. This lesson examines that practical proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the survival argument
Read the second half of the passage. Note the reasoning: if they didn't know causes, they would have perished.
2
List the examples
Nasafi gives examples: food, clothing, avoiding fire, high places, snakes, scorpions. These are real dangers.
3
Connect to the self-refutation
The deniers also say 'there is no reality' which is itself a statement claiming reality, so their own words refute them.

Refuting the Denial of Realities
Abu al-Mu'in refutes Sophists by self-contradiction and everyday evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Abu al-Mu'in refutes the denial of realities by showing its self-contradiction and by pointing to everyday evidence of survival.

Core Idea
Denying reality is self-refuting; your own survival proves you know what's real.

In this passage, Abu al-Mu'in al-Nasafi confronts a philosophical sect that denies the reality of things. Rather than engaging in abstract debate, he uses a simple yet powerful argument: if nothing is real, then your own claim has no reality, and your very existence contradicts your assertion. Let's see how he dismantles their position.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
2
Identify the self-refutation argument
Notice how the author argues that the Sophists' denial itself proves they cannot claim truth for their doctrine, making their position self-defeating.
This is the first prong of the refutation.
3
Examine the survival evidence
The author points to their continued existence as proof that they know realities, since they avoid dangers and seek benefits. Identify the examples he gives.
Examples: eating, clothing, avoiding fire, snakes, etc.
4
Reflect on the overall argument
Consider why this combination of logical self-refutation and empirical evidence is effective. How does it close the door on the Sophist position?
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بالحقائق، ثم هم بإنكارهم العلوم والحقائق مقرون أن لا حقيقة لقولهم ومذهبهم، وأنهم لا يعلمون صحة مذهبهم، وبطلان قول خصومهم، ومن أقر ببطلان مذهبه، كفى خصمه مؤنة مجادلته، وكذا بقاؤه إلى هذه المدة دليل أنه يعرف الحقائق إذ لو لم يكن عالما بأسباب البقاء فاجتلبها، وبأسباب الهلاك فاجتنبها لما تصور بقاؤه، بل تلف بأوهى مدة، فإن من لم يتناول الأغذية، ولم يلبس الثياب الدافعة لمضرة الحر والبرد، ولم يتحرز عن اقتحام النيران المضطرمة، وإسقاط نفسه في الأمكنة المرتفعة ومقاربة الأفاعي الناهشة والعقارب اللاذعة لتلف من ساعته، فدل بقاؤهم إلى هذه المدة على علمهم بحقائق الأشياء، وكذا قولهم: لا حقيقة للأشياء تحقيق منهم لنفي الحقائق، فكانوا مبطلين مقالتهم بنفس مقالتهم.
By realities, then by their denial of knowledge and realities they are forced (to admit) that there is no reality to their statement and their doctrine, and that they do not know the correctness of their doctrine nor the falsity of their opponents' statement. Whoever admits the falsity of his doctrine spares his opponent the burden of debating him. Likewise, their survival until this time is proof that he knows realities, for if he were not knowledgeable of the causes of survival to draw them, and the causes of destruction to avoid them, his survival would not be conceivable; rather he would perish in the weakest time. Indeed, whoever does not consume nourishment, does not wear protective garments against the harm of heat and cold, does not guard against plunging into blazing fires, dropping himself from high places, or approaching biting snakes and stinging scorpions — he would perish in that very hour. So their survival until this time indicates their knowledge of the realities of things. Likewise, their statement 'There is no reality for things' is a confirmation from them of the negation of realities, so they refute their own statement by their very statement.
Source pages p. 142

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Sophists who deny the reality of things (حقائق). Abu al-Mu'in al-Nasafi employs a reductio ad absurdum argument: if the Sophists truly believed that nothing has reality, then their own statement would have no reality, and they could not claim to know the truth of their doctrine or the falsity of others'. Moreover, their very survival in the world proves that they have knowledge of realities — they eat to avoid hunger, wear clothes to protect against weather, avoid fire and dangerous animals. These actions are based on real knowledge of cause and effect, contradicting their claim. Thus, their denial is self-refuting: by asserting that there is no reality, they implicitly affirm the reality of their assertion, destroying their own position.

Key Terms
الحقائق
الإنكار
بقاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some people deny that things have any real existence or essence.
Correction: Their own survival and actions (eating, clothing, avoiding harm) prove they acknowledge realities, and their statement itself affirms a reality.

Checkpoint
What is the main evidence Abu al-Mu'in uses to prove that the deniers of realities actually know realities?
A) Their use of logic in arguments
B) Their continued survival by avoiding harm and seeking benefit
C) Their admission that they are wrong
D) Their reliance on sense perception

Key Takeaway
The denial of realities is self-refuting, and everyday survival proves that people possess real knowledge of causes and effects.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

?
The Sophist’s denial is self-refuting. What belief do you hold that, if you really lived by it, would make your own life impossible? How does this lesson call you to align your actions with truth?

The Sophist Doubt Concerning the Senses and Its Refutation
Abu al-Mu'in presents and refutes the Sophist doubt about the senses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Abu al-Mu'in refutes the Sophist doubt about the senses by showing that the doubt itself presupposes knowledge of realities.

Core Idea
The Sophist's own argument proves they know realities.

The Sophists argued that the senses are unreliable because they give contradictory information. But Abu al-Mu'in turns this argument on its head: to even formulate the doubt, the Sophists must already know the realities of honey, bitterness, sight, etc. Thus, their doubt self-destructs. Let's see how.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the structure: first the doubt, then the refutation.
The passage is a single continuous argument.
2
Identify the Sophist doubt
Find the examples given (jaundiced person, cross-eyed). Understand why they think it invalidates sense knowledge.
The doubt claims contradictory judgments undermine the senses as a source of knowledge.
3
Understand the refutation
See how Abu al-Mu'in points out that the Sophists must know realities to claim contradiction. Why does this refute their position?
The refutation is based on self-refutation: the doubt proves the opposite.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
شبهتهم في الحواس: وشبهتهم أن أعلا أسباب العلم عندكم الحواس الخمس، وهي لا تصلح سببا له، لأن قضاياها متناقضة، فإن الممرور يجد العسل مرا، وغيره يجده حلوا، والأحول يرى الشيء شيئين، وغيره يراه واحدا وذلك كله عمل الحس، وما تناقضت قضاياه، كل هذا التناقض لا يصلح دليلا لشيء، فضلا عن أن يكون سببا مثبتا. وهذه الشبهة تدل أنهم يعلمون الحقائق ويثبتونها، غير أنهم يعاندون فإنهم لو لم يعرفوا الحواس أنها ما هي وأن قضاياها متناقضة وأن ما يتناقض قضاياه لا يصلح دليلا، وأن القضية ما هي وأن الدليل ما هو، وأن العسل ما هو، وأن الممرور ما هو، وأن المرارة ما هي، وأنه يجد العسل مرا، وأن الأحول من هو، وأن الرؤية ما هي، وأنه يرى الواحد اثنين، وأن الواحد ما هو، وأن الاثنين ما هما، لو لم يعلموا بحقائق هذه الأشياء لما اشتغلوا بإيراد هذه الشبهة، فعين ما استدلوا به دليل بطلان قولهم.
Their doubt concerning the senses: their doubt is that the highest causes of knowledge according to you are the five senses, yet they are not fit to be a cause for it, because their judgments are contradictory. For the jaundiced person finds honey bitter, while another finds it sweet; the cross-eyed person sees a thing as two, while another sees it as one — all of that is the work of sense. And what has contradictory judgments, all this contradiction does not constitute a proof for anything, let alone be an affirmative cause. And this doubt indicates that they know and affirm realities, but they are obstinate. For if they did not know what the senses are, that their judgments are contradictory, that what has contradictory judgments is not fit to be a proof, what a proposition is, what a proof is, what honey is, what the jaundiced person is, what bitterness is, that he finds honey bitter, what the cross-eyed person is, what sight is, that he sees the one as two, what the one is, and what the two are — if they did not know the realities of these things, they would not have busied themselves with presenting this doubt. So the very thing they used as proof is proof of the falsehood of their statement.
Source pages p. 142

Commentary
This passage presents the Sophists' main argument against the reliability of sense perception: the senses contradict themselves (e.g., honey tastes sweet to one and bitter to a jaundiced person; a cross-eyed person sees one object as two). Therefore, sense perception cannot be a valid source of knowledge. Abu al-Mu'in refutes this by pointing out that the Sophists' very argument presupposes knowledge of realities. To claim that the senses are contradictory, they must know what 'honey', 'bitter', 'sweet', 'cross-eyed', 'two', 'one', etc. mean in reality. They must also know what a 'judgment', 'contradiction', and 'proof' are. Without such knowledge, they could not formulate their doubt. Thus, their argument actually demonstrates that they do know realities, contradicting their own initial stance. The author concludes that their doubt is self-refuting: the evidence they rely on (the contradictions) is precisely what proves their position false.

Key Terms
الحواس
القضايا المتناقضة
الممرور
الأحول
المشاكسة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Senses are always contradictory and cannot provide any knowledge.
Correction: Abu al-Mu'in does not deny that senses can be contradictory, but shows that the Sophist's argument itself relies on knowledge of realities, so they cannot use that to deny all knowledge.

Checkpoint
How does Abu al-Mu'in refute the Sophist argument that the senses are contradictory?
A) By denying that contradictions occur
B) By pointing out that the Sophists must know realities to claim contradiction
C) By saying the senses are always reliable
D) By dismissing the examples as false

Key Takeaway
The Sophist's doubt about the senses is self-refuting because it requires knowledge of the very realities it denies.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a contradiction between your own experience and someone else's, do you immediately dismiss both as unreliable, or do you seek a deeper explanation? This passage shows that even contradictions can point to underlying realities. Where in your life are you too quick to doubt what you know because of a single inconsistency?
?
The Sophists used their own knowledge of honey, bitterness, and sight to argue that senses can't be trusted. Are there beliefs you hold that you use as tools to argue against the very foundations of those beliefs? How might you be 'obstinate' like the Sophist, denying truths that you actually rely on?
?
If you truly believed that the senses give no certain knowledge, how would you act differently today? Yet you still trust them to eat, walk, and speak. This lesson exposes a hypocrisy in doubting without living that doubt. Will you commit today to honestly acknowledging the realities you actually depend on, rather than arguing against them for the sake of debate?

Refuting the Skeptics: The Self-Refuting Nature of Radical Skepticism
Abu al-Mu'in refutes the skeptics using dialectical and empirical arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the dialectical argument used to refute the skeptical claim of not knowing whether things have reality.

Core Idea
Radical skepticism self-destructs when asked: 'Do you know that you do not know?'

Abu al-Mu'in addresses a group of skeptics who neither affirm nor deny the reality of things but claim ignorance. He presents a powerful dialectical refutation that forces them into contradiction or infinite regress, and also notes that practical experience and survival reveal their implicit knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Understand the skeptics' position
The skeptics say: 'We do not know whether things have a reality or not.' They do not deny reality but suspend judgment.
They are known as Lā-Adriyya (I-don't-knowers).
2
Analyze the first dialectical trap
Ask them: 'Do you know that you do not know?' If they say yes, they admit to knowing something, contradicting their skepticism.
3
Consider the infinite regress
If they say no, they do not even know that they are ignorant. Then ask about that level of ignorance, leading to an infinite regress.
This shows the absurdity of their position.
4
Reflect on empirical and survival arguments
The author notes that cutting limbs causes distress, showing they acknowledge pain and thus reality. Their very survival also indicates knowledge of realities.
These refutations reveal their obstinacy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وطائفة من هؤلاء المتجاهلة، لا يثبتون القول بنفي الحقائق، بل يقولون لا ندري هل للأشياء حقيقة أم لا؟ وهم المتشككة. ويقال لهم: هل تدرون أنكم لا تدرون؟ فإن قالوا: نعم، فقد أقروا أنهم يدرون، وهو نقض مذهبهم، وإن قالوا: لا أنا لا ندري سئلوا عن نفي الدراية عن درايتهم، ثم عن الثالث والرابع إلى مالا يتناهى، ثم هذا يبطل قولهم حيث أقروا أنهم لا يدرون مذهبهم، ثم يعلمون بما بينا من قطع الجوارح وغيرها ليظهر عند ضجرهم أنهم يدرون، وكذا بقاؤهم يدل أنهم يدرون الحقائق ويعاندون.
And a group of these ignoramuses do not affirm the statement of denying realities, but rather they say: 'We do not know whether things have a reality or not.' They are the skeptics. And it is said to them: 'Do you know that you do not know?' If they say 'Yes', then they have admitted that they know, and that undermines their doctrine. And if they say 'No, I do not know', then they are asked concerning the negation of knowledge from their knowledge, and then concerning the third and fourth ad infinitum. Then this refutes their statement, since they admit that they do not know their doctrine. Then they are made to know by what we have explained of cutting limbs and other things, so that when they become distressed, it becomes evident that they do know. Likewise, their survival indicates that they know realities but are obstinate.
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This passage deals with another sophist group: the skeptics (المتشككة), also known as the Lā-Adriyya (I-don't-knowers). They do not outright deny realities but claim that they do not know whether things have reality or not. Abu al-Mu'in presents a dialectical refutation: ask them whether they know that they do not know. If they answer 'yes', they admit to knowing something, contradicting their skepticism. If they answer 'no', then they do not even know that they are ignorant, leading to an infinite regress when asked about each level of ignorance. This shows the absurdity of their position. Additionally, the author points out that practical tests (like cutting limbs) force them to show that they do know realities (they would react in pain). Their very survival, as argued earlier, also evidences implicit knowledge of realities. Thus, their skepticism is insincere and self-refuting.

Key Terms
المتشككة
اللاأدرية
الدراية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Skepticism about the reality of things is a valid philosophical stance.
Correction: Skepticism is self-refuting because the skeptic either admits knowing something or falls into infinite regress; also practical evidence shows they do acknowledge reality.

Checkpoint
What is the first question used to refute the skeptics?
A) Do you know that there is a reality?
B) Do you know that you do not know?
C) Do you know what honey is?
D) Do you know the causes of survival?

Key Takeaway
The skeptics' claim of not knowing is self-refuting because it either admits knowledge or leads to logical absurdity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever used 'I don't know' as a shield to avoid accepting a truth that would demand you change your life? If so, what truth are you running from, and will you today admit that you actually know it in your heart?
?
When you feel pain or distress, do you immediately acknowledge the reality of your situation, or do you sometimes retreat into denial? How does this lesson expose the inconsistency of claiming ignorance while living in a world of undeniable realities?
?
If your very survival—every heartbeat, every breath—proves that you know reality, then what is holding you back from acknowledging the ultimate Reality (Allah) and submitting to Him? Will you continue to hide behind skepticism, or will you respond to the evidence that is already within you?

The Relativists: Truth as Belief-Dependent
Abu al-Mu'in states the relativist claim that realities follow beliefs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the relativist position that truths depend on individual beliefs and the inherent self-contradiction in that claim.

Core Idea
What you believe doesn't make reality — reality makes belief possible.

After dealing with those who deny reality altogether and those who suspend judgment, Abu al-Mu'in now mentions a third group: the relativists. They argue that the truth of a thing is nothing more than what each person believes about it. This short sentence introduces a position that, while seductive, collapses under its own weight. Let's examine it carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and its translation. Notice that it is a single declarative sentence stating the claim of a group.
The group is called 'al-Indiyya' (the relativists).
2
Understand the claim
Reflect on what it means for realities to depend on beliefs. Consider an example: if you believe honey is sweet and I believe it is bitter, does that mean honey is both sweet and bitter at the same time?
This position rejects any objective reality independent of the observer.
3
Identify the self-refutation
Think about the status of the claim itself. Is it presented as an objective truth? If so, it contradicts its own premise that all truths are relative. If it is merely a relative truth, then it doesn't apply to those who don't believe it.
This aligns with the earlier arguments against other sophist groups.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وطائفة أخرى منهم يزعمون أن حقائق الأشياء تابعة لاعتقادات المعتقدين.
And another group of them claim that the realities of things are dependent upon the beliefs of the believers.
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This very brief passage introduces a third sophist faction: those who assert that the realities of things are not objective but are relative to the beliefs of each person (often called the 'Indiyya' or relativists). This position means that if someone believes honey is sweet, it is sweet for them; if another believes it is bitter, it is bitter for them — there is no independent reality. While the author does not refute them explicitly in this short excerpt, the refutation is implicit from the earlier arguments. The same logic that exposed the self-contradiction of the first two groups applies here: to claim that realities depend on belief, one must already know what a 'thing' and 'belief' are, and the statement itself claims to be an objective truth about reality, which contradicts the relativist premise. The brevity suggests this is a mere enumeration of sophist sects.

Key Terms
الحقائق تابعة للاعتقادات
العندية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: People often think that if everyone has their own perspective, then there is no objective truth.
Correction: The relativist's claim that 'all truths are relative' is itself an absolute claim, which is contradictory. Also, shared concepts like 'existence' and 'belief' are presupposed.

Checkpoint
What is the claim of the group mentioned in this passage?
A) There is no reality at all
B) We do not know if there is reality
C) Realities depend on each person's beliefs
D) The senses are always wrong

Key Takeaway
The relativist claim that truths depend on beliefs is self-defeating because it makes an absolute truth claim about the nature of reality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hold a strong belief, do you ever assume that your view is the only true reality? How does this lesson challenge the comfort of thinking your perspective is absolute? Will you acknowledge the difference between your belief and objective truth, even when it's uncomfortable?
?
Have you ever avoided seeking truth because it might disrupt your current worldview? The relativist position allows people to escape accountability to reality. In what area of your life are you most tempted to believe that 'my truth is true for me'? Commit to one step this week to submit your beliefs to objective evidence.
?
If realities do not depend on beliefs, then you are accountable to a real standard. Does this thought make you uneasy? Why? What specific belief do you hold that you have not honestly tested against the truth? Will you let this lesson drive you to that test?

Refutation of the Skeptics (Mutashakkika)
Al-Nasafi refutes the skeptics using a logical dilemma and practical proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the skeptics' position is self-refuting through logical dilemma and practical proof.

Core Idea
Skepticism is self-defeating: you cannot deny knowledge without knowing.

Al-Nasafi confronts the skeptics who claim we cannot know if things have reality. He first traps them in a logical dilemma that forces them to admit knowledge or face infinite regress. Then he appeals to undeniable human experiences like pain and survival to show that even skeptics act as if they know reality.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without pausing. Then read the translation to grasp the full argument Al-Nasafi presents against the skeptics.
Notice the subtlety of the first question: 'Do you know that you do not know?'
2
Identify the logical dilemma
Examine the two possible answers to the question. See how each answer leads to a contradiction or an infinite regress, refuting skepticism.
This is a classic 'dilemma' argument in kalam.
3
Consider the practical proof
Reflect on the example of cutting limbs and the fact that skeptics react with distress. Also, their very survival indicates they know realities like food and drink.
This empirical proof complements the logical one.
4
Reflect on the overall refutation
Ask yourself why this argument is considered decisive. How does it show that skepticism is not a viable position in Islamic theology?
This connects to the broader purpose of proving the knowability of truths about God and creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وطائفة من هؤلاء المتجاهلة، لا يثبتون القول بنفي الحقائق، بل يقولون لا ندري هل للأشياء حقيقة أم لا؟ وهم المتشككة. ويقال لهم: هل تدرون أنكم لا تدرون؟ فإن قالوا: نعم، فقد أقروا أنهم يدرون، وهو نقض مذهبهم، وإن قالوا: لا أنا لا ندري سئلوا عن نفي الدراية عن درايتهم، ثم عن الثالث والرابع إلى ما لا يتناهى، ثم هذا يبطل قولهم حيث أقروا أنهم لا يدرون مذهبهم، ثم يعلمون بما بينا من قطع الجوارح وغيرها ليظهر عند ضجرهم أنهم يدرون، وكذا بقاؤهم يدل أنهم يدرون الحقائق ويعاندون
And a group of these ignorant people do not affirm the statement of denying realities, but rather they say: we do not know whether things have a reality or not? They are the skeptics. And it is said to them: Do you know that you do not know? If they say: Yes, they have affirmed that they know, and that refutes their doctrine. And if they say: No, we do not know, they are asked about the negation of knowledge from their knowledge, then about the third and fourth ad infinitum. Then this invalidates their statement since they affirmed that they do not know their own doctrine. Then they are made to know by what we mentioned of cutting limbs etc. to make it appear by their distress that they know. And likewise their remaining alive indicates that they know realities and are obstinate.
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This passage refutes the skeptic school (al-mutashakkika) who suspend judgment on whether things have realities. The author presents a logical dilemma: if they answer 'yes' to the question 'Do you know that you do not know?', they admit knowing something, contradicting their skepticism. If they answer 'no', they are pressed on whether they know that they don't know their own state, leading to an infinite regress. Additionally, the author uses a practical proof: when these skeptics are subjected to pain (cutting limbs), they react with distress, showing they know the reality of pain. Their very survival also indicates they recognize real things like food and drink. Thus, their claim is self-refuting and their obstinacy is exposed.

Key Terms
المتشككة
الدراية
الحقائق
نفي الدراية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Skepticism is a logically consistent and defensible position.
Correction: Al-Nasafi shows that skepticism collapses under the question 'Do you know that you do not know?' because any answer either affirms knowledge or leads to infinite regress. Additionally, practical experience (pain, survival) demonstrates that skeptics do know realities.

Checkpoint
What is the first question posed to the skeptics to expose the self-contradiction in their position?
A) Do you know that things have realities?
B) Do you know that you do not know?
C) Do you deny realities?
D) Do you affirm realities?

Key Takeaway
Skepticism refutes itself because the very claim of ignorance implies knowledge of ignorance, and our actions show we recognize reality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever claimed uncertainty about something you actually act upon with confidence? What does that reveal about your true knowledge?
?
When you face a painful truth about your own character, do you respond with honest acknowledgement or with skeptic-like avoidance? What are you protecting?
?
Your daily actions—eating when hungry, avoiding harm—prove you know reality. Now, in what area of your faith do your actions betray a claimed doubt? Will you today align your confession with your conduct?

Refuting Relativist Beliefs about Reality
Al-Nasafi refutes those who make realities dependent on belief.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Nasafi refutes the claim that realities are dependent on belief using a logical dilemma and a practical demonstration.

Core Idea
Realities are fixed, not subject to belief.

This lesson examines al-Nasafi's refutation of a group who claimed that the realities of things are relative to each person's belief. He uses a clever logical dilemma and a practical test to expose the absurdity of their position. The passage demonstrates the objectivity of reality, especially in the case of pain.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the structure: opponent's claim, then a two-part refutation.
The author uses a direct address style as if speaking to the opponent.
2
Identify the opponent's claim
The opponent claims that the realities of things are dependent on the beliefs of individuals. Find this statement in the text.
3
Examine the logical dilemma
Al-Nasafi presents a dilemma: If we believe realities are not dependent on belief, does that make them independent? If the opponent says yes, their doctrine is false. If no, they admit belief doesn't affect reality.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum.
4
Understand the practical test
He orders that they be beaten and cut. When they cry out, they are told to believe it is pleasure. Their inability to do so proves pain's reality is fixed.
This test shows the absurdity of subjectivism in a tangible way.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وطائفة أخرى منهم يزعمون أن حقائق الأشياء تابعة لاعتقادات المعتقدين. فيقال لهم: إنا نعتقد أن حقائق الأشياء ليست بتابعة لاعتقادات المعتقدين فهل خرجت الحقائق عن أن تكون تابعة لاعتقادات المعتقدين تبعا لاعتقادنا، فإن قالوا: نعم، فقد أقروا ببطلان مذهبهم، وإن قالوا: لا، فقد أقروا أيضا بأن المعتقد لم يصر تابعا للاعتقاد، ثم يؤلمون بالضرب وقطع الجوارح، فإذا ضجروا وصاحوا واستغاثوا، يقال لهم: اعتقدوا أن ما يفعل بكم هو الذاذ وإنعام وإيصال الراحة ليصير كذلك تبعا لاعتقادكم فتنهتك أستارهم ويبين عنادهم ومكابرتهم، وهؤلاء الفرق يسمون السوفسطائية والله الموفق.
And another group of them claim that the realities of things are dependent on the beliefs of the believers. So it is said to them: We believe that the realities of things are not dependent on the beliefs of the believers. So did the realities depart from being dependent on the beliefs of the believers in accordance with our belief? If they say: Yes, they have affirmed the invalidity of their doctrine. And if they say: No, they have also affirmed that the object of belief did not become dependent on the belief. Then they are pained by beating and cutting of limbs. If they become distressed, shout, and seek help, it is said to them: Believe that what is done to you is pleasure, beneficence, and bringing comfort, so that it becomes thus dependent on your belief. Then their cover is torn and their obstinacy and stubbornness becomes clear. And these groups are called the Sophists. And God is the granter of success.
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This passage refutes the group (al-indiyya) who hold that the realities of things are relative to each person's belief. The author uses their own principle: if we believe that realities are not dependent on belief, then according to them that belief should make reality non-dependent, which contradicts their doctrine. Then he uses a practical test: subject them to painful beating and cutting. If reality were dependent on belief, they could simply believe it is pleasure and it would become so. But when they cry out in pain, their belief fails, showing that pain is a fixed reality independent of their conviction. Thus their obstinacy is exposed. The author concludes that these groups are all sophists (al-sufistā'iyya).

Key Terms
العندية
حقائق الأشياء
الاعتقادات
السوفسطائية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Realities can change based on what one believes.
Correction: Realities are fixed; pain remains pain even if one believes otherwise, as shown by the practical test.

Checkpoint
How does al-Nasafi practically demonstrate that realities are not dependent on belief?
A) By asking a logical question about existence
B) By subjecting the opponent to pain and seeing if they can believe it away
C) By appealing to consensus
D) By quoting scripture

Key Takeaway
Al-Nasafi proves the objectivity of reality by showing that pain cannot be changed by belief, refuting relativism with both logic and experience.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever tried to convince yourself that a painful reality (like a sin or a loss) is actually good, only to find that your heart still aches? What does this tell you about the limits of your own belief?
?
What belief do you hold that you would be unwilling to test by exposing it to harsh reality? Could it be that you cling to it precisely because you fear it might shatter?
?
If you truly believe that Allah's commands are based on fixed realities, not your feelings, where in your life today must you submit to a truth you find unpleasant? Will you do it?

Refuting Sophist Relativism: Realities Do Not Depend on Belief
Nasafi refutes the Sophist claim that realities depend on beliefs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical refutation of Sophist relativism as presented by Imam Nasafi, and why it demonstrates that realities are independent of individual belief.

Core Idea
Realities do not bend to belief; belief bends to reality.

This passage presents a powerful logical challenge to those who claim that truth is subjective and depends on what one believes. Imam Nasafi confronts the Sophist school with a dilemma that forces them to admit either the falsehood of their position or its practical absurdity. As you read, notice how the argument shifts from abstract logic to a concrete, visceral test.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Let the author's argument flow before diving into details.
Pay attention to the opening statement and the question posed to the Sophists.
2
Identify the dilemma
Find the two possible answers the Sophists could give to the question. What does each admission imply about their doctrine?
The two horns of the dilemma are: 'yes' (realities change) or 'no' (realities don't change).
3
Consider the practical test
The author then describes a test: the Sophists are beaten and told to believe it is pleasure. Why does this expose the weakness of their position?
Focus on the reaction of the Sophists when subjected to pain.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The passage ends by naming the group as Sophists. How does the test reveal their obstinacy?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حقائق الأشياء ليست بتابعة لاعتقادات المعتقدين] (فيقال لهم: إنا نعتقد أن حقائق الأشياء ليست بتابعة لاعتقادات (۱) المعتقدين (۲)(۳) فهل خرجت الحقائق عن أن تكون تابعة لاعتقادات المعتقدين تبعا لاعتقادنا، فإن قالوا: نعم، فقد أقروا ببطلان مذهبهم ، وإن قالوا : لا ، فقد أقروا أيضا بأن المعتقد لم يصر تابعا للاعتقاد، ثم يؤلمون بالضرب (٤) وقطع الجوارح، فإذا ضجروا وصاحوا واستغاثوا ، يقال لهم : اعتقدوا أن ما يفعل بكم هو الذاذ وإنعام() وإيصال الراحة ليصير كذلك تبعا لاعتقادكم فتنهتك أستارهم ويبين عنادهم ومكابرتهم، وهؤلاء الفرق يسمون السوفسطائية (٦) والله الموفق.
The realities of things are not dependent on the beliefs of the believers. (So it is said to them: We believe that the realities of things are not dependent on the beliefs (1) of the believers (2)(3). Have the realities departed from being dependent on the beliefs of the believers following our belief? If they say: Yes, they have admitted the falsehood of their doctrine. And if they say: No, they have also admitted that the object of belief has not become dependent on belief. Then they are pained by beating (4) and cutting of limbs, and when they are distressed and cry out and seek help, it is said to them: Believe that what is done to you is pleasure and favor ( ) and bringing of comfort, so that it becomes so following your belief. Thus their curtains are torn and their obstinacy and stubbornness are exposed. These sects are called the Sophists (6). And Allah is the granter of success.)
Source pages p. 144

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Sophist position that realities are dependent on individual belief. The author presents a logical dilemma: if the Sophists admit that realities do not become dependent on belief because the author believes they are not dependent, then they have conceded the point. If they deny it, they admit that the object of belief does not become dependent on belief, which also undermines their position. Then the author illustrates the absurdity by a practical test: if the Sophists are subjected to pain and torture, they are told to believe it is pleasure—if their doctrine were true, the pain would become pleasure by belief. Their inability to do so exposes their obstinacy. The passage concludes by naming these sects as Sophists.

Correction: The refutation is a rigorous logical reduction ad absurdum: it forces the Sophists into a contradiction and then uses a practical test to demonstrate the impossibility of their claim.

?

Sects That Deny the Causes of Knowledge
Nasafi classifies sects that reject senses, report, or intellect as sources of knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the different sectarian positions regarding the three causes of knowledge: senses, report, and intellect.

Core Idea
Different sects reject different causes of knowledge, showing varying epistemological errors.

This passage from Nasafi's Tabsirat al-Adilla classifies various groups according to which of the three causes of knowledge (senses, report, intellect) they accept or reject. This clarifies the spectrum of deviation from the epistemological consensus held by rational people and highlights the unique position of the Barahima.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Note the three causes: senses, report, intellect.
The Sophists deny all three.
2
Identify each sect's position
List the sects mentioned: Sophists, Samaniyya, Barahima, Mulhida, Rafida, Mushabbiha. Determine for each which causes they accept or reject.
Use the translation to match terms.
3
Compare with the Sophists
Notice which sects agree with the Sophists on each cause. The text uses 'وافقهم' (agreed with them) to show alignment.
Samaniyya and Barahima agree on rejecting report; only Samaniyya agrees on rejecting intellect.
4
Understand the Barahima's distinction
The Barahima are singled out as affirming the intellect. Contrast this with the Mulhida, Rafida, and Mushabbiha who deny it.
This shows the Barahima are not entirely irrational.
5
Reflect on epistemological implications
Consider why rejecting any cause of knowledge leads to error. The consensus of 'rational people' affirms all three.
This classification helps identify extreme positions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والسوفسطائية أنكروها كلها، وقد مر الكلام في الحواس. وقد أقر بكون الحواس من أسباب المعارف العقلاء سوى هؤلاء المتجاهلة، ووافقهم في نفي كون الخبر من أسباب جميع المعارف فريقان (من الأوائل)، أحدهما : السمنية والآخر : البراهمة. ووافقهم في نفي كون العقل من أسباب المعارف السمنية لا غير، وإلى القول ببطلان النظر وخروج العقل من أن يكون من أسباب المعارف ذهبت الملحدة والرافضة وجماعة من المشبهة فأما البراهمة فإنهم يقرون بكون العقل من أسباب المعارف.
And the Sophists denied all of them, and the discussion of the senses has passed. And rational people other than these pretenders have affirmed that the senses are among the causes of knowledge. Two groups (from the ancients) agreed with them in denying that report is among the causes of all knowledge: one of them is the Samaniyya and the other is the Barahima. And the Samaniyya alone agreed with them in denying that the intellect is among the causes of knowledge. And the Mulhida, the Rafida, and a group of the Mushabbiha went to the position of the invalidity of discursive reasoning and the exclusion of the intellect from being among the causes of knowledge. As for the Barahima, they affirm that the intellect is among the causes of knowledge.
Source pages p. 144

Commentary
This passage is a classification of sectarian positions on the three causes of knowledge. The Sophists reject all three. The Samaniyya and Barahima join the Sophists in rejecting report, but the Barahima accept the intellect, while the Samaniyya reject it along with the Mulhida, Rafida, and some Mushabbiha. The author notes that the Barahima affirm the intellect, distinguishing them from the others. This classification shows the varying degrees of deviation from the epistemological consensus.

Key Terms

السمنية
البراهمة
الملحدة
الرافضة
المشبهة
النظر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the Barahima reject all causes like the Samaniyya, or that the Mulhida and Rafida accept intellect.
Correction: The Barahima actually affirm the intellect, while the Mulhida, Rafida, and Mushabbiha reject it.

Checkpoint

A) The Sophists
B) The Samaniyya
C) The Barahima
D) The Mulhida

Key Takeaway
The classification of sects shows that rejecting any of the three causes of knowledge (senses, report, intellect) leads to epistemological error; only the Barahima among the dissenters accept the intellect.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Barahima accepted intellect but rejected report. Are there teachings in the Qur'an or Sunnah that you find difficult to accept because your intellect resists them? How will you train yourself to submit to authentic revelation even when it seems contrary to your reasoning?
?
This lesson shows that rejecting even one source of knowledge can lead a person or sect into error. Which of the three causes (senses, report, intellect) do you most distrust or neglect in your daily life, and what practical step can you take today to restore its rightful place in how you seek truth?
?
The Sophists denied all knowledge – a position of complete skepticism. Do you personally know anyone who has fallen into extreme doubt, perhaps due to being misled by a group or philosophy? How can this lesson equip you to speak to them with clarity and compassion?

Why Inner Feelings Cannot Determine Religious Truth
The author refutes the claim that subjective feeling of goodness determines religious truth.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why subjective feelings of goodness cannot be a valid criterion for determining the truth or falsehood of religious claims.

Core Idea
Subjective goodness leads to contradictions, so it cannot be a criterion for truth.

In this passage, the author concludes his discussion on the means of knowledge by refuting a common claim: that whatever feels good in one's heart must be true. He shows through logical reasoning that this subjective criterion would make contradictory beliefs equally valid, which is impossible. Let's examine his argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the strong and clear language in the refutation.
2
Identify the author's premise
The author begins by affirming that knowledge comes only from senses, reports, and intellect. This sets the stage for rejecting any additional criterion like subjective feeling.
3
Understand the reductio ad absurdum
The author shows that if subjective goodness were a criterion, then contradictory beliefs (like world being created vs. eternal) would all be correct. This is impossible, so the premise must be false.
This step is crucial for grasping the refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكلام في إبطال كون ما يقع في القلب حسنه والإلهام والتقليد من أسباب معرفة صحة الأديان: فإذا ثبت بما ذكرنا كون الحواس والأخبار والعقول من أسباب المعارف فنقول: ليس وراء هذه الأشياء سبب يعرف به صحة الأديان وفسادها. وقال قوم: من وقع في قلبه حسن شيء لزمه التمسك به، وهذا محال، لأن كثرة الأديان ظاهرة، وتضادها أمر بين، وكل يدعي وقوع حسن ما يدين به، وقبح ما يدين به غيره في قلبه، فيؤدي إلى أن يكون كل من يدين بشيء كان دينا صحيحا، لوقوع حسنه في قلبه المتدين به، فيكون القول بحدوث العالم وقدمه، وثبوت الصانع وتعطيله، وتوحيده وتثنيته، وثبوت الصفات له ونفيها صحيحا، وهذا محال، وكذا ينبغي أن يكون كل دين حقا، ولوقوع حسنه في قلب من اعتقد، باطلا، لوقوع قبحه في قلب من لم يعتقده واعتقد ضده، وفساد هذا مما لا يخفى على المجانين، والله الموفق.
Discussion on nullifying that what occurs in the heart of its goodness (ḥusn), inspiration (ilhām), and blind following (taqlīd) are means of knowing the correctness of religions. So when it is established, by what we have mentioned, that the senses, reports, and intellects are means of knowledge, we say: There is nothing beyond these things by which the correctness and corruption of religions can be known. Some people say: Whoever feels in his heart that something is good must adhere to it. This is impossible, because the multiplicity of religions is apparent and their contradiction is clear. Everyone claims that what he adheres to appears good in his heart and what others adhere to appears evil in his heart. This leads to the conclusion that every religion someone adheres to would be correct because of the occurrence of its goodness in the heart of the adherent. Then the statements that the world is originated and eternal, that there is a Creator and its denial, that He is one and two, that He has attributes and their negation—all would be correct. This is impossible. Likewise, every religion would be true because of the occurrence of goodness in the heart of the one who believes it, and false because of the occurrence of evil in the heart of the one who does not believe it and believes its opposite. The corruption of this is not hidden even from the insane. And Allah is the giver of success.
Source pages p. 153

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of using subjective moral intuition (ḥusn) as a criterion for religious truth. The author first sets the stage by affirming that knowledge comes only from the senses, reports, and intellect, and nothing else. Then he addresses those who claim that whatever feels good in the heart is binding. The author argues that since religions are many and contradictory, and each follower feels his own religion is good and others' bad, this would make every contradictory claim true—such as the world being both created and eternal, God existing and not existing, etc. This is absurd. Moreover, it would lead to each religion being true for its follower and false for its opponent simultaneously. The author emphasizes that even insane people would recognize the absurdity of this.

Misconception: A strong inner conviction that something is good is a reliable indicator of religious truth.
Correction: The author demonstrates that this leads to accepting contradictory beliefs as true, so it cannot be a valid criterion.

Checkpoint

A) It would make all religions false
B) It would make contradictory religions all equally correct
C) It would prove the existence of God
D) It would eliminate religious diversity

Key Takeaway
Subjective feeling cannot determine religious truth because it leads to accepting contradictory beliefs as true.
