Inspiration Cannot Validate Itself
The author refutes the claim that inspiration (ilham) can validate religious beliefs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical refutation of personal inspiration (ilham) as a reliable criterion for religious truth, and why rational proof (dalil) is ultimately necessary.

Core Idea
Inspiration cannot validate itself; it needs external proof.

The author now tackles a second claim: that spiritual inspiration (ilham) can determine which religion is true. On the surface, this seems plausible – one might feel inwardly convinced. But the author exposes a devastating self-refutation: if someone is inspired that inspiration itself is a false guide, what then? This short but powerful argument forces the opponent to admit that inspiration cannot be an absolute standard.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the English translation to get the gist of the argument.
Notice the concise and logical style of the author.
2
Identify the claim under refutation
The passage begins by stating the claim: 'Some people say that inspiration is a means of knowing the correctness of religions.' This is the target of refutation.
The author considers this similar to a previously refuted argument about subjective goodness.
3
Understand the self-refuting dilemma
The author asks: what if someone is inspired that the statement 'inspiration is the tool of correctness' is itself corrupt? This creates a dilemma. If the opponent says that inspiration is correct, then that specific inspiration (declaring the tool corrupt) is correct, so some inspirations are corrupt. If he says it is corrupt, then not all inspiration is correct.
Either way, the opponent must admit that some inspiration is false.
4
Reach the conclusion
Since inspiration can be both true and false, it cannot be an absolute criterion. One needs an external proof (dalil) to distinguish true from false inspiration. Thus, the reference is to proof, not inspiration.
This argument does not deny that true inspiration can occur; it only denies it as a universal criterion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال قوم بأن الإلهام سبب معرفة صحة الأديان والمذاهب، وهذا أيضاً مثل الأول، لأن كلا يدعي أنه ألهم صحة قول نفسه، وفساد مذهب خصمه، فيؤدي إلى القول بصحة الأديان المتناقضة على ما قررنا، وإلى القول بأن كل فاسد، ويقال لهؤلاء: إنى ألهمت أن القول بأن الإلهام آلة صحة الأديان فاسد، أصحيح إلهامي هذا أم فاسد؟ فإن قال هو صحيح، أقر أن القول بأن الإلهام آلة معرفة صحة الأديان والمذاهب قول فاسد، فقد أقر بكون شيء من الإلهام فاسدا، وإذا كان الإلهام بعضه صحيحا، وبعضه فاسدا لم يمكن الحكم بصحة كل الإلهام على الإطلاق، ما لم يقم دليل صحته، فصار المرجع إلى الدليل دون الإلهام، والله الموفق.
Some people say that inspiration (ilhām) is a means of knowing the correctness of religions and doctrines. This too is like the first [argument], because each person claims that he has been inspired with the correctness of his own view and the corruption of his opponent's doctrine. This leads to the claim that contradictory religions are correct, as we have established, and to the claim that everything is corrupt. It is said to them: I have been inspired that the statement that inspiration is the tool of the correctness of religions is corrupt. Is my inspiration correct or corrupt? If he says it is correct, he admits that the statement that inspiration is a tool for knowing the correctness of religions and doctrines is corrupt. Thus he admits that some inspiration is corrupt. And if some inspiration is correct and some corrupt, it is not possible to judge all inspiration as valid absolutely, as long as a proof of its soundness is not established. So the reference becomes to proof (dalīl), not to inspiration. And Allah is the giver of success.
Source pages p. 154

Commentary
This passage refutes the claim that spiritual inspiration (ilhām) can serve as a criterion for religious truth. The author compares it to the previous refutation of subjective goodness. Each person claims inspiration for his own beliefs, leading to contradictory religions all being considered correct. Then the author presents a powerful argument: the opponent is asked to consider that someone might be inspired to believe that inspiration itself is a corrupt tool. If the opponent says that inspiration is correct, he admits that an inspiration claiming inspiration is false is correct, thus admitting that some inspirations are corrupt. If he says it is corrupt, then he has acknowledged that not all inspiration is valid. In either case, one cannot rely on inspiration alone; a proof (dalīl) external to inspiration is needed to distinguish true from false inspiration. Therefore, the ultimate reference is to rational proof, not inspiration.

Key Terms
إلهام (ilhām)
آلة (āla)
دليل (dalīl)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Personal inspiration is self-authenticating and needs no external check.
Correction: The author shows that inspiration can be conflicting, and even if one claims to be inspired about the validity of inspiration itself, it leads to a contradiction. Hence, inspiration requires an external proof to be considered valid.

Checkpoint
What is the self-refuting argument the author uses against inspiration as a criterion?
A) Different people have different inspirations, so all must be false
B) If someone is inspired that inspiration is false, this creates a contradiction
C) Inspiration is only for prophets, not common people
D) Inspiration is always true for Muslims but false for non-Muslims

Key Takeaway
Without an external proof, inspiration cannot distinguish truth from falsehood; thus, rational proof (dalil) is the ultimate reference.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever felt absolutely certain about a belief because of a strong inner feeling, to the point that you dismissed all opposing views? What external proof did you have that your feeling was from Allah and not from your own desire? Will you commit to seeking clear evidence before trusting such feelings?
?
If a sincere follower of another religion told you they were inspired that their path is true, how would you respond without appealing to any external evidence? Could you prove them wrong using only your own inspiration? What does this lesson demand of you in such a situation?
?
This lesson teaches that even your most profound spiritual experiences must be tested against objective proof. Is there an area of your faith where you rely more on personal inspiration than on the clear proofs of revelation and reason? What will you do today to bring that area under the authority of Allah's proof?

The Self-Referential Refutation of 'Every Mujtahid is Correct'
Refutes the Mu'tazilite claim that every mujtahid is correct using a self-referential argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a self-referential argument refutes the Mu'tazilite doctrine that every mujtahid is correct.

Core Idea
“Every mujtahid is correct” is self-defeating.

This passage presents a powerful dialectical refutation of the Mu'tazilite view that every mujtahid is correct. A Maturidi scholar constructs a dilemma: if his own ijtihad concludes that mujtahids can err, then either his ijtihad is correct or mistaken, and both outcomes undermine the absolute claim. Let's examine the argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the Argument
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the first-person voice and the conditional structure.
The argument is framed as a direct challenge to the Mu'tazili opponent.
2
Identify the Two Branches of the Dilemma
Find where the opponent is given two choices: either say the scholar's ijtihad is mistaken, or say it is correct. Each leads to a contradiction.
These are the two horns of the dilemma.
3
Understand the Contradiction
See how each response forces the Mu'tazili to admit something that contradicts their original claim. The argument is airtight.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبمثل هذا استدل بعض أصحابنا على المعتزلة في قولهم: كل مجتهد مصيب، فقال: إني اجتهدت فأدى اجتهادي إلى أن المجتهد يخطئ ويصيب، أمصيب في هذا الاجتهاد أم مخطئ؟ فإن قلت لي : إنك مخطئ، فقد أقررت بفساد شيء من الاجتهاد، وبطل قولك : كل مجتهد مصيب، وإن قلت: إنك مصيب في اجتهادك فقد أقررت بصحة قول من يقول : إن المجتهد يخطئ ويصيب، والله أعلم.
And with something similar like this, some of our companions argued against the Mu'tazila regarding their statement: 'Every mujtahid is correct.' He said: 'I performed ijtihad and my ijtihad led me to the conclusion that the mujtahid sometimes errs and is sometimes correct. Am I correct in this ijtihad or mistaken? If you say to me: 'You are mistaken,' then you have admitted that some ijtihad is corrupt, and your statement that every mujtahid is correct becomes false. And if you say: 'You are correct in your ijtihad,' then you have admitted the validity of the statement that the mujtahid sometimes errs and is sometimes correct. And God knows best.'
Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This passage presents a classical refutation of the Mu'tazilite doctrine that every mujtahid is correct (taswib). The argument is in the form of a dialectical challenge. A Maturidi scholar says: Suppose I perform ijtihad and my ijtihad leads me to the conclusion that mujtahids can be correct or mistaken (i.e., not every mujtahid is correct). Now ask the Mu'tazili: regarding this specific ijtihad of mine, am I correct or mistaken? If he says I am mistaken, then he admits that some ijtihad (namely mine) is mistaken, thus contradicting the claim that every mujtahid is correct. If he says I am correct, then he admits that the conclusion of my ijtihad is correct, namely that mujtahids can be mistaken, which also contradicts the absolute claim that every mujtahid is correct. Thus in either case, the Mu'tazilite position is refuted.

Key Terms
الاجتهاد
المجتهد
المعتزلة
المصيب
الخطأ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Mu'tazilite view means all opinions are equally valid and cannot be wrong.
Correction: The argument shows the view is self-defeating because it cannot consistently evaluate a contradictory ijtihad.

Checkpoint
In the refutation of 'every mujtahid is correct', what is the outcome if the Mu'tazili says the opponent's ijtihad is correct?
A) It confirms that every mujtahid is correct.
B) It admits that the opponent's ijtihad (which says mujtahids can be mistaken) is correct, thus contradicting the original claim.
C) It leads to an infinite regress.
D) It proves that ijtihad is invalid.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilite position that every mujtahid is correct is self-defeating because any specific ijtihad that denies it cannot be consistently evaluated.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hold a strong belief, do you welcome arguments that test its consistency, or do you avoid them? This lesson shows that even a claim like 'everyone is correct' can be self-defeating. Consider a belief you hold with certainty. Could it be that you are unwilling to examine it because you fear it might self-destruct? What would it cost you to sincerely test it?
?
Have you ever been in a situation where your own position, if applied consistently to your own reasoning, would undermine itself? How did you react? This lesson demands that we apply our principles to ourselves. Are you ready to do that with your most cherished opinions?
?
The Mu'tazilite claim seemed generous—affirming every mujtahid's correctness—but it was logically untenable. Think about areas in your life where you try to avoid conflict by declaring 'everyone is right' in matters of truth. Is that compassion or intellectual laziness? This week, identify one area where you need to hold to a truth even if it means someone else is wrong, and resolve to do it with humility.

Proof That Not Every Mujtahid Is Correct
Nasafi presents a proof against the Mu'tazila claim that every mujtahid is correct.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical dilemma used by some Maturidi scholars to refute the Mu'tazila claim that every mujtahid is correct.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila claim is self-refuting: any ijtihad about it forces inconsistency.

The Mu'tazila held that every mujtahid is correct (taṣwīb). Some of our Maturidi companions devised a clever dilemma to disprove this. By asking the Mu'tazilite to judge a specific ijtihad that concludes the possibility of error, they expose a fatal contradiction. Let's examine this proof closely.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Read the Arabic text once to get its rhythm. Then read the English translation. Notice the question posed at the heart of the argument.
The passage is a reported debate, so imagine the dialogue.
2
Identify the dilemma structure
The proof presents two possible answers to a single question, both leading to a contradiction of the Mu'tazila position. Find the question and the two horns.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum.
3
Trace the logical consequence of each horn
Consider each answer: if the Mu'tazila says the proponent is mistaken, then they admit some ijtihad is wrong. If they say he is correct, they admit the proponent's conclusion that mujtahids can err. Both contradict their original claim.
4
Reflect on why this refutes the Mu'tazila
The Mu'tazila cannot consistently hold their view. The dilemma shows that their position is internally incoherent, because any judgment about it forces them to accept its opposite.
This is a powerful method of theological argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ليس كل مجتهد مصيب كما تقول المعتزلة وبمثل هذا استدل بعض أصحابنا على المعتزلة في قولهم: كل مجتهد مصيب، فقال: إني اجتهدت فأدى اجتهادي إلى أن المجتهد يخطئ ويصيب، أمصيب في هذا الاجتهاد أم مخطئ؟ فإن قلت لي : إنك مخطئ، فقد أقررت بفساد شيء من الاجتهاد، وبطل قولك : كل مجتهد مصيب، وإن قلت: إنك مصيب في اجتهادك فقد أقررت بصحة قول من يقول : إن المجتهد يخطئ ويصيب، والله أعلم.
Not every mujtahid is correct as the Mu'tazila say. By something like this some of our companions argued against the Mu'tazila in their statement: Every mujtahid is correct. He said: I performed ijtihad and my ijtihad led me to the conclusion that the mujtahid errs and is correct. Am I correct in this ijtihad or mistaken? If you say to me: You are mistaken, then you have admitted the corruption of some ijtihad, and your statement 'Every mujtahid is correct' is invalidated. And if you say: You are correct in your ijtihad, then you have admitted the validity of the statement of those who say that the mujtahid errs and is correct. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This passage presents a proof against the Mu'tazila position that every mujtahid is correct (taṣwīb). The author relates that some of his companions used the following dilemma: The interlocutor says he performed ijtihad and concluded that the mujtahid may err or be correct. He then asks the Mu'tazilite: Am I correct in this ijtihad or mistaken? If the opponent says he is mistaken, then the opponent admits that some ijtihad is corrupt, which contradicts the claim that every mujtahid is correct. If the opponent says he is correct, then he admits the validity of the statement that the mujtahid may err and be correct, which also contradicts the Mu'tazila view. In either case, the Mu'tazila position is refuted. The proof is a classic logical dilemma that exposes the inconsistency in the opponent's doctrine.

Key Terms
مصيب
مخطئ
اجتهاد
معتزلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the proof is circular because it assumes the conclusion.
Correction: The proof is a dilemma that relies only on the opponent's own admission. It does not beg the question; it forces the opponent to contradict himself regardless of his answer.

Checkpoint
What does the proof force the Mu'tazila to admit if they say the proponent is correct?
A) That the mujtahid always errs.
B) That the mujtahid is always correct.
C) That the mujtahid may err and be correct.
D) That ijtihad is invalid.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila position that every mujtahid is correct is self-defeating because any ijtihad that concludes otherwise forces them into inconsistency.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever held a belief so strongly that you refused to consider evidence that contradicted it? How does the dilemma in this passage challenge you to examine your own attachment to positions, even religious ones? Will you commit to seeking truth even when it means admitting you were wrong?
?
The proof forces the Mu'tazilite into a corner where any answer disproves their claim. When you face a disagreement with someone about a matter of faith, do you honestly consider the possibility that you might be mistaken? Or do you, like the Mu'tazilite, insist on your view regardless of the logical consequences? Answer before Allah.
?
The interlocutor in the proof uses ijtihad to reach a conclusion that contradicts the Mu'tazila's dogma. In your own life, are you willing to use your intellect and the tools of Islamic reasoning to question common narratives, even if it makes you uncomfortable? Or do you prefer the safety of inherited certainty? Pray for the courage to follow the truth wherever it leads.

The Branched Dispute on Repentance from Major Sins
Nasafi outlines the branches of dispute over repentance from major sins.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the dispute about repentance from major sins is a branched issue, and that repentance from disbelief does not remove the need to repent from each major sin individually.

Core Idea
Repentance from disbelief does not erase major sins—each sin requires its own repentance.

Nasafi begins by clarifying a key point: when a person repents from disbelief, they remain accountable for any unrepented major sins. This seemingly simple observation leads to a larger theological dispute with the Mu'tazila and Abu Hashim about the nature of repentance and its scope. In this passage, he sets the stage for a discussion that will explore whether repentance from one major sin can be valid while persisting in another.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the literal translation. Pay attention to the two scenarios described.
The passage is short but dense; take your time.
2
Identify the two scenarios
Notice that the author presents two cases: a person who repents from disbelief but not from a major sin, and a person who repents from one major sin but not from another. In both cases, the unrepented sin remains.
This establishes that repentance is specific to each sin.
3
Recognize the branch status
The author calls the disagreement a 'branch' (far‘), meaning it is derived from a more fundamental principle. Understand that this debate is not about the root of faith but about its details.
4
Note the two specific discussions to come
The passage mentions two upcoming topics: the status of the committer of a major sin with the Mu'tazila, and the validity of repentance from one major sin while persisting in another with Abu Hashim.
These will be addressed in subsequent lessons.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تاب عن الكفر بقي فيه إذا لم يتب عن كبيرة وجدت منه، من غصب مال وأشباه ذلك وإن كان تاب عن معصيته بقي فيها إذا لم يتب عن كبيرة أخرى إن كان نشأ عن هذا، فإذا الاختلاف في هذه المسألة فرع فنتكلم في مسألة صاحب الكبيرة مع عامة المعتزلة، وفي مسألة صحة التوبة عن كبيرة مع بقائه على كبيرة أخرى مع أبي هاشم
He repented from disbelief, he remains in it if he did not repent from a major sin found from him, such as usurping wealth and similar. And if he repented from his disobedience, he remains in it if he did not repent from another major sin if it arose from this. So the disagreement in this matter is a branch, so we will discuss the issue of the committer of a major sin with the generality of the Mu'tazila, and the issue of the validity of repentance from a major sin while remaining upon another major sin with Abu Hashim.
Source pages p. 164

Commentary
This passage defines the scope of a dispute about repentance. It states that if a person repents from disbelief but does not repent from a major sin like usurping wealth, he remains in that sin. Similarly, if he repents from one disobedience but not another. The author then notes that the disagreement over these matters is a branch issue, not the root, and that the discussion will address two specific sub-issues: the status of the committer of a major sin with the Mu'tazila, and the validity of repentance from one major sin while persisting in another, as argued with Abu Hashim.

Key Terms
الكبيرة
التوبة
صاحب الكبيرة
المعتزلة
أبو هاشم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that repentance from disbelief automatically removes all major sins.
Correction: The passage shows that each major sin requires its own repentance; repenting from disbelief does not cover other sins.

Checkpoint
With which two groups does the author say he will discuss the branch issues concerning major sins?
A) The Mu'tazila and Abu Hashim
B) The Ash'aris and the Mu'tazila
C) The Hanafis and the Malikis
D) The Qadariyya and the Murji'a

Key Takeaway
Repentance from one sin (even disbelief) does not automatically clear other major sins; each sin must be repented individually, and the precise implications are debated among theologians.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are you aware of any major sin in your life that you have not sincerely repented from, perhaps because you think your overall faith or repentance from other sins covers it? What are you going to do about it today?
?
Have you ever minimized a persistent major sin by thinking, 'At least I am not doing something worse,' or 'I will repent later'? How does this lesson's insistence on specific repentance for each sin challenge that comfort zone?
?
Imagine standing before Allah having repented from one major sin but still clinging to another. Would that repentance be accepted? What does this lesson demand from you about the sins you still tolerate?

Refutation of the Mu'tazila: The Prophet Would Not Be Negligent
Al-Nasafi refutes the Mu'tazila by showing absurd consequences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Mu'tazila requirement of rational proof for faith leads to an absurd conclusion about the Prophet.

Core Idea
If rational proof were necessary, the Prophet's actions would be foolish.

Al-Nasafi has already established that the Prophet and his companions did not demand rational proof before accepting faith. Now he uses this to refute the Mu'tazila: if they were right, then the Prophet would have been negligent in his duty. This lesson shows the force of that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp al-Nasafi's argument. Notice his starting point: he declares the corruption of the Mu'tazila view.
The passage is a single continuous argument.
2
Identify the two consequences
Al-Nasafi lists two absurd outcomes if the Mu'tazila were correct: the Prophet's actions would be foolishness, and he would have left an obligation unfulfilled.
3
Reflect on the impossibility
Understand why the second consequence is impossible for a prophet—it would imply negligence, which cannot be attributed to the Prophet. This refutes the Mu'tazila position.
This is the core of the refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بيان فساد قول المعتزلة يحقق هذا أن الصنيع من الرسول وخلفائه وصحابته وأئمة أمته على رأي الخصوم داخل في حيز السفه، وبقيت على الرسول صلى الله عليه وسلم عهدة التكليف بتعليم من بلغ إليهم الرسالة نهاية علم الأصول، وإذا فارق الدنيا ولم يفعل ذلك وحكم
Demonstration of the corruption of the Mu'tazila's statement: This confirms that the actions of the Messenger, his caliphs, his companions, and the imams of his community, according to the opponents' view, fall within the scope of foolishness, and there would remain upon the Messenger of God (peace be upon him) the obligation of teaching those to whom the message reached the entirety of the science of principles. And when he departed the world without having done that, he would be judged...
Source pages p. 171

Commentary
This passage begins a refutation of the Mu'tazila school. Al-Nasafi states that his previous proof (that the Prophet and caliphs did not require rational proof for faith) demonstrates the falsehood of the Mu'tazila position. He argues that if the Mu'tazila view (that rational proof is necessary for faith) were correct, then the actions of the Prophet, his caliphs, companions, and imams would be considered foolish (safah), because they accepted faith without requiring such proof. Moreover, the Prophet would have been obligated to teach everyone the entire 'science of principles' ('ilm al-usul) up to its ultimate extent. Since he did not do so during his lifetime, he would have left a duty unfulfilled, implying negligence—which is impossible for a prophet. The text breaks off at 'and he would be judged' (wa hukima), but the argument is clear: the Mu'tazila position leads to an absurd and blasphemous conclusion, so it is false.

Key Terms
فساد (corruption)
سفه (foolishness)
عهدة التكليف (obligation of duty)
علم الأصول (science of principles)
الخصوم (opponents)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that requiring rational proof for faith is a harmless intellectual stance.
Correction: Al-Nasafi shows it leads to accusing the Prophet of foolishness and negligence, which are impossible.

Checkpoint
According to al-Nasafi, what would necessarily follow if the Mu'tazila view (that rational proof is required for faith) were true?
A) The Prophet and his companions acted foolishly and the Prophet failed in his duty.
B) The Prophet was a great teacher of rational theology.
C) The common people are excused from belief.
D) Faith is only valid for scholars.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila requirement of rational proof is refuted because it would imply the Prophet acted foolishly and left a duty unfulfilled.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you find yourself demanding rational proof before accepting a teaching of the Prophet (peace be upon him), ask yourself: does this demand reveal a lack of trust in his wisdom and complete conveyance? How can you cultivate a heart that submits without hesitation?
?
The Prophet's every action was free from foolishness. Look at your own daily choices—your time, speech, and priorities. Where are you acting foolishly by neglecting his sunnah? Commit today to change one habit that contradicts his guidance.
?
The Prophet fulfilled his duty to teach the fundamentals completely. Are you fulfilling your duty to learn those fundamentals and live by them? What is one essential principle of faith you have neglected or taken lightly? Resolve to study it deeply this week.

Refutation of al-Ash'ari's Intermediate Position on Faith
The author criticizes al-Ash'ari's classification of the believer who delays reflection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Maturidi author argues that al-Ash'ari's classification of the believer who delays reflection is contradictory and identical to the Mu'tazilite position.

Core Idea
Al-Ash'ari's 'intermediate' between faith and unbelief is the same as the Mu'tazilite view he rejected.

The author examines al-Ash'ari's claim that a person who affirms belief but has not reflected intellectually is neither a believer nor an unbeliever. He points out the internal inconsistency: al-Ash'ari removes such a person from unbelief but does not grant them the status of faith. The author then compares this to the Mu'tazilite doctrine of the 'intermediate position' and highlights further problems with al-Ash'ari's analogy to sinful believers.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contradiction
Notice that al-Ash'ari takes the person out of kufr but does not bring them into iman. This is the core inconsistency the author highlights.
The author says: 'He takes him out of unbelief by this and does not bring him into faith by it.'
2
Recognize the parallel to Mu'tazila
The author explicitly states that this is establishing the intermediate position (al-manzila bayna al-manzilatayn), just as the Mu'tazila did, even though al-Ash'ari rejects that view.
This shows the internal contradiction in al-Ash'ari's own thought.
3
Evaluate the fasiq comparison
Al-Ash'ari compares the person to a fasiq (sinner) who is punished and then enters Paradise. The author questions: if the person is a fasiq for neglecting reflection, why is he not considered a Muslim? Major sins do not negate Islam.
The author uses Maturidi principle that major sin does not negate iman.
4
Understand the reward issue
Allah only promises Paradise to believers. So how can al-Ash'ari admit a non-believer to Paradise? The author finds this as erroneous as the Mu'tazilite claim of eternal Fire for non-unbelievers.
The argument rests on the Qur'anic promise of reward for believers only.

And if faith were not present, he would not be repelled from unbelief by it. Then this from al-Ash'ari is an apparent contradiction, for he takes him out of unbelief by this and does not bring him into faith by it. And this from him is establishing the intermediate position (al-manzila bayna al-manzilatayn), just as the Mu'tazila did, and he rejects that. Its verification is that reward is known by the promise of Allah, and Allah has only promised Paradise to believers. So by what proof does he say that one who is not a believer enters Paradise? And if he said: 'I do not say that he is a believer, and he is an unbeliever and he will remain forever in the Fire,' it would be said to him: 'How can he be an unbeliever when denial has been replaced by affirmation, and you say that faith is affirmation and unbelief is denial?' Commentary
This passage is a refutation of al-Ash'ari's view that a person who believes a prophet but has not engaged in proper intellectual reflection is neither a believer nor an unbeliever but an intermediate. The author argues that this is contradictory: al-Ash'ari takes him out of kufr but does not bring him into iman, which is precisely the Mu'tazilite 'manzila bayna al-manzilatayn' position that al-Ash'ari himself rejected. The author further examines al-Ash'ari's claim that such a person is like a fasiq (sinner) who will be punished and then enter Paradise. The author questions: if the person is a fasiq for neglecting reflection (nazar), why is he not considered a Muslim? Major sins do not negate Islam. If his act of tashdiq is iman, then why does he remain in kufr? The author then draws a parallel: the Mu'tazila erred by consigning non-believers to eternal Fire, and al-Ash'ari errs by admitting a non-believer to Paradise. The author bases this on the fact that Allah only promises Paradise to believers. The argument ends with a rhetorical question about how one can be a kafir when he has tashdiq. The passage ends mid-sentence due to page break.

Al-Ka'bi's View on the Muqallid: Believer but Not Scholar
Reports al-Baghdadi's inference that al-Ka'bi considered the muqallid a believer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that al-Ka'bi considered the muqallid (blind follower) a believer, even though such belief is not considered knowledge (ʿilm).

Core Idea
For al-Ka'bi, faith can be valid without demonstrative knowledge.

This passage reports a key debate in early kalām: Can a person who accepts religious truths without independent investigation be considered a true believer? Al-Baghdadi attributes to al-Ka'bi a nuanced position—the muqallid is indeed a believer, but not a scholar. This distinction hinges on the definition of knowledge versus mere belief.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the compact style and the reasoning attributed to al-Baghdadi.
The passage is a single, dense sentence in the original.
2
Identify the two parts of the report
The passage presents two components: (1) al-Ka'bi inclined to call the muqallid a believer, (2) he invalidated his own definition of knowledge by pointing out that a commoner's belief in God and creation is belief of a thing as it is, yet is not considered knowledge.
3
Understand the conclusion
From these two points, the author concludes that al-Ka'bi considered the muqallid a believer despite lacking knowledge. Reflect on why this distinction matters for defining faith.
This conclusion challenges the idea that faith requires intellectual demonstration.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كلامي عبد القاهر البغدادي من جملة متكلمي أهل الحديث، يدل على أنه كان يرى هذا الرأي أن المقلد مؤمن وليس بعالم، فإنه مال إلى جعله مؤمنا ثم أبطل على تحديده للعلام أنه اعتقاد الشيء على ما هو به باعتقاد العامي حدوث العالم وثبوت الصانع أنه اعتقاد الشيء على ما هو به وليس بعلم، فكان مجموع هذين الكلامين دليلا على أنه كان يجعل المقلد مؤمنا وإن لم يكن علم بما اعتقده وبمن آمن به والله الموفق.
The statement of 'Abd al-Qahir al-Baghdadi, among the theologians of the people of Hadith, indicates that he held this opinion: that the muqallid (blind follower) is a believer but not a scholar. For he inclined to consider him a believer, then he invalidated his definition of knowledge—that it is the belief of a thing as it is—by the commoner's belief in the origination of the world and the existence of the Maker, which is belief in a thing as it is and is not knowledge. So the total of these two statements was evidence that he considered the muqallid a believer even if he did not know what he believed and in whom he believed. And Allah is the grantor of success.
Source pages p. 179

Commentary
This passage reports and analyzes the view of al-Ka'bi as transmitted by 'Abd al-Qahir al-Baghdadi. The author states that al-Baghdadi's remarks show that al-Ka'bi considered the muqallid—one who accepts faith without independent investigation—to be a believer but not a scholar ('alim). The reasoning: al-Ka'bi first inclined to call the muqallid a believer, then he refuted his own definition of knowledge (as belief of a thing as it is) by noting that the commoner's belief in the origination of the world and the existence of the Creator is indeed belief of a thing as it is, yet this belief is not considered knowledge. From these two points, the author concludes that al-Ka'bi viewed the muqallid as a believer despite his lack of demonstrative knowledge.

Key Terms
المقلد (al-muqallid)
العالم (al-'alim)
العلام (al-'alām - knowledge)
اعتقد (i'tiqād)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that the muqallid cannot be a believer at all.
Correction: This passage shows that at least one early theologian (al-Ka'bi) considered the muqallid a believer, though not a scholar.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what did al-Ka'bi hold regarding the muqallid?
A) He is neither a believer nor a scholar.
B) He is a believer and a scholar.
C) He is a believer but not a scholar.
D) He is a scholar but not a believer.

Key Takeaway
Al-Ka'bi held that the muqallid's belief qualifies as faith (īmān) but not as knowledge (ʿilm), thus allowing for a valid yet uninformed faith.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

Al-Maturidi's Division and Definition of 'Ayn
Al-Maturidi rejects the jawhar/arad division, proposes a'rad and a'yan, and defines 'ayn.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Maturidi rejected the jawhar/arad division, how he redefined 'ayn as qa'im bi-nafsih meaning existence without a locus, and how he refuted al-Ash'ari and Burghuth.

Core Idea
Al-Maturidi’s 'ayn subsists by itself – not independently of God, but without a physical locus.

This passage introduces al-Maturidi’s unique division of the world into a'rad and a'yan, rejecting the standard jawhar/arad split. He redefines what it means for something to be self-subsistent (qa'im bi-nafsih). Al-Maturidi clarifies that this does not imply metaphysical independence from the Creator, but merely that an entity can exist without inhering in a substrate. He then refutes al-Ash'ari's denial of self-subsistent jawahir and Burghuth's absurd claim that God cannot create less than two parts. This lesson will walk through his argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage once
Read the Arabic and translation to get a sense of the flow. Notice that Maturidi starts with a critique, then proposes his division, then defines terms, and finally refutes opponents.
Pay attention to the contrast between Maturidi's and Ash'ari's understanding of 'qa'im bi-nafsih'.
2
Identify Maturidi's division
Find where Maturidi states his division: the world is a'rad and a'yan. Note how he defines each and why he rejects the jawhar/arad division.
He rejects jawhar/arad because bodies (ajsam) are composed of jawahir, causing overlap.
3
Understand the definition of 'qa'im bi-nafsih'
Maturidi says 'subsisting by itself' means existence without a locus (mahall). Contrast this with the Ash'ari view that it implies independence from all else.
This is the key doctrinal point: Maturidi limits self-subsistence to not needing a substrate, not to being uncreated.
4
Trace the refutations
See how Maturidi refutes al-Ash'ari (who denied any jawhar can be self-subsistent) and Burghuth (who denied that anything can exist without a locus). Notice the logical flaw in Burghuth's argument.
Burghuth's position leads to a contradiction: if one part is a place for another, that place itself exists without a place.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والشيخ أبو منصور الماتريدي لم يرض بهذه القسمة لما فيها من عيب التداخل، فإن الأجسام هي جواهر، لأنها مركبة منها، فاختار بأن قال: العالم قسمان: أعراض وأعيان يعني بالأعراض ما يستحيل قيامه بنفسه. وبالأعيان ما يقوم بنفسه ونعني بقولنا: ما يقوم بنفسه أن يصح وجوده من غير محل يقوم به لا ما سبق إلى وهم الأشعري.
الرد على الأشعري وبرغوث في تعريفهم للعين: إن القائم بنفسه ما يستغني في وجوده عن غيره. ولهذا أنكر كون الجواهر قائمة بأنفسها وقال: لا قايم بالنفس إلا الله تعالى. وإذا كان مرادنا من هذا اللفظ ما ذكرنا فلا معنى لإنكاره، لأن كل جوهر متركب كان أو غير متركب يصح وجوده في غير محل يقوم به، ولهذا قام جميع العالم لا في محل خلافا لما يقوله محمد بن عيسى الملقب ببرغوث أن ذلك لا يجوز. ولهذا زعم أنه ليس يجوز أن يخلق الله تعالى أقل من جزءين ليكون أحدهما مكانا للآخر، وهذا مع ما فيه من تعجيز الله، فاسد، إذ لو كان أحدهما مكانا للآخر ولم يكن الآخر مكانا له فهذا الذي هو المكان موجود لا فى مكان، وهذا عين ما أباه، ولو كان كل واحد منهما مكانا للآخر لكان كل واحد منهما متمكنا فيما هو متمكن فيه ومكانا لما مکانه هو محال، والله الموفق.
And Shaykh Abu Mansur al-Maturidi was not satisfied with this division because of the defect of overlap in it, for bodies are jawahir, since they are composed of them. So he chose to say: The world is two divisions: a'rad (accidents) and a'yan (entities). He means by a'rad that which is impossible for it to subsist by itself. And by a'yan that which subsists by itself. And by our saying 'that which subsists by itself' we mean that its existence is sound without a locus in which it subsists, not what occurred to the mind of al-Ash'ari.
The refutation of al-Ash'ari and Burghuth regarding their definition of al-'ayn: Indeed that which subsists by itself is that which is independent in its existence from other than it. For this reason he (al-Ash'ari) denied that jawahir subsist by themselves and said: 'None subsists by itself except Allah the Most High.' And if what is intended by this expression is what we have mentioned, then there is no meaning to his denial, because every jawhar, whether composite or non-composite, its existence is sound without a locus in which it subsists. For this reason the entire world subsists not in a locus, contrary to what Muhammad ibn 'Isa known as Burghuth says that this is not permissible. And for this reason he claimed that it is not permissible for Allah the Most High to create less than two parts so that one of them is a place for the other. And this, along with what it contains of attributing incapability to Allah, is corrupt, because if one of them is a place for the other and the other is not a place for it, then this which is the place exists not in a place, and this is exactly what he opposed. And if each one of them were a place for the other, then each one of them would be located in that in which it is located and a place for that which is its place — this is impossible. And Allah is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 183

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of al-Ash'ari and Burghuth's definition of 'ayn (entity), combined with al-Maturidi's own alternative division of the world. The author first explains that al-Maturidi rejected the common division into jawahir (substances) and a'rad (accidents) because of overlap: bodies (ajsam) are themselves composed of jawahir, so the division is not exclusive. Instead, al-Maturidi divides the world into a'rad (accidents) and a'yan (entities). He defines a'rad as what cannot subsist by itself, and a'yan as what subsists by itself, clarifying that 'subsisting by itself' means existing without needing a locus (mahall) to inhere in. This is contrasted with al-Ash'ari's misunderstanding, who thought 'subsisting by itself' meant absolute independence from anything else, leading him to deny that any jawhar other than God can be self-subsistent. The author then refutes al-Ash'ari by pointing out that every jawhar, whether simple or composite, can exist without a locus, as does the entire world. He also refutes Burghuth, who denied the possibility of a body existing without a locus, and who absurdly claimed that God cannot create less than two parts (juz') because one must serve as a place for the other. The author shows the self-contradiction in Burghuth's position: if one part is a place for the other, then that place itself exists without a place, which Burghuth rejects; if each is a place for the other, circularity results. Thus the Maturidi definition stands.

Key Terms
عين
عرض
جوهر
جسم
قائم بنفسه
برغوث
محل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think al-Maturidi's 'qa'im bi-nafsih' means absolute independence from Allah, like al-Ash'ari assumed.
Correction: Al-Maturidi explicitly defines it as merely 'existence without a locus', not independence from the Creator.

Checkpoint
According to al-Maturidi, what does 'subsisting by itself' (al-qa'im bi-nafsih) mean?
A) Being completely independent of any other being
B) Existing without a locus (mahall) in which to inhere
C) Being composed of parts
D) Being eternal and uncreated

Key Takeaway
Al-Maturidi redefines self-subsistence as existence without a locus, not independence from God, thereby preserving divine uniqueness while allowing created entities to be self-subsistent in a qualified sense.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever limited God's power in your mind by insisting that creation must follow the rules of your own logic, just as Burghuth did? Will you repent of that intellectual presumption and embrace God's absolute capability to create as He wills, even beyond your understanding?
?
When you hear a theological argument that seems rational, do you accept it without testing whether it truly honors Allah or actually diminishes His majesty? How will you now examine your own deeply held beliefs to ensure they do not attribute 'incapability' to the Creator?
?
You agree that the universe exists without a physical container—that is the direct corollary of al-Maturidi's point. Does your heart truly rest in the fact that Allah sustains all things without needing any medium, or do you secretly think He operates through mechanisms you can grasp? Commit today to letting go of the need to confine God's work within your mental categories.
